EXCHANGE --- Business & Finance News: Wells Fargo in Talks to Sell Real-Estate Unit

By Craig Karmin and Justin Baer 
479 words
30 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B12
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Eastdil Secured, the real-estate brokerage and investment bank owned by Wells Fargo & Co., is in advanced talks to sell the property firm to a venture led by Guggenheim Investments and a Singaporean sovereign-wealth fund, according to people familiar with the matter.

The management team at Eastdil, led by Chief Executive Roy March, would also take an ownership stake along with Guggenheim and Singapore's Temasek Holdings, these people said.

It isn't clear how much the buyers are paying for Eastdil, and there is still a chance an agreement won't be reached.

A deal with the Singapore fund and Guggenheim could provide a big lift for Eastdil, a niche firm that focuses on selling large buildings, mostly in major urban markets. The Singapore fund is expected to help open up new opportunities for Eastdil in Asia, where it has a limited presence. Eastdil would also face fewer regulatory restrictions if it is no longer part of a U.S. bank.

For Wells Fargo, an Eastdil sale would be consistent with the bank's strategy of shedding noncore businesses. Wells Fargo bought Eastdil in 1999 for about $150 million, according to a person with knowledge of the sale, in an effort to bolster its commercial real-estate-lending business.

Guggenheim Investments is the money-management arm of Guggenheim Partners, a sprawling financial conglomerate with interests ranging from investment banking to insurance. Eastdil and Guggenheim Investments share the same Wilshire Boulevard address in Santa Monica, Calif.

Guggenheim views the potential Eastdil deal as a financial investment, not a business it intends to fold into its operations, people familiar with the matter said.

An Eastdil sale would represent a crowning achievement for Mr. March, who would regain some control of the company where he has worked for more than 40 years. He became CEO in 2006, when Eastdil merged with Secured Capital.

Eastdil is smaller than such rivals as CBRE Group and Cushman & Wakefield Inc., which offer services including property management that provide steadier income and are less vulnerable to economic cycles. It has dominated larger commercial real estate sales in Manhattan and other major markets for years. Among U.S. sales of $100 million or more in 2018, Eastdil ranked No. 1, according to Real Capital Analytics.

But the firm has lost some ground to competitors recently. And it lost a top-performing group in New York City led by Douglas Harmon and Adam Spies when they moved to Cushman & Wakefield in 2016. In May, Newmark Group Inc. poached a senior hotel sales and financing team. Late last year, Eastdil hired Gary Phillips, head of acquisitions for Allianz Real Estate of America, to bolster the firm.

---

P.R. Venkat contributed to this story.

News
What's the alternative as quantitative easing turns to quantitative tightening?

MatthewVincent 
780 words
30 March 2019
Financial Times
FTFTA
1; Usa
6
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Alternative facts were first unleashed upon a partsuspecting world by president Donald Trump's political adviser in 2017. Since then, the US candour-mangler-inchief has provided no shortage of his own most recently about the economy and interest rates.

In late 2018, he said: "I think the [US Federal Reserve] right now is a much bigger problem than China ... I don't like what they're doing in terms of interest rates"; "they're raising interest rates too fast"; "the ... problem our economy has is the Fed" all apparently contradicting Fed chair Jay Powell, who judged the economy strong enough for rate rises.

In February, Mr Trump declared: "We are considered far and away the hottest economy anywhere in the world ... an economic miracle is taking place" in apparent contradiction of Mr Powell's renewed caution.

All this left equity investors struggling to discern truth from fiction helping to send volatility in the S&P 500 index to its third-highest level in seven years. The recent inversion of the US yield curve seen by many as a signal of a coming recession rattled investors.

Wealth managers, however, think they have the perfect counter to alternative facts: alternative assets.

Hedge funds, commodities and assetbacked lending can all protect portfolios from what Iain Tait, partner at London &Capital, deems "the great Powell U-turn".

Mr Tait has spent months working out what the shift in Fed tone means for risk assets and risk-averse clients. As he puts it, the markets "convulsed" at the thought of the Fed raising rates and reversing the quantitative easing stimulus of past years.

"The greatest threat to wealth and families is the transition from QE to QT, or quantitative tightening," he says. "You don't pivot to tightening without extreme volatility."

For more conservative wealth managers such as L&C, this creates a headache. They had thought the QEfuelled equity bull run would burn out by mid year, but the Trump/Fed turnabouts suggest it will now last further into 2019. Even so, Mr Tait concludes it is an "exhausted-looking cycle for stocks and bonds".

His head of alternatives, Kristian Richmond, has been looking to hedge funds. "Going back to traditional longshort equity funds is beneficial at this point," he suggests. But he needs managers with a verifiable record of making money at the end of a bull run. L&C uses Sandler US Equity.

Alternatives to long-only equity holdings are global hedge funds, says Mr Richmond. Here, he favours Informed Portfolio Management's Systematic Global Macro strategy, which trades assets based on economic fundamentals.

Ben Gutteridge, head of fund research at Brewin Dolphin, shares L&C's faith in long-short funds notably Janus Henderson's and BlackRock's but reckons it is currently a challenge for global macro hedge fund managers to put big economic and asset class calls at the heart of an investment strategy.

As a result, Markus Stadlmann, chief investment officer at Lloyds Bank Private Banking, puts his trust in technology to outperform delusional humans: "The systematic models used by machine learning trading strategies are agnostic as to the direction of their positions and can help reduce the risk of unconscious bias."

Bias can be removed by holding assets highly uncorrelated to the stock market or economy, argues Mr Tait. Here, he recommends renewable energy investments, where returns are driven by government subsidies and technological advances, such as battery storage systems. Examples used by L&C include the Foresight Solar fund and Gresham House's new Energy Storage fund.

For some, there is no alternative to classic alternatives. "The other way to diversify an equities-heavy portfolio is an exposure to commodities, and more specifically to gold," says Stphane Monier, chief investment officer at Swiss bank Lombard Odier.

"The precious metal tends to perform well in higher volatility and weakening US dollar environments, especially if market participants start having doubts about monetary policies," he adds.

Recommended allocations to alternatives range from 5 per cent at Hassium to 15-17 per cent at L&C, for wealthy families familiar with the risks and liquidity profile.

But, as with all things alternative, from political populists to uncorrelated asset classes, the cycle eventually turns. All the way back to active equity funds, Mr Monier believes: "As volatility rises above the all-time lows experienced in 2017, active management could once again be in a position to extract alpha."

Extracting truth from the White House's resident "Alpha" may take a little longer.

Matthew Vincent is the FT's Lombard columnist; matthew.vincent@ft.com; Twitter: MPJVincent

Facebook Accused Of Aiding Bias in Housing

By John D. McKinnon and Jeff Horwitz 
961 words
29 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON -- Federal officials accused Facebook Inc. Thursday of unlawful discrimination by allowing real-estate companies to target potential customers by race, religion and other factors, and signaled other technology firms are in its crosshairs.

The Department of Housing and Urban Development said the social-media giant violated the Fair Housing Act "by restricting who can view housing-related ads."

HUD has sent letters to a number of technology companies, including Alphabet Inc. unit Google and Twitter Inc., asking for more information about their advertising practices, an agency official said. Those inquiries are preliminary and don't amount to formal investigations, but could lead to additional probes.

"Just because a process to deliver advertising is opaque and complex doesn't mean that it exempts Facebook and others from our scrutiny and the law of the land," HUD General Counsel Paul Compton said in a statement. "Fashioning appropriate remedies and the rules of the road for today's technology as it impacts housing are a priority for HUD."

A spokesman for Facebook said the company was surprised by HUD's action, saying it had been working with the department to address the concerns. The spokesman said the company last year eliminated thousands of targeting options subject to misuse, among other measures.

"While we were eager to find a solution, HUD insisted on access to sensitive information -- like user data -- without adequate safeguards," the spokesman said in a statement. "We're disappointed by today's developments, but we'll continue working with civil rights experts on these issues."

Google didn't address questions about how it has responded to HUD's request and whether the alleged flaws in Facebook's algorithms would also be common to its own.

"We've had policies in place for many years that prohibit targeting ads on the basis of sensitive categories," a Google spokeswoman said in an email.

Twitter didn't respond to a request for comment.

The allegations against Facebook amount to a modern spin on redlining, or the historic practice by some real-estate brokers, lenders and others of drawing red lines around low-income and minority neighborhoods and either denying services, or targeting them with higher rates.

Facebook's trove of data allowed it to go much further, according to HUD -- for example, enabling ad buyers to exclude people that some unscrupulous landlords might seek to avoid, including people who expressed interest in an "assistance dog," a "mobility scooter" or "deaf culture."

It also allowed advertisers to block people who identified themselves as interested in "Puerto Rico Islanders," "Hijab Fashion" and "Hispanic Culture," according to HUD. Facebook offered customers hundreds of thousands of exclusionary categories.

Galen Sherwin, a senior staff attorney for the ACLU Women's Rights Project who helped lead a settlement that was announced last week with Facebook over many of its practices, said her group "would encourage HUD to investigate other platforms."

The ability to target consumers by their identifying characteristics "is a feature that's being touted," Ms. Sherwin said. "That's true in general of digital advertising -- it allows microtargeting based on user data in ways that were never possible before."

Private lawsuits against Facebook already have led the company to agree to changes that could lessen the impact of discrimination. But the company's use of algorithms and machine learning in advertising also is a big problem, according to critics. The HUD case could go further in forcing the company to change those practices as well.

Among other things, HUD alleged that Facebook's machine learning predicts likely responses to an ad to help determine its distribution. That can effectively become a source of bias against protected groups itself, the agency alleged. "Respondent's mechanisms function just like an advertiser who intentionally targets or excludes users based on their protected class," the complaint alleges.

Some experts in online discrimination said the HUD allegations show the need for Congress to step in and clarify that existing antidiscrimination rules in housing, employment and lending apply just as clearly in the digital world as in the physical world.

"Congress really needs to engage in a very deliberative process to see what applies to the digital space," said Nicol Turner Lee, a Brookings Institution fellow. "We don't want a regress" in civil rights because antidiscrimination laws were generally written before the internet, she said.

One potential problem for the government's effort to impose antidiscrimination rules online is a law written by Congress in the 1990s. It gives online platforms sweeping immunity from liability for the actions of their users. That law has been used by online firms to shield themselves from government action and private lawsuits alike.

Still, Thursday's action underscores how big internet companies are running into more problems.

In addition to the HUD action, Facebook is under investigation by the Federal Trade Commission over privacy concerns. Last year, Congress also made it easier to go after online platforms for sex trafficking.

Facebook's discrimination troubles date to 2016, when the ProPublica news organization reported how advertising could be targeted to -- or exclude -- users of particular races, genders and ages. Such targeting is acceptable in certain contexts, but federal law and state laws prohibit such discrimination in advertising for housing, jobs and many financial products.

Following the revelations, Facebook pledged to prevent such discriminatory advertising by blocking advertisers' ability to screen people protected by federal discrimination laws from seeing their advertising. HUD opened -- and then dropped -- an investigation into Facebook's practices.

HUD reopened its investigation in 2018, and Facebook settled a lawsuit with the state of Washington by agreeing, again, to prevent discrimination on its advertising platform.

HUD Action Against Facebook Signals Trouble for Other Platforms; Department accuses social-media firm of fostering discrimination in advertising based on race, national origin, religion and more

By John D. McKinnon and Jeff Horwitz 
1,462 words
28 March 2019
22:35
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON Federal officials accused Facebook Inc. Thursday of unlawful discrimination by allowing real-estate companies to target potential customers by race, religion and other factors, and signaled that other online advertising platforms are in its crosshairs.

The Department of Housing and Urban Development said the social-media giant violated the Fair Housing Act "by restricting who can view housing-related ads."

HUD has sent letters to a number of technology companies, including Alphabet Inc. unit Google and Twitter Inc., asking for more information about their sophisticated advertising systems, an agency official said. Those inquiries are preliminary and don't amount to formal investigations, but could lead to additional probes.

"Just because a process to deliver advertising is opaque and complex doesn't mean that it exempts Facebook and others from our scrutiny and the law of the land," HUD General Counsel Paul Compton said in a statement. "Fashioning appropriate remedies and the rules of the road for today's technology as it impacts housing are a priority for HUD."

A spokesman for Facebook said the company was surprised by HUD's action, saying it had been working with the department to address the agency's concerns. The spokesman said the company last year eliminated thousands of targeting options subject to misuse, among other measures.

"While we were eager to find a solution, HUD insisted on access to sensitive information like user data without adequate safeguards," the spokesman said in a statement. "We're disappointed by today's developments, but we'll continue working with civil rights experts on these issues." 

### picture data found, picture dimensions are 640 by 426, depth 1
pict005.jpg

'Using a computer to limit a person's housing choices can be just as discriminatory as slamming a door in someone's face,' said HUD Secretary Ben Carson, seen above center in Austin, Texas, this month. PHOTO: Julia Robinson for The Wall Street Journal

Google didn't address questions from the Journal about how it has responded to HUD's request and whether the alleged flaws in Facebook's algorithms would also be common to its own.

"We've had policies in place for many years that prohibit targeting ads on the basis of sensitive categories," a Google spokeswoman said in an email. "Our policies are designed to protect users and ensure that advertisers are using our platforms in a responsible manner."

Twitter didn't immediately respond to a request for comment.

The allegations against Facebook amount to a modern spin on redlining, or the historic practice by some real-estate brokers, lenders and others of drawing red lines around low-income and minority neighborhoods and either denying services, or targeting them with higher rates.

Facebook's trove of data allowed it to go much farther, according to HUD for example, enabling ad buyers to exclude people that some unscrupulous landlords might seek to avoid, including people who expressed interest in an "assistance dog," a "mobility scooter" or "deaf culture."

It also allowed advertisers to block people who identified themselves as interested in "Puerto Rico Islanders," "Hijab Fashion" and "Hispanic Culture," according to HUD. Facebook offered customers hundreds of thousands of exclusionary categories.

Galen Sherwin, a senior staff attorney for the ACLU Women's Rights Project, who helped lead a settlement announced last week with Facebook over many of its practices, said her group "would encourage HUD to investigate other platforms."

The ability to target consumers by their identifying characteristics "is a feature that's being touted," Ms. Sherwin said. "That's true in general of digital advertising it allows microtargeting based on user data in ways that were never possible before."

Private lawsuits against Facebook already have led the company to agree to changes that could lessen the impact of discrimination, for example by limiting the audience demographic categories that advertisers can target.

But the company's use of algorithms and machine learning in advertising also is a big problem, according to critics. The HUD case could go further in forcing the company to change those practices as well.

Among other things, HUD charged that Facebook's machine learning predicts likely responses to an ad to help determine its distribution. That can effectively become a source of bias against protected groups itself, the agency said. "Respondent's mechanisms function just like an advertiser who intentionally targets or excludes users based on their protected class," the complaint says.

Some experts in online discrimination said the HUD charges show the need for Congress to step in and clarify that existing antidiscrimination rules in housing, employment and lending apply just as clearly in the digital world as in the physical world. That hasn't always been clear.

"Congress really needs to engage in a very deliberative process to see what applies to the digital space," said Nicol Turner Lee, a Brookings Institution fellow. "We don't want a regress" in civil rights because antidiscrimination laws were generally written before the internet, she said.

One potential problem for the government's effort to impose antidiscrimination rules online is a law written by Congress in the 1990s. It gives online platforms sweeping immunity from liability for the actions of their users.

That law has been used by online firms in the past to shield themselves from government action and private lawsuits alike. The federal courts are currently divided over how it applies in the housing discrimination context, but it could arise as a potential hurdle for HUD's case against Facebook, lawyers said.

Still, Thursday's action underscores how big internet companies once darlings of Washington are running into more problems. In addition to the HUD action, Facebook is under investigation by the Federal Trade Commission over privacy concerns. Congress last year also made it easier to go after online platforms for sex trafficking.

Meanwhile, the Justice Department last summer filed a brief siding with the National Fair Housing Alliance in a case against Facebook. Justice Department lawyers said that Facebook could be held liable for discriminatory practices, despite the 1990s immunity law. The government also said decisively that it was unlawful to send ads selectively in a discriminatory way.

"It was a critical turning point showing the federal government was on our side," said Peter Romer-Friedman, a lawyer with Outten & Golden LLP.

Facebook's discrimination troubles date to 2016, when the ProPublica news organization reported how advertising could be targeted to or exclude users of particular races, genders and ages. Such targeting is acceptable in certain contexts, but federal law and state laws prohibit such discrimination in advertising for housing, jobs and many financial products.

Following the revelations, Facebook pledged to prevent such discriminatory advertising by blocking advertisers' ability to screen people protected by federal discrimination laws from seeing their advertising. HUD opened and then dropped an investigation into Facebook's practices.

HUD reopened its investigation in 2018, and Facebook settled a lawsuit with the state of Washington by agreeing, again, to prevent discrimination on its advertising platform. The ACLU and fair-housing groups also filed a suit that Facebook settled just last week that included payments of just under $5 million. As part of that settlement, Facebook said it was removing age, gender and ZIP Code targeting for housing, employment and credit-related advertisements.

In the wake of that settlement, Facebook said it believed it was working toward a similar agreement with HUD. But negotiations over how to proceed on a HUD request for key Facebook data broke down.

According to someone familiar with the request, HUD sought an array of data Facebook uses to personalize content to specific users, including location information, users' consumption of past content, what features of Facebook they engage with and who they interact with on the platform. Facebook balked, this person said, and HUD sued.

Advertisers are waiting to see if changes Facebook makes going forward will prohibit targeting that makes the platform so useful, said an executive at a large ad-buying agency.

In the meantime, buyers are identifying additional, alternative mediums for advertisers targeting people in the market for a home. The executive expects home sites to see bigger commitments from advertisers, and therefore an increase in ad revenue, in 2020.

WSJ Pro
VC Daily: Grindr Case's Chilling Effect | The Trouble With VC-Funded Ad Spending

1,676 words
28 March 2019
12:56
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. The U.S. is forcing a Chinese company to sell U.S. mobile app Grindr on national security grounds. That development will likely further turn off Chinese acquirers' pursuit of U.S. assets, sealing off an important exit path for American VCs.

"The Chinese internet companies are very large, flooded with cash, have massive valuations and would be great acquirers that have an appetite to expand into the U.S.," said David Pakman, a partner at Venrock.

The Committee on Foreign Investment in the U.S. ordered Beijing Kunlun Tech Co. Ltd. to sell Grindr out of concern that personal information obtained by the gay-dating app could be exploited by the Chinese government for espionage, The Wall Street Journal reports. Kunlun acquired a majority stake in Grindr in 2016.

And now on to the news...

In the News

Admissions affair: Lightspeed Venture Partners has cut ties with a co-founder of the firm after learning he had a link to the nationwide college admissions scandal.

* Chris Schaepe, who has invested for Lightspeed in dozens of software companies, hasn't been charged but he was dismissed from the firm after notifying Lightspeed of his connection to a central figure in the case, the firm said.

* "Lightspeed Partner Chris Schaepe recently made the firm aware of a personal matter," a spokesperson for the Menlo Park, Calif., firm said in a statement, referring to the admissions scandal. "We determined to separate from Chris to ensure this matter does not interfere with firm operations. The matter does not involve the firm, its personnel or its portfolio companies." "https://www.wsj.com/articles/lightspeed-co-founder-out-after-being-linked-to-college-admissions-scandal-11553735612?".

Insurance broker retools: Clover Health Inc., a health insurance startup specializing in Medicare Advantage plans, is planning to lay off around 140 employees, or about 25% of its staff, as it moves to bulk up on people with health-care industry experience.

* "We need to ensure that our focus and cost structure match our mission. This means shifting our team from generalists to more specialists," co-founder and Chief Executive Vivek Garipalli wrote in an email to employees that was reviewed by WSJ Pro Venture Capital.

* In January, WSJ Pro reported the company raised $500 million in a round led by Greenoaks Capital, bringing its total raised to date to $925 million. Other investors include Sequoia Capital, GV and Baillie Gifford.

* Mr. Garipalli added in his email: "In our early days, we learned the hard way that operating a Medicare Advantage plan was more challenging than we had initially anticipated." "https://www.wsj.com/articles/insurance-startup-clover-health-to-cut-25-of-workforce-11553705863?".

De luxe site: An online marketplace targeting the ultrawealthy just got some cash of its own.

* 1stdibs.com Inc. said it has raised a $76 million Series D financing round at a valuation above $500 million.

* The round of new capital, led by T. Rowe Price Associates Inc., will be used to fund the expansion of its marketplace for high-end furniture, art, jewelry and handbags.

* Existing investors include Index Ventures , Benchmark and Spark Capital. Including the current round, the company said it has raised a total of $170 million. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/marketplace-1stdibs-grabs-76-million-to-expand-luxury-platform-11553772600?".

Geron's Take: Who Really Benefits as Startups Fuel Growth Via Venture-Funded Ads?

By Tomio Geron

Gaining customers the key for growth used to be as cheap as unveiling an app in the Apple App Store or launching a Facebook app. But these days, the cost has skyrocketed as those channels have become heavily saturated, making it nearly impossible to get seen above the crowd.

* Some of the most successful companies like Google, Facebook and Stripe have grown through word-of-mouth. But many of today's startups rely on ads. As a result, they're raising more and more venture funding at higher valuations leaving venture firms to arm themselves with larger funds and higher management fees.

* Some startups that are put off by that trend are experimenting with an alternative that directly funds their customer-acquisition tactics.

* One specialist that provides funds to pay for ads is called Clearbanc. The company provides $5,000 to $10 million to startups to buy ads. Startups split the revenue with Clearbanc that results from the ads, or about 6% of the capital.

* Startups are getting the message. Toronto-based Clearbanc says it did 500 deals last year and expects to do 2,000 this year.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/gerons-take-startups-pay-heavily-for-facebook-and-google-ads-but-who-benefits-11553726190?"

Exits

Still climbing: Lyft Inc. raised its targeted price range to $70 to $72 a share for its initial public offering.

* At the top of the new range, which was disclosed in a filing Wednesday, the ride-hailing service would be valued at more than $23 billion on a fully diluted basis.

* The Wall Street Journal had previously reported that Lyft was telling some investors it was likely to price the stock in the low $70s, above its previously targeted range of between $62 and $68 a share. The WSJ's Corrie Driebusch has the story.

Vapor wager: With its $12.8 billion bet on Juul Labs Inc., Altria Group Inc. made a calculated gamble: Buying a startup whose stated goal is to get smokers to drop cigarettes would help the Marlboro maker, the biggest U.S. tobacco company, keep up with a quickly changing market. The risk: It could hasten its own decline.

New Money

Casper Sleep Inc., the direct-to-consumer mattress company, has a $1.1 billion post-money valuation following a fresh round of funding. The New York-based startup said it raised $100 million with participation from New Enterprise Associates, IVP and Norwest Venture Partners. Casper also said its revenue topped $400 million last year. Joining Casper's board are Karen Katz, former CEO of Neiman Marcus Group, and Dani Reiss, chairman of Canada Goose. "https://casper.com"

Kong Inc., a San Francisco-based API platform, raised $43 million in Series C funding. Index Ventures led the round, with participation from Andreessen Horowitz, Charles Rivers Ventures, GGV Capital and World Innovation Lab. "https://konghq.com"

Octopus Ventures led the investment, with participation from Sonae IM, Sequoia Capital and Pereg Ventures. The company has offices in New York, London and Tel Aviv. "https://cb4.com"

Qupital Ltd., a Hong Kong-based trade finance platform for small- and medium-sized enterprises across China, picked up $15 million in Series A funding. CreditEase FinTech Investment Fund led the round, which included participation from Alibaba Hong Kong Entrepreneurs Fund and MindWorks Ventures. "https://www.qupital.com"

PhotoShelter Inc., a New York-based visual media manager for professional photographers and brands, snagged an $8 million investment from Level Structured Capital. "https://www.photoshelter.com"

Alpha Foods, a Glendale, Calif.-based startup making plant-based versions of classic comfort foods, was seeded with a $7 million investment co-led by New Crop Capital and AccelFoods. "https://alphaplantbased.com"

Tripalink, a Los Angeles startup providing co-living spaces for millennials, raised $5 million in Series A financing. Calin SJG Fund led the round, and was joined by K2VC, e-Broker and Tekton Ventures. "http://www.tripalink.com"

Amenify Corp., a San Francisco-based provider of services such as cleaning and pet-care for apartment dwellers, nabbed $2.7 million in new funding led by RET Ventures. "https://www.amenify.com"

Trellis Research Inc., a Los Angeles-based startup providing a database of California court records, collected $2 million in seed financing from investors including Okapi Venture Capital, Craft Ventures, Sequoia Scout, Revel Ventures and Intrepid Ventures. "https://trellis.law"

People

Scale Venture Partners promoted Jeremy Kaufmann to principal. Since joining the firm in 2015, he worked on the firm's investments in KeepTruckin, Cognata, Solvvy, and TechSee.

Cloud storage provider Qumulo Inc. appointed Dan Miller to its board of directors. Mr. Miller is executive vice president of world-wide field sales, services and support for Tableau Software. Seattle-based Qumulo is backed by investors including BlackRock, Highland Capital Partners, Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, Madrona Venture Group, Valhalla Partners and Goldman Sachs.

Equifax, FICO Creator Team Up

By AnnaMaria Andriotis 
605 words
28 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Two consumer-credit giants plan to start working together to sell consumers' data to banks, the latest attempt to feed banks' appetite for more information on customers.

Equifax Inc. and Fair Isaac Corp., creator of the widely used FICO credit score, started pitching each other's services earlier this month, and the companies announced the partnership Wednesday. Both companies already sell their services to banks, but now their sales employees will pitch each other's services as well. Sometimes the companies will approach clients together.

Equifax, a credit-reporting giant still trying to recover from its massive 2017 data breach, maintains extensive data on U.S. adults that often include their income, bank account balances and whether they pay their gas and cellphone bills. FICO's software analyzes the data to help banks get a better read on loan applicants.

The partnership is the latest effort by companies that have been the bedrock of the U.S. consumer-credit system to diversify beyond the nuts and bolts of credit reports and scores.

For decades, most U.S. lenders have reviewed loan applicants' reports and scores to determine whether to approve them and what interest rate to charge.

After years of relatively cautious lending, banks and other lenders are seeking additional consumer data to help them make loans to more borrowers, including people with little credit history or with credit blemishes. Credit reporting and scoring firms have been pivoting to address the demand.

Late last year, FICO and another credit-reporting giant, Experian PLC, said they would begin factoring how consumers manage their bank accounts into credit scores. Soon after, Experian separately said it would add cellphone and utility payments into its credit reports and the FICO scores it sells to lenders. The new methodologies, which are optional for consumers, are designed to boost the number of approvals for credit cards, personal loans and other products.

Equifax and FICO argue that buying their services together will help lenders make underwriting decisions more quickly, especially for borrowers with thin credit histories. They also say that lenders will be able to make more precise decisions about what products to pitch to a customer, such as a plain-vanilla credit card versus a premium rewards card, and will be able to better screen people who apply for bank accounts.

But the companies will face competition from data providers and financial-technology firms that do the same and banks' own in-house analytics resources.

Equifax, Experian and competitor TransUnion have for years been amassing growing piles of data about American adults that go far beyond people's history managing their debts. At Equifax that includes information on people's employers, brokerage accounts and other information. Most of that extra data haven't made its way into credit reports or FICO credit scores, but credit-reporting firms have been selling that data separately to lenders.

Deciding which loan applicants to approve is complex. Making risky borrowers look more creditworthy can expose lenders to default risk. Denying someone who additional data might prove creditworthy -- like someone who pays their cellphone and other non-loan bills on time -- would hurt revenue. Approving a consumer whose credit reports and scores look good but has other hidden red flags, such as minimal savings, would elevate the lender's risk.

The FICO-Equifax deal was spearheaded by the companies' chief executives. FICO CEO Will Lansing and Equifax CEO Mark Begor have known each other for decades, and Mr. Begor was a FICO board member until he took over Equifax last year.

MPs tear into fund groups over conflict of interest on pay

Jennifer Thompson and Owen Walker 
1,205 words
28 March 2019
05:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Politicians warn high pay in asset management undermines efforts to hold companies to account

UK chief executives were caught in politicians  crosshairs following the release of a report on  eye-watering  pay this week, but a less prominent group also came in for stinging criticism: asset managers.

MPs on parliament s business select committee said soaring executive packages at FTSE 100 companies tarnished Britain s reputation and were a symbol of corporate greed.

As the biggest shareholders in Britain s listed companies, institutional investors have considerable power to push for change in investee groups. They can talk to executives, use their votes and, ultimately, wield the threat of divestment.

Many asset managers like to burnish their credentials as responsible investors, which is especially the case with active managers as they try to show they offer more than their passive rivals.

The MPs  report, however, was withering over the quality of this work. While it noted that asset managers were giving more attention to pay, it said many were not doing enough. It cast doubt on their ability to curtail pay through engagement alone and suggested that high salaries in fund management were a stumbling block.

 In general stewardship is not working as well as it should,  it said.  We do not have confidence in . . . institutional investors in exercising their stewardship functions, in a way that consistently bears down on executive pay . . . We cannot rely on shareholders to exert pressure. 

Amin Rajan, chief executive of Create-Research, the consultancy, said the report  makes uncomfortable reading for asset managers .

 Some will quibble with the report s broad-brush approach,  he said.  The rest will accept the spirit behind its conclusions. 

The committee called for a stronger correlation between employee and executive pay and questioned whether asset managers were best placed to police the issue.

The report said it was not impossible for investors to take  a principled stand  but that fund managers were often themselves extremely highly paid.  It may be awkward, if not hypocritical, for them (or the corporate governance teams which engage directly with companies) to criticise pay policies for prioritising short-term financial incentives,  said the report.

Peter Harrison, chief executive of Schroders, received a package of more than 6.7m in 2018   63 times that of median employee pay at the UK s second-largest listed fund manager. Average pay at the group was 103,615 across all staff.

Dick Weil, chief executive of UK-headquartered Janus Henderson, took home just over $9m (6.8m) last year after seeing his salary rise 26 per cent in six months.

Meanwhile, Prudential, the insurance group, paid an unnamed employee 16.6m last year   more than double the total package earned by chief executive Mike Wells. It is widely believed that worker is Richard Woolnough, a star bond manager in the company s asset management division.

 As long as the people in charge of putting ticks in the boxes that push through such pay deals are themselves paid large amounts of money, their idea of what is normal is skewed,  said Fergus Moffatt, head of UK policy at responsible investment group ShareAction.

The MPs also noted that even protest votes against pay packages, usually considered a last resort, were largely ineffective.

Both Royal Mail and Persimmon suffered shareholder rebellions over executive pay last year but neither vote resulted in change. The vote was nonbinding in the case of Royal Mail while at Persimmon abstentions were not counted.

However, Andrew Ninian, director of stewardship and corporate governance at the Investment Association, the trade body, said there was evidence that stewardship was working.  Firms such as HSBC, Aviva and Lloyds have all changed their pay packages in response to investor concerns,  he said.

The three companies have all recently cut the amount of money to which top executives are entitled in lieu of a pension contribution or defined benefit pension payments.

IA members undertook more than 7,000 engagements with companies in 2018 and 63 companies received more than a 20 per cent vote against a remuneration resolution last year.

 Shareholders want companies to be able to demonstrate the link between executive pay and company performance,  said Mr Ninian.  Asset managers play a key role in the stewardship process. 

Angeli Benham, corporate governance manager at Legal & General Investment Management, the UK s biggest fund manager, welcomed the call to make asset owners more responsible in holding managers to account but highlighted the difficulties of doing so.

 Getting companies to listen is often a challenge,  she said.  That is partly because they receive different feedback from different asset managers, some of whom may not have enough corporate governance resources to engage with as many companies in as much depth as they d like to. 

She added that LGIM avoided conflict of interest over pay by having the corporate governance team report directly to the chief executive and vote independently of the investment team.

Schroders, Standard Life Aberdeen, M&G Prudential and Aviva Investors declined to comment.

Mr Rajan said asset managers were still best placed to enforce a crackdown on lavish pay.  I don t think they re the only people but they re among the best. They own the companies, they have a very clear role,  he said.

However, he added that investors  should not be afraid  to signal their displeasure publicly.

The report urged large investors to make known their stance on generous executive pay, noting that it is  relatively rare  for them to do so. It called on them to explain policies and voting records.

Asset owners, such as pension funds, also faced MPs  criticism. The report said the  primary responsibility for changing the environment on executive pay rests with asset owners, rather than asset managers . It recommended that the new stewardship code should require them to provide more information on their objectives in relation to executive pay.

 We call for greater transparency in the way they set investment objectives, including on executive pay, and that the regulator is given powers to take effective action against those who do not meet their responsibilities under a revised stewardship code. 

The committee concluded that improved stewardship alone was not the answer, recommending drastic measures such as an absolute cap on total remuneration for executives in any year, allowing workers to sit on committees and including staff in profit-sharing schemes.

George Dallas, policy director at the International Corporate Governance Network, said stewardship could be improved but argued that concern over the lack of trust in business and economic inequality was political and separate to the job of investors, which is to ensure good returns for clients.

 There is room for improvement [but] public policy is not necessarily their role,  he said.

Sarah Wilson, chief executive of Minerva Analytics, a consultancy that advises shareholders, welcomed the committee s findings. She said it would increase scrutiny on shareholders who backed lavish pay deals but warned it would be difficult to implement the recommendation that boards should set an absolute cap on total pay.

 That s very challenging because it would seem to be a wages policy, which is not left open to the market.

WSJ Pro
Stanford, Anthem Blue Cross Set Out to Address AI Bias,  Black Box' Problem in Health Care; Partners look to build confidence and trust in machine-based decisions relating to treatment and prices

By John Murawski 
476 words
27 March 2019
19:43
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Corrections & Amplifications

Anthem Blue Cross insures 8.4 million people in California. An earlier version of this article incorrectly said it insures nearly 40 million people in the state. (March 27, 2019)

Anthem Blue Cross and Stanford University are working together to develop artificial intelligence algorithms for health care that can make unbiased, transparent decisions about insurance prices and treatment approvals.

The health insurer, a Thousand Oaks, Calif.-based subsidiary of Anthem Inc., said in a statement last week it will share the results of the research project with affiliated health plans in 11 states.

Anthem Blue Cross, which insures 8.4 million people in California, will become the first member of Stanford's AI for Health Affiliation program, a new research initiative within the university's Department of Computer Science. Founding members must contribute $300,000 a year and are expected to be members for at least three years, a spokesman for Stanford said.

The increased use of artificial intelligence in business is prompting questions about the soundness of AI-based decisions that could potentially affect who gets a car loan, who is selected for marketing specials or who is cleared to use costly new medications. The U.S. Food and Drug Administration last year approved the first AI-powered medical device to help diagnose strokes in patients by analyzing digital images of the brain.

The Stanford initiative will initially focus on developing AI for patient engagement and health-care transparency to help consumers understand their options and make informed decisions, the announcement said.

Over time, the Stanford collaboration will address two well-known and persistent AI challenges: the risk of bias, and the "black box problem" of explaining how an AI system arrived at a particular decision, said Rajeev Ronanki, senior vice president and chief digital officer of Indianapolis, Ind.-based Anthem Inc.

Anthem Blue Cross is looking to develop fair, interpretable AI that in the future could justify rates and pricing to the public and to regulators, back up medical diagnoses used to approve or deny coverage, and provide evidence in claims denials or disputes.

Anthem already uses AI to help staff make decisions in some areas, including billing and prior authorizations for treatment, Mr. Ronanki said.

For Anthem, seeking internal confirmation of its AI-based decisions is as important as convincing outsiders, Mr. Ronanki said. The company hasn't yet determined how making AI easier to explain or less biased could change the way it interacts with regulators or customers, Mr. Ronanki said.

With regard to bias, Anthem is trying to get ahead of potential mishaps. The issue is not theoretical: AI systems have in some cases been found to favor whites and males in job applications, for instance.

Bias is "a challenge that we see coming," Mr. Ronanki said. "Even internally we want to be sure [AI] is accurate."

Arby's Parent Taps Sephora Executive as CIO; Raghu Sagi, known for 'cutting-edge technologies in the retail space,' will help Inspire Brands improve online ordering and mobile payments

The owner of Arby's and Buffalo Wild Wings is tapping a high-end-retail technology leader as its chief information officer, as the newly merged restaurant chains seek to leverage digital tools to improve online ordering, mobile payments and other services.

Inspire Brands Inc., "https://www.wsj.com/articles/arbys-buffalo-wild-wings-merge-to-compete-in-fast-changing-industry-1517841000" after Arby's Restaurant Group acquired Buffalo Wild Wings Inc., said this week that Raghu Sagi will take on his new role April 15. He will lead a team of more than 200 IT professionals, a company spokesman said.

Mr. Sagi, who for the past two years served as chief engineering officer at Sephora America, succeeds departing CIO Darla Morse, who is leaving to oversee IT at CKE Restaurants Holdings Inc., the parent company of Carl's Jr. and Hardee's restaurants, Inspire Brands said. He will report to Inspire Chief Executive Paul Brown.

"We have the opportunity to build something that will set us apart in the industry," Mr. Sagi told CIO Journal. Among his priorities will be to "further develop our digital ecosystem to differentiate and personalize the guest experience while also driving brand loyalty," he said.

For two years, Mr. Sagi has served as chief engineering officer at Sephora America, the beauty retailer owned by Paris-based luxury-goods company LVMH Mot Hennessy Louis Vuitton SE.

At Sephora he held a range of tech posts since 2011, including vice president of digital and retail technology and vice president of engineering. Earlier, he was director of engineering at Walmart Inc. and an applications architect at Best Buy Co.

Based in Atlanta, Inspire Brands oversees more than 8,300 restaurants, including Arby's, Buffalo Wild Wings, Sonic Drive-In and Rusty Taco locations world-wide. Together, they serve more than 180 million guests every year, the company said.

Mr. Sagi will oversee efforts to leverage "existing and emerging technologies" across the chain to improve customer service, the company said.

Mr. Brown, in a statement, described the CIO role as instrumental to the company's digital strategies, adding that many tech initiatives Mr. Sagi led in the past are "widely considered the benchmark for the implementation of cutting-edge technologies in the retail space." These include improving data insights at Sephora to boost personalization and customer loyalty capabilities, among other projects.

Across the restaurant industry, the most common customer-facing tech tools include guest Wi-Fi services, online ordering or reservations, mobile payments and other smartphone apps, according to the National Restaurant Association.

More advanced capabilities, such as predictive ordering or automated menu-price adjustments based on demand, require data analytics and artificial-intelligence skills that are in high demand across all industries.

Technology can help restaurant operators increase sales and productivity, while providing an edge over competitors, the trade group says. The most common barrier to tech adoption is cost, followed by a lack of infrastructure and staff training, it says.

 Artificial Intelligence  Daily: Stanford, Anthem Address Bias Issues; the Adversarial AI Problem; Google Creates Ethics Council

1,425 words

12:38
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Stanford, Anthem out to address the  block box' issue. Anthem Blue Cross and Stanford University are working together to develop artificial intelligence algorithms for health care that can make unbiased, transparent decisions about insurance prices and treatment approvals, reports WSJ Pro's John Murawski.

Anthem Blue Cross, which insures nearly 40 million people in California, will become the first member of Stanford's AI for Health Affiliation program, a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/stanford-anthem-blue-cross-set-out-to-address-ai-bias-black-box-problem-in-health-care-11553679051?" within the university's Department of Computer Science. The health insurer, a Thousand Oaks, Calif.-based subsidiary of Anthem Inc., said in a statement last week it will share the results of the research project with affiliated health plans in 11 states.

The increased use of artificial intelligence in business is prompting questions about the soundness of AI-based decisions that could potentially affect who gets a car loan, who is selected for marketing specials or who is cleared to use costly new medications.

Tackling key issues. The Stanford initiative, over time, will address two well-known and persistent AI challenges: the risk of bias and the "black box problem" of explaining how an AI system arrived at a particular decision, said Rajeev Ronanki, senior vice president and chief digital officer of Anthem.

Anthem's aim. Anthem Blue Cross is looking to develop fair, interpretable AI that in the future could justify rates and pricing to the public and to regulators, back up medical diagnoses used to approve or deny coverage, and provide evidence in claims denials or disputes. Anthem already uses AI to help staff make decisions in some areas, including billing and prior authorizations for treatment, Mr. Ronanki said.

Adversarial AI

Why adversarial AI could be a big problem. Dawn Song, a professor at the University of California, Berkeley, specializing in security risks involved with AI and machine learning, said adversarial machine learning methods could be used to attack just about any system built on the technology, reports MIT Technology Review.

Defining the term. Adversarial machine learning involves a bit of experimentation. The aim is to feed data into an algorithm to reveal the information it has been trained on or distorting an input and causing the system to malfunction. By putting tons of images into a computer vision algorithm, for example, someone can reverse-engineer its functioning and get it to produce certain outputs, including incorrect ones, according to MIT Technology Review.

Examples. Ms. Song provided some examples of adversarial-learning. One project, which was done with Alphabet Inc.'s Google, involved probing a machine-learning algorithm that was programmed to generate automatic responses from emails. The results demonstrated that by creating the right messages, the model could be made to output sensitive data such as credit card numbers. Google used the findings to prevent Smart Compose, a tool that automatically generates text in Gmail, from being abused.

Adversarial AI, with its huge potential to be used both defensively and offensively, is of particular interest to the defense systems industry. This year, the Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency began a project called Guaranteeing AI Robustness against Deception to study adversarial machine learning.

Ethics

Google establishes ethics council. Alphabet Inc.'s Google is establishing an advisory council to address the ethical issues around AI and other emerging technologies, reports Reuters.

At the table. The council includes technology experts, digital ethicists, and people with public policy backgrounds, according to Kent Walker, Google's senior vice president for global affairs. Among its members are Joanna Bryson, an associate professor in computing at the University of Bath; William J. Burns, a former U.S. deputy secretary of state; and Dyan Gibbens, chief executive of drone startup Trumbull, according to a Google blog post.

The goal. The council's mission is to provide recommendations for Google and others working on technologies such as facial recognition software. The group is expected to release a report at the end of the year.

Quotable

"When I see it applied to something that without AI, it is just too complex, we never would have seen how that system works, that I feel like,  Wow, that is a very good thing.'"

  Microsoft co-founder Bill Gates

Customer Service

AI at your service. AI is on the verge of a mass adoption by customer service organizations as public expectations have risen to the point that customers now expect organizations to anticipate their needs, reports ZDNet.

Growing fast. Currently, fewer than a quarter of customer service organizations use chatbots, but AI adoption is projected to grow 143% in the next two years, ZDNet said, citing a recent report from Salesforce, the digital sales and marketing platform company. The most common use of AI right now is handling repetitive tasks, such as asking customers for basic information, helping reset passwords or providing status updates on order shipments. AI will free up sales agents from such mundane tasks to acting as "strategic client advocates."

The businesses that get the most out of AI will be the ones that see the technology not merely as a labor-saving device but as a liberator of human potential.

Innovators

Unsung women powered  intelligence' behind first digital computer. Male physicists and engineers were the public face for ENIAC, the world's first general-purpose electronic computer, but six women working at the University of Pennsylvania in the mid-1940s were the brains, IEEE Spectrum reports.

No acclaim. The group was responsible for configuring the 6,000-switch mega machine to perform specific computations, and they sometimes had to climb inside to replace parts. But they received virtually no acknowledgment at the time, in part because programming ENIAC was closely associated with human computation "and was therefore considered to be a type of  subprofessional' women's work," said the report.

Drones

Humans dominate AI in drone racing. Simulations are one thing, but performance tests in the real world show there is still work to be done when it comes to AI-controlled drones, according to the New York Times.

Three-dimensional spaces contain more unknowns than a simulation can offer. This creates unforeseen challenges for programmers. NASA's Jet Propulsion Laboratory, along with other companies, challenged world-class drone pilots to race their machines. With a commendable speed for automated drones being 5.6 miles per hour, there was little competition.

Take it slow. The images AI uses to navigate become blurred as the flying speed of a drone increases. This causes the machine to confuse where it is in a three-dimensional space. While Alphabet Inc.'s AI team, DeepMind, has created systems that consistently best human players in computer games, AI has a long way to go to compete with humans.

"For autonomous drones to be useful in disaster zones as delivery vehicles or in rural areas they will need to be able to fly far, fast and without human oversight, often in environments where they can't rely on external guidance systems, like GPS," according to the Times.

Automation

Worries of an AI takeover. Should AI ever prove to be harmful to humans, it probably won't come in the form of The Terminator. OpenAI's Paul Christiano categorized two disaster scenarios human irrelevance and humans being influenced by AI, reports Vox. Both are theoretical, but as automated machines play a larger role in society, a failure could prove to be devastating.

"I definitely think humans are bad at precisely stating what they want, and that combined with a powerful enough technology, human failure could lead to very bad outcomes," Vox said.

Governance

Regulators give the go-ahead for private sector innovation. Roll Call reported on enthusiasm in the financial services industry after receiving encouragement from regulators. The news comes as a call-to-action for banks and consulting firms to increase protection against "money laundering, terrorist financing and other illicit activity," regulators said. Navigant's managing editor, Tim Mueller, said the new rush is reminiscent of banks using the World Wide Web to bolster business.

In Brief

The CEO of Alphabet Inc.'s Google will meet with the chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, following criticism about Google's artificial intelligence work in China. (Bloomberg)

Food Lion and five sister U.S. grocery chains are turning to artificial intelligence to overhaul the way they order food from suppliers. (WSJ)

The 2018 Turing Award, known as the "Nobel Prize of computing," was given to three researchers who laid the foundations for the boom in artificial intelligence. (The Verge)

News
Inside Manhattan's 'Silicon Valley' fund

Robin Wigglesworth 
2,363 words
27 March 2019
Financial Times
FTFTA
1; Usa
7
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Notoriously secretive, DE Shaw mixes quantitative investing with common sense to manage $50bn in assets. But as rivals adapt this hybrid approach, the hedge fund faces a fight to keep its edge in the market. By Robin Wigglesworth

I n 1988, Revolution Books, a tatty Communist bookstore near New York's Union Square, got some strange new upstairs neighbours: a bunch of geeky programmers trying to crack the code to financial markets.

In the early days, the embryonic hedge fund founded by David Shaw, a former computer science professor at Columbia University, was a ramshackle start-up. Exposed pipes and extension cords meant that tripping on a cable could take out its entire trading system. Yet today DE Shaw is one of the hedge fund industry's biggest players, managing over $50bn of assets.

It has enjoyed some mainstream fame as the place where a young Jeff Bezos first worked on what would ultimately become Amazon. But most importantly for a wider investment industry desperately trying to reinvent itself for the 21st century, DE Shaw has evolved dramatically from the algorithmic, computerdriven "quantitative" trading it helped pioneer in the 1980s.

It is now a leader in combining quantitative investing with traditional "fundamental" strategies driven by humans, such as stockpicking. This symbiosis has been dubbed "quantamental" by asset managers now attempting to do the same. Many in the industry believe this is the future, and are rushing to hire computer scientists to help realise the benefits of big data and artificial intelligence in their strategies.

Eric Schmidt, the former Google chairman who owns a 20 per cent stake in DE Shaw, predicts that this approach will profoundly reshape the investment management industry. "People have gone insane about this, but in a good way," Mr Schmidt says. "We are at the beginning of a new era in artificial intelligence. These technologies should benefit investing as well."

There are plenty of pitfalls though, with experts warning that poor implementation can lead to disastrous results. Wall Street has seen several cycles of quant hype before, and many remain sceptical that traditional firms can retool their culture sufficiently to unlock the potential advantages of a more hybrid approach.

The combination of DE Shaw's performance and the secrecy around exactly what it does both vexes and fascinates rivals and counterparties. "They're like a calibrated machine that can respond to nearly every market," says the head of an investment bank's hedge fund trading desk. In a series of interviews with senior DE Shaw executives, the Financial Times has had a rare glimpse of how the "machine" operates.

Geek show Little known outside investing's arcane corners, DE Shaw is the fourth-highest grossing hedge fund group of all time, having made over $29bn for its investors since those early days near Union Square, according to LCH Investments.

Last year its flagship $14bn Composite Fund which has been closed to new investors since 2013 returned over 11 per cent to investors net of fees, despite the turmoil in financial markets. That was its seventh double-digit gain of the past decade, over which period it has not suffered a losing year. Its $7.6bn "macro" fund, Oculus, returned 5.9 per cent in 2018, and the $7bn stocksfocused Valence fund made 8 per cent. Even among peers on Wall Street, DE Shaw is still a largely unknown quantity. "They're really smart, but I've never quite understood them," says one quant hedge fund manager. "They are one of those places where you just don't know exactly what [it is] they do, except that it is some mix of quantitative and discretionary investing."

This hybrid approach is not new. DE Shaw ventured out of its quantitative roots soon after its founding. But it now manages a wide array of strategies, ranging from completely machinedriven and dizzyingly complex, to human and artisanal, such as "distressed debt" investing and activism. Roughly half of the $50bn it manages are invested in quant strategies, and the rest in discretionary or more hybrid funds.

"The world tends to view quantitative and fully discretionary investing as distinct and separate, but the opportunity set [to make money] is not as cleanly divided," says Max Stone, one of five members of DE Shaw's executive committee, along with Eddie Fishman, Eric Wepsic, Julius Gaudio and Anne Dinning.

Some rivals question whether it has departed too far from its roots. For instance, Two Sigma a major quant hedge fund started by former senior DE Shaw executives has eschewed their former colleagues' hybrid methods.

DE Shaw executives stress that their one constant is to have a data-driven "quanty" approach across the board, whether it is in high-speed arbitrage or investing in renewable energy. "Our core strength is thinking scientifically about things, so it doesn't feel like we are wandering away from our roots," says Alexis Halaby, head of investor relations at the company.

It currently employs about 1,300 people, which includes more than 80 PhDs and 25 International Math Olympiad medal holders. All interviewees at DE Shaw face a series of analytical questions to demonstrate their suitability to work there something even former US Treasury secretary Larry Summers had to go through ahead of a stint at the fund in 2006.

That approach seeps through into the culture, say observers. Mahmood Noorani, a former hedge fund manager who now leads Quant Insight, an analytics company, describes the people at DE Shaw as "less alpha male and more gentle scientists".

This has helped the company survive the type of leadership transition that has felled some rivals. Most hedge funds see their fortunes fade once their founder steps down, but DE Shaw has thrived since Mr Shaw, 67, semi-retired in the early 2000s to pursue research into "computational biochemistry".

Fittingly for a company started above a bookstore selling Marxist treatises, the day-to-day running of the hedge fund is now handled by the central committee of five, rather than a single, imperial impresario typical of the industry. "You'd be hard-pressed to find a management textbook that says a committee is a good way of running a company," says Mr Stone. "But it works for us."

This was among the factors that attracted Mr Schmidt when he scooped up the 20 per cent stake in DE Shaw held by the bankrupt estate of Lehman Brothers in 2015. "It feels like Silicon Valley in Manhattan," he says. "People get consumed by hierarchy, but the evidence shows that flat structures and diverse teams operating collectively have better outcomes."

Sometimes things go awry, however.

In an unusually public spat for a company that shuns publicity, DE Shaw last year fired Daniel Michalow, a senior fund manager, after an internal review found "gross violations of our standards and values".

In an open letter Mr Michalow conceded that he might have deserved his dismissal for being "an abrasive boss" but insisted that his departure was not related to any sexual misconduct. He did, however, paint a very different picture of DE Shaw criticising the hedge fund for "lavish, alcohol-filled parties" and said visits to strip clubs and senior employee relationships with their juniors were common. DE Shaw declined to comment on the accusations, citing ongoing legal proceedings. Mr Michalow is suing DE Shaw for defamation and other claims relating to the announcement of his termination.

The hedge fund's executives are happier to discuss how it manages money, even if the details can be opaque. DE Shaw runs some quant strategies so complex or quick that they are in practice almost beyond human understanding something that many quantitative analysts are reluctant to concede.

The goal is to find patterns on the fuzzy edge of observability in financial markets, so faint that they haven't already been exploited by other quants. They then hoard as many of these signals as possible and systematically mine them until they run dry and repeat the process. These can range from tiny, fleeting arbitrage opportunities between closely-linked stocks that only machines can detect, to using new alternative data sets such as satellite imagery and mobile phone data to get a better understanding of a company's results.

Common sense investing Yet, the hedge fund's executives say they also frequently use common sense to overrule their algorithms, another anathema in an industry where human tinkering can be considered a foible.

Some of these manual interventions are obvious. When Russia annexed the Crimean part of Ukraine in 2014 and started fomenting unrest in its eastern province, DE Shaw quickly dialled back its exposure to the Moscow stock market. And after the Volkswagen emission cheating scandal erupted a year later another of the unexpected shocks that machines are ill-equipped to deal with it pared back bets on the carmaker.

Other strategies require a heavier human hand, such as taking advantage of periodically wide discrepancies between Tencent and Naspers, the South African holding company that owns nearly a third of the Chinese tech giant. Normally they trade in lockstep, but sometimes they diverge because of emerging market stress or South African politics opening up a valuable opportunity. The optimal time to pounce can be modelled, but is best paired with the discretion of a human fund manager.

Yet, DE Shaw still sees plenty of opportunities in the quantitative investing side, especially its "long-only", nonhedge fund investing business, DE Shaw Investment Management. DESIM has quintupled in size since 2011 and now manages $24bn. To grow this further, the company is expanding into something dubbed "risk premia", systematically exploiting theoretically timeless drivers of returns, such as the tendency for smaller or cheaper stocks to outperform the overall market over time.

Historically these have been factors that hedge funds might explicitly or indirectly harness and charge hefty fees for but they have now been packaged up into simpler, cheaper vehicles by the likes of AQR and BlackRock.

DE Shaw is also ramping up its investment in computer science, setting up a semi-independent machine learning research group and investing in a quantum computing start-up.

It is early days, but Cedo Crnkovic, a managing director at DE Shaw, says a fully-functioning quantum computer could potentially prove revolutionary. "Computing power drives everything, and sets a limit to what we can do, so exponentially more computing power would be transformative," he says.

March of technology Nearly every traditional investment company is scrambling to hire data scientists, programmers and technologists, and turn themselves into human-machine hybrids. DE Shaw's apparent success in bridging those two worlds offers an alluring template for rivals.

However, many "pure" quants are sceptical that traditional asset managers have the cultural architecture needed to make a success, arguing that companies cannot just hire a bunch of computer scientists, tell them to work with 50-year-old fund managers with MBAs and hope that magic will ensue. Others fret that by not fully grasping the limitations, they might even do damage to themselves, and or investors.

Mr Stone has a stuffed albino peacock sitting in his office, a reminder that sometimes markets like nature serve up the unexpected. He is wary of criticising the quantamental rush, but also cautions that it could end in tears.

"There are some good ideas at the intersection of systematic and discretionary investing," he says. Nonetheless, "if you don't have experience of separating signal from noise," he adds, "you can easily be led astray by extraneous data."

Kicking on DE Shaw has thrived since its founder semi-retired to pursue 'computational biochemistry' research Human lead The fund's executives often use common sense to overrule algorithms in areas like political risk 'Premia' play Its 'long-only' non-hedge fund investing business has quintupled in size since 2011, managing $24bn 'Crowding' fears Will quants be able to ride out the next crisis? For many, the summer of 1998 was defined by the Monica Lewinsky scandal enveloping the White House, the Spice Girls losing a member and France's stunning victory at the football World Cup. But for the finance industry, it will forever be remembered as the period when some of the finest minds on Wall Street came undone.

The collapse of Long-Term Capital Management, the hedge fund led by Salomon Brothers' former star trader John Meriwether and advised by Nobel laureates Myron Scholes and Robert Merton, hogged the headlines. But the market maelstrom also nearly killed DE Shaw, which had made many of the same trades as LTCM with similarly massive dollops of leverage. "The market environment was harrowing," says Eddie Fishman, who now sits on the DE Shaw executive committee. "But the lessons served us well in subsequent crises."

The 1998 crisis, and the "quant quake" in August 2007, are reminders that even the most sophisticated computerpowered strategies can implode. Given the popularity of quantitative investing and the competition to find and mine new trading signals, there are concerns that markets are primed for a rerun.

History indicates that the two main dangers are leverage and "crowding". The toxicity of aggressive leverage whether debt or through derivatives is well-known, but too many investors crowding into the same security or trade can also cause severe damage, especially when trading conditions deteriorate quickly.

While most hedge funds use far less leverage than in 1998 or 2007, the sheer amount of money raised by quant funds since the crisis is leading to fears of crowding, lowering returns for everyone and ultimately raising the risk of an abrupt reversal, which would in turn lead to a crash as burnt investors dash for the exit.

'You just don't know exactly what it is they [DE Shaw] do, except that it is some mix of quantitative and discretionary investing'
