VC Daily: Y Combinator Goes Seed to Weed | Insiders in Biotech IPOs Are Big-Time Boosters

1,131 words
18 March 2019
12:05
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. Madness. That's what many observers have said about the idea of virtual-reality entertainment as a viable consumer market.

Now madness of another kind is at hand: March Madness. The annual tradition of the NCAA national basketball tournament returns this week. And for people who are serious fans of college hoops as well as a little curious about VR and sports, there will be virtual madness.

CBS and its broadcast partners will provide live VR coverage of the tournament for people using Oculus and Gear VR headsets. If this experience is as compelling as some critics have suggested, this could mean a lot more excitement about home VR possibilities. And a lot more madness, too.

And now on to the news...

In the News

Seed to weed: A new chapter is taking shape for a once-taboo slice of the venture ecosystem.

The presence of three cannabis businesses in Y Combinator's cohort of roughly 200 startups signals a shift in Silicon Valley.

Until now, major venture-capital firms eschewed the "plant-touching" category, betting instead on startups that don't actually handle marijuana crops or processed plants.

Venture-backed companies such as Eaze Solutions LLC, an online marketplace for cannabis products not funded by Y Combinator, have been the model for VC firms whose charters barred them from backing "plant-touching" businesses.

"We are definitely plant-touching, and most big-name investors are still scared of that," said cannabis distributor Nabis LLC Chief Executive Vince Ning. "YC is the first one that took that bet." WSJ Pro's Heather Mack has the story.

Ready for Lyft-off: Lyft Inc. plans to peg its valuation at between $21 billion and $23 billion when the ride-hailing service kicks off the roadshow to market its initial public offering Monday, according to people familiar with the matter.

The range, equating to between $62 and $68 a share, is preliminary and could change by the time the shares start trading around the end of next week, the people said.

Lyft plans to raise up to $2.09 billion in the offering, according to an amended prospectus filed Monday morning.

A final IPO price will be based on feedback from investors in the roadshow. The WSJ's Maureen Farrell has the exclusive.

Street duty: As Andreessen Horowitz gets ready for Lyft to go public, another one of its portfolio companies is following suit.

PagerDuty Inc., which helps companies manage their web operations, has unveiled its paperwork to hold an initial public offering.

Andreessen Horowitz is the company's largest shareholder, owning 18.4%. Accel is next at 12.3%, followed by Bessemer Venture Partners with 12.2%.

San Francisco-based PagerDuty has raised about $173 million in private capital since it was founded in 2009 and was valued at $1.3 billion in a funding round last year. WSJ Pro's Katie Roof has the story.

Gormley's Take: In Biotech IPOs, Former Danger Signal is Now Sign of Strength

By Brian Gormley

Biotech IPO buyers once viewed heavy insider participation with suspicion a sign the company can't attract new investors. Now they see it as an endorsement.

Instead of depending on venture capitalists, more entrepreneurs also are bringing public-market investors into venture rounds.

Last year, insiders purchased shares in 77% of health-care IPOs, which were predominantly biotech stock sales, according to law firm Proskauer Rose LLP. Existing investors bought shares in 25% of all other IPOs.

"The market has become much more comfortable seeing insiders purchasing a higher percentage," said Daniel Forman, an attorney who advises Proskauer clients on IPOs and other deals. "If you were to see that five and definitely 10 years ago, it would have been a red flag."

More in Gormley's Take

Sports media and technology platform Minute Media acquired The Big Lead, a digital sports and news publication, for an undisclosed sum. New York- and Tel Aviv-based Minute Media is backed by investors including Battery Ventures, Dawn Capital, Gemini Ventures, Goldman Sachs, Qumra Capital, Remagine Ventures and Vintage Investments.

Accel has raised about $2.5 billion for three new funds, including $525 million for Accel XIV LP, $500 million for Accel Leaders Fund II LP and $1.5 billion for Accel Growth Fund V LP. WSJ Pro previously wrote about Accel raising the funds.

New Money

Igneous Inc., a Seattle-based provider of a hybrid cloud backup and archive service for unstructured data, raised $25 million in Series C funding. WestRiver Group led the round, with participation from Madrona Venture Group, New Enterprise Associates, Vulcan Capital and Redpoint Ventures. "https://www.igneous.io"

Aeponyx Inc., a Montreal-based micro optical switch maker, closed a 7.9 million Canadian dollar ($5.9 million) Series A investment. Pangaea Ventures led the round, which includes participation from investors including Fonds InnovExport and Ecofuel Fund. "http://www.aeponyx.com"

Ovation.io, a Cambridge, Mass.-based provider of diagnostic lab software, picked up $5 million in Series A funding. Madrona Venture Group led the round, with participation from Borealis Ventures and others. "https://www.ovation.io"

Populus, a San Francisco-based startup helping cities access data to plan for shared scooters, bikes and cars, landed $3.1 million in seed funding from investors including Relay Ventures and Precursor Ventures. Charles Hudson, managing partner of Precursor, joined the company's board. "https://www.populus.ai"

ProdPerfect Inc., a Boston-based startup providing quality assurance testing for web applications, was seeded with a $2.6 million investment. Eniac Ventures and Fika Ventures led the round, and were joined by Entrepreneurs Roundtable Accelerator. "https://prodperfect.com"

Additional investors including Bertelsmann Digital Media Investments, Ascend Venture Capital, Techstars and Capital Factory also participated. "https://storyfit.com"

Industry News

Naspers Ltd., Africa's biggest company best known for its bet on Chinese internet giant Tencent Holdings Ltd., is building a foothold in Silicon Valley. But unlike many other tech investors, Naspers isn't solely focused on finding its next big hit here. The WSJ's Alexandra Wexler has the story.

Big Issues: Personal Finance (A Special Report) --- Is Now the Time for Active Investing to Make a Comeback?

1,533 words
18 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
R2
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Few rifts in the world of mutual funds are as pronounced as the one dividing advocates of active management from those of index, or passive, investing.

For years, the flow of new assets, reflecting investor preferences, has tilted more to low-cost index and exchange-traded funds than to active funds.

Index funds have boomed in the decades since their introduction by the late John Bogle because they capture the returns of entire markets and so eliminate the "middleman," the stock-picking fund manager, with his trading costs and fees. When markets rise over time, the passive camp says, it only dilutes one's profits to pay premiums for managers who mostly, in any given year, fail to outperform the indexes of the markets in which they specialize.

But active management's supporters say the game has changed in recent years. Many active managers have had to lower their fees to remain competitive. And proponents believe that the reduced gap in expenses in many cases is more than justified by the expertise some managers offer -- especially in these volatile times.

---

WSJ Pro
Mortgage Providers Look to AI to Process Home Loans Faster; The technology is speedier than manually processing mortgage applications, but some worry it also is more prone to errors

By John Murawski 
946 words
18 March 2019
08:53
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

The home loan industry is looking to artificial intelligence to shorten the wait times for a mortgage approval, from a month or more to a tolerable week or two.

A speedy loan turnaround not only moves capital for banks and investors; it also reduces the reliance on costly human labor.

"Mortgages take forever to close," said Al Pascual, senior vice president of research at Javelin Strategy & Research in Pleasanton, Calif. "If they can cut a week off the mortgage process, that's meaningful."

The industry took a step in that direction last month, when Ellie Mae Inc., a major player in home loans, started offering an AI option to its 3,000 customers banks and other lenders to speed-read mortgage applications for accuracy and completeness.

Ellie Mae, a Pleasanton-based software and services company that processes about 40% of all U.S. home mortgage applications, selected an AI technology developed by Wakefield, Mass.-based home loan data automation outfit AI Foundry.

"They will market their services directly to our customers," said Joe Tyrrell, Ellie Mae's executive vice president for strategy and technology.

While AI is light years faster than people in reading documents, human loan analysts will be necessary to manually validate the loan documents that intelligent machine can't decipher. And in the home-loan business, there will never be a shortage of crumpled, corrupted or esoteric documents.

Ellie Mae's loan platform makes sure the loan packet is complete and properly filled out, so that lenders can act on it. The company has been using automation to read credit reports, title reports and home appraisals, which are submitted by institutions and come in standardized formats.

The average loan takes 52 days to process, but Ellie Mae has been able to reduce that to 30 days, Mr. Tyrrell said. The company hopes to reduce that to 10 days by further harnessing AI, he said.

But the loan industry has been constrained by a missing link in its AI initiatives: reading personal documents submitted by home buyers. These include W2 forms, pay stubs, tax forms, divorce decrees, bank statements and other documents that validate the buyer's finances. Many of these documents are irregular and have been more difficult to automate.

AI Foundry's technology, called Agile Mortgages Solution, currently is being used by about a dozen banks and lenders, said Steve Butler, the company's founder and general manager. It takes less than five minutes to read several hundred pages of documents that a human would need an hour to slog through, he said.

"The mortgage industry is ripe for this because it is so document intensive," Mr. Butler said. AI Foundry is a three-year-old business unit of Kodak Alaris Holdings Ltd.

Still, the system also needs some human help. Users have to train the AI to recognize when it can't read a loan document, or to ask for help when its confidence level is shaky, so that a person can intervene.

Mortgage processing takes so long in large part because lenders must comply with safeguards established after the subprime mortgage crisis and 2007-09 recession, said Craig Focardi, a senior analyst in the banking practice of Boston-based research firm Celent.

Problematic paperwork is a concern for more than just regulators. Institutional investors who buy the bundled loans on the secondary market are aggressive about spotting defects in documents on loans that have defaulted.

"The big risk is being told by someone who bought the [defaulted] loan that now you have to buy it back," Mr. Tyrrell said. "The investor can look at the documents for anything that was missed. There's a lot at stake if the [originating lenders] get it wrong."

Mr. Butler said the mortgage papers come in as "one huge PDF, all connected." So Agile Mortgages must first learn how to identify, separate and classify the incoming documents. Then AI has to read the files, extract the relevant data from the appropriate line or box, and enter it into the bank's system.

One of the first AI Foundry customers, Radius Financial Group Inc. in Norwell, Mass., has been using Agile Mortgages since 2017. Keith Polaski, chief operating officer at Radius, said it took about six months to train the AI on three years of mortgage applications, spanning 180 different types of documents. After 20 months of ingesting data, Agile Mortgages has improved its accuracy rate to about 85% from 40% of documents it can recognize and read correctly, Mr. Polaski said.

Humans are about 97% accurate, based on industry data. Mr. Butler said the ultimate goal for Agile Mortgages is to match human performance. Mr. Polaski said it is only a matter of time until Agile Mortgages is so reliable it will be able to approve and issue some portion of home loans without human assistance.

The volume of paperwork Agile Mortgages can process in one year for Radius is equivalent to the labor of 10 loan analysts performing 6,500 hours of work, at a combined cost of $227,500, for classification and indexing. Agile Mortgages has "freed them up by two hours per day to do human brain power things," Mr. Polaski said.

AI won't attain perfection anytime soon. Radius doesn't train Agile Mortgages on about 50 types of mortgage documents that are so uncommon they will always have to be handled manually, Mr. Polaski said. They include payout verifications from wills, insurance, lotteries and casinos.

News
Shake-up fails to please shareholders

OWEN WALKER ;SIOBHAN RIDING 
1,064 words
18 March 2019
Financial Times
FTFTA
1; Main
6
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

ANALYSIS STANDARD LIFE ABERDEEN

ACTIVE MANAGEMENT Co-leadership is axed but group's problems show no sign of abating

M artin Gilbert's decision to step down as cochief executive of Standard Life Aberdeen last week ended one of the longest reigns in European asset management.

After setting up Aberdeen Asset Management in 1983 and merging it with Standard Life in 2017, Mr Gilbert is now vice-chairman of the group and chairs its investment division. Keith Skeoch, who led Standard Life into the merger, is sole chief executive.

But the change, which followed shortly after the arrival of Douglas Flint as chairman, will do little to appease shareholders. Since the merger their paper losses, taking into account the 1.3bn returned to shareholders, are 36 per cent.

One investor said the announcement was "delusional" and "depressing" for shareholders. He said: "The market wants both to go ... and [to see] an external appointment."

Two shareholders that were big backers of the merger have given up on the project, which analysts viewed as a defensive tie-up of midsized active managers that were struggling to compete against passive funds.

Lloyds Banking Group owned 9.9 per cent of Aberdeen before the union but sold its final stake in June after a quarrel over a 109bn investment mandate. Arbitration in that dispute should conclude this month.

SLA's biggest investor, Mitsubishi UFJ Trust and Banking Corporation of Japan, offloaded its stake five weeks ago. It had once owned 19.9 per cent of Aberdeen.

The loss of these two Aberdeen investors harmed Mr Gilbert's claim to the throne. His roles outside SLA had also raised eyebrows. Last year he took leave of absence from the board of Glencore, the miner, after violating UK governance rules on "overboarding" directors holding too many posts. At the time he was deputy chairman of Sky and coordinating its sale to Comcast. After leaving Sky, he resumed his job at Glencore. Giving up the role of co-chief executive at SLA has freed time for Mr Gilbert to take other positions. Last week Revolut, the digital bank facing questions over how it is managing its rapid growth, named him as an adviser to chief executive Nikolay Storonsky.

Mr Gilbert has other non-corporate roles and may add to his job portfolio as he nears retirement.

Overboarding is a controversial issue and several large shareholders including BlackRock, SLA's biggest, and Norway's sovereign wealth fund, a top-20 holder increasingly take a hard line.

While Mr Gilbert's jobs caused controversy, the issue did not affect Mr Skeoch, whose only significant position outside SLA is on the board of the soon-to-be-defunct Financial Reporting Council.

Meanwhile, shareholders resent Mr Gilbert's financial package in his new positions at SLA. His base salary will stay at 600,000 while his bonus potential is cut to 350 per cent from 600 per cent. "Gilbert staying on doing what he does and getting paid [that amount] is incredible," a shareholder said.

At the same time SLA's problems show no sign of abating. It had been hoped the merger would staunch outflows from flagship products, especially Standard Life's multi-asset Global Absolute Return Strategies (Gars) product and Aberdeen's emerging market equity funds, but redemptions accelerated as poor performance continued.

SLA bled a net 40.9bn in 2018, equivalent to 7 per cent of its assets at the start of the year, with most of the money flowing from equity and multi-asset funds.

The exodus of money affected revenues, and pre-tax profits for the group's core asset management business, excluding its stake in insurer Phoenix Group and joint ventures, fell by 15 per cent last year.

Industry observers expect outflows from the flagship products to continue. Gars in particular is challenged by the market environment, which has also taken a toll on rival absolute return products offered by Aviva Investors and Invesco.

After SLA announced its 2018 results last week, Mr Gilbert said the coming months would be a "tough time for asset management", adding: "We need to weather the storm."

Mr Skeoch said SLA was "working hard to deliver what is within our control". The company is investing in new asset classes, such as private markets; it launched 32 funds last year and made four bolt-on acquisitions.

SLA is also trying to improve performance, notably by implementing a new suite of risk-analytics tools and an integrated research platform. It is unclear how successful these efforts will be in the medium term. Analysts doubt SLA will be able to turn around the flagship products' performance or seed strategies that will quickly replace them as a revenue source. "It's a managed decline for the next few years, " says one analyst, speaking anonymously.

Gordon Aitken, analyst at RBC Capital Markets, notes that the My Folio multi-asset retail product has been successful but comes nowhere near to rivalling Gars, which at its peak managed 48bn.

Another lever SLA can pull is cost cuts. The company has already delivered most of the 350m annualised savings it promised as part of the merger and made 56m worth of extra cuts last year.

Yet these go only so far. As SLA's asset base falls, it will have to slash harder to make a difference.

Mr Aitken says SLA lost from the sale of its insurance business to Phoenix last year. SLA retains a 20 per cent stake in Phoenix and said in its results that it benefited from changes the insurer made to life expectancy assumptions.

RBC estimates that half the assumption changes were in the business acquired from SLA. "If SLA had continued to own [the insurance business] it would have benefited from 50 per cent of these rather than 20 per cent," says Mr Aitken.

SLA has begun to offload stakes in other side-businesses, such as HDFC, its Indian life insurance company. If it sells these entities in their entirety, it could invest the proceeds in its core business. Shareholders will watch with interest.

It is delusional and it is depressing. The market wants both to go ... and to see an external appointment MAJOR INVESTOR

The coming months will be a tough time for the industry ... we need to weather the storm MARTIN GILBERT

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in the UK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Single price: Based on a mid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows: ? 0001 to 1100 hours; ? 1101 to 1400 hours; ?1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge from fund managers/operators.

Features
Shake-up fails to please shareholders

OWEN WALKER ;SIOBHAN RIDING 
1,020 words
18 March 2019
Financial Times
FTFTA
1; Europe
6
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

ACTIVE MANAGEMENT Co-leadership is axed but group's problems show no sign of abating

M artin Gilbert's decision to step down as cochief executive of Standard Life Aberdeen last week ended one of the longest reigns in European asset management.

After setting up Aberdeen Asset Management in 1983 and merging it with Standard Life in 2017, Mr Gilbert is now vice-chairman of the group and chairs its investment division. Keith Skeoch, who led Standard Life into the merger, is sole chief executive.

But the change, which followed shortly after the arrival of Douglas Flint as chairman, will do little to appease shareholders. Since the merger their paper losses, taking into account the 1.3bn returned to shareholders, are 36 per cent.

One investor said the announcement was "delusional" and "depressing" for shareholders. He said: "The market wants both to go ... and [to see] an external appointment."

Two shareholders that were big backers of the merger have given up on the project, which analysts viewed as a defensive tie-up of midsized active managers that were struggling to compete against passive funds.

Lloyds Banking Group owned 9.9 per cent of Aberdeen before the union but sold its final stake in June after a quarrel over a 109bn investment mandate. Arbitration in that dispute should conclude this month.

SLA's biggest investor, Mitsubishi UFJ Trust and Banking Corporation of Japan, offloaded its stake five weeks ago. It had once owned 19.9 per cent of Aberdeen.

The loss of these two Aberdeen investors harmed Mr Gilbert's claim to the throne. His roles outside SLA had also raised eyebrows. Last year he took leave of absence from the board of Glencore, the miner, after violating UK governance rules on "overboarding" directors holding too many posts. At the time he was deputy chairman of Sky and coordinating its sale to Comcast. After leaving Sky, he resumed his job at Glencore. Giving up the role of co-chief executive at SLA has freed time for Mr Gilbert to take other positions. Last week Revolut, the digital bank facing questions over how it is managing its rapid growth, named him as an adviser to chief executive Nikolay Storonsky.

Mr Gilbert has other non-corporate roles and may add to his job portfolio as he nears retirement.

Overboarding is a controversial issue and several large shareholders including BlackRock, SLA's biggest, and Norway's sovereign wealth fund, a top-20 holder increasingly take a hard line.

While Mr Gilbert's jobs caused controversy, the issue did not affect Mr Skeoch, whose only significant position outside SLA is on the board of the soon-to-be-defunct Financial Reporting Council.

Meanwhile, shareholders resent Mr Gilbert's financial package in his new positions at SLA. His base salary will stay at 600,000 while his bonus potential is cut to 350 per cent from 600 per cent. "Gilbert staying on doing what he does and getting paid [that amount] is incredible," a shareholder said.

At the same time SLA's problems show no sign of abating. It had been hoped the merger would staunch outflows from flagship products, especially Standard Life's multi-asset Global Absolute Return Strategies (Gars) product and Aberdeen's emerging market equity funds, but redemptions accelerated as poor performance continued.

SLA bled a net 40.9bn in 2018, equivalent to 7 per cent of its assets at the start of the year, with most of the money flowing from equity and multi-asset funds.

The exodus of money affected revenues, and pre-tax profits for the group's core asset management business, excluding its stake in insurer Phoenix Group and joint ventures, fell by 15 per cent last year.

Industry observers expect outflows from the flagship products to continue. Gars in particular is challenged by the market environment, which has also taken a toll on rival absolute return products offered by Aviva Investors and Invesco.

After SLA announced its 2018 results last week, Mr Gilbert said the coming months would be a "tough time for asset management", adding: "We need to weather the storm."

Mr Skeoch said SLA was "working hard to deliver what is within our control". The company is investing in new asset classes, such as private markets; it launched 32 funds last year and made four bolt-on acquisitions.

SLA is also trying to improve performance, notably by implementing a new suite of risk-analytics tools and an integrated research platform. It is unclear how successful these efforts will be in the medium term. Analysts doubt SLA will be able to turn around the flagship products' performance or seed strategies that will quickly replace them as a revenue source. "It's a managed decline for the next few years, " says one analyst, speaking anonymously.

Gordon Aitken, analyst at RBC Capital Markets, notes that the My Folio multi-asset retail product has been successful but comes nowhere near to rivalling Gars, which at its peak managed 48bn.

Another lever SLA can pull is cost cuts. The company has already delivered most of the 350m annualised savings it promised as part of the merger and made 56m worth of extra cuts last year.

Yet these go only so far. As SLA's asset base falls, it will have to slash harder to make a difference.

Mr Aitken says SLA lost from the sale of its insurance business to Phoenix last year. SLA retains a 20 per cent stake in Phoenix and said in its results that it benefited from changes the insurer made to life expectancy assumptions.

RBC estimates that half the assumption changes were in the business acquired from SLA. "If SLA had continued to own [the insurance business] it would have benefited from 50 per cent of these rather than 20 per cent," says Mr Aitken.

SLA has begun to offload stakes in other side-businesses, such as HDFC, its Indian life insurance company. If it sells these entities in their entirety, it could invest the proceeds in its core business. Shareholders will watch with interest.

EXCHANGE --- Volume Soars Amid 'Quad Witching'

By Asjylyn Loder 
838 words
16 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Friday was the busiest trading day so far this year.

The session marked a quarterly collision that traders call "quad witching," when equity and index futures and options expire. Adding to the fun, dozens of S&P indexes, including the popular S&P 500, were scheduled to rebalance their holdings at the end of the day.

That means exchange-traded funds and other index traders needed to realign their portfolios to match their updated benchmarks, buying companies that were added and selling stocks that dropped out. By some estimates, ETFs needed to complete $100 billion in combined buying and selling.

"It's one of the five biggest rebalancing days of the year," said Josh Lukeman, a managing director at Credit Suisse.

Index changes often drive heavy trading volumes, especially at the end of the day, because portfolio managers want to make sure they achieve the same performance as their benchmark. When FTSE Russell's widely followed stock indexes rebalanced in June, a record 1.2 billion shares, worth more than $39 billion, traded in less than one second in Nasdaq's closing auction. At the time, $9.2 trillion were pegged to Russell benchmarks.

Heavy trading doesn't necessarily mean major price moves. The changes are broadcast well in advance, and traders and index funds have ample time to prepare. Major indexes started Friday with small gains and then drifted higher throughout much of the afternoon, buoyed by rallying technology shares.

The S&P 500 rose 0.5% to close at a fresh high for 2019, while the Nasdaq Composite advanced 0.8%. Both indexes posted weekly gains, with analysts attributing some of the moves to bets that central banks will suspend monetary tightening while they gauge the softening across the global economy.

It was a sharp contrast from Dec. 21 quad witching, when the index dropped more than 2%. On Friday, more than 10.4 billion shares traded, the most since then. Volume was 37% above the 60-day average.

"It tends to be quite orderly," Mr. Lukeman said.

In September, quad witching coincided with major changes to several popular ETFs pegged to sector indexes. S&P Dow Jones Indices and MSCI Inc. reclassified tech companies including Facebook Inc., Twitter Inc. and Google-parent Alphabet Inc. into a new "communication-services" segment. The move affected 23 S&P companies worth a combined $2.7 trillion.

For ETFs, conforming to these index changes might look to investors like a surge in new money piling into certain ETFs, followed a few days later by that same amount of money coming right back out. Such fund flows look a lot like the peaks and valleys on an electrocardiogram, earning them the nickname "heartbeat flows."

In the week leading up to last year's March witching, U.S. ETFs raised $45 billion, a weekly record. Some analysts mistook it as a sign that bullish sentiment had returned to the U.S. stock market. But more than half of the money came back out again within days.

For example, BlackRock Inc.'s iShares Select Dividend ETF saw a record single-day inflow of $3.6 billion on March 15, 2018, according to FactSet. The money came back out on March 19. There were similar flow patterns for the iShares Core S&P Small Cap ETF, the Vanguard Dividend Appreciation ETF and the Schwab U.S. Dividend Equity ETF.

The ebbs and flows of cash offer a window into the behind-the-scenes mechanics of ETFs. Called "kickers" or "friendlies," these sizable trades are typically arranged between portfolio managers and some of the largest market makers on the street, often big banks that are comfortable tying up billions of dollars over several days.

The trades serve a twofold purpose. First, portfolio managers use them to realign the ETF's holdings with any changes in the underlying index. Second, they help the fund shed shares that might otherwise trigger a taxable gain.

But the trades come at a cost, said Elisabeth Kashner, head of ETF research and analytics at FactSet. Traders get ahead of coming index changes, and can drive up the price of stocks being added to the index and depress prices of the deletions, she said. To match the closing price on index-rebalancing day, funds are forced to buy high and sell low. The risk is low for big, heavily traded stocks but can be significant in smaller, thinly traded markets. Ms. Kashner estimated that the rebalance trade of one ETF last year cost the fund's investors 0.06%.

"Rebalances are not free," Ms. Kashner said. The cost is largely invisible to investors, since the fund matched its index, she said. Moreover, it is usually well worth it. "To the average investor in a taxable account, the cost that they give up in slippage is a pittance in comparison to what they're saving in tax liability."

---

Akane Otani contributed to this article.

WSJ Pro
VC Daily: More Startups Face Limits on Taking Foreign Money | Tiger's Fixel Exits

1,328 words
15 March 2019
12:17
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. A coming wave of tech IPOs means a lot to the early investors and employees of the companies going public.

It's also shaping up as a huge potential source of revenue for the city of San Francisco, home to the likes of Lyft Inc., Uber Technologies Inc. and Airbnb Inc.

City officials are scrambling to size up what kind impact the city should expect when there's a sudden minting of thousands of new millionaires. But that could be just the beginning. Collecting a tax on people's stock options is a popular idea among some San Franciscans who want to fund more affordable housing in a city famous for its sky-high prices.

At least one local politician, city Supervisor Gordon Mar, is investigating the possibility. Maybe San Francisco will get a cut of some of the year's biggest deals.

And now on to the news...

In the News

Foreigners' funds reconsidered: Startups are used to having ready access to foreign capital. Now they are learning to examine fundraising sources more closely, as Chinese investments come under increased scrutiny in the U.S.

Concern that Chinese investment in U.S. companies could pose a national-security threat prompted lawmakers last year to boost the government's authority to block transactions involving foreign firms.

Among the newly identified technologies that could be under scrutiny are biotech, artificial intelligence, machine learning and data analytics.

Startups in certain cases must now declare their intention to fundraise from foreign investors 45 days before the deal closes or face fines of up to the value of the investment.

So far, Chinese investment in U.S. startups has held up, according to a new report from research firm Rhodium Group. But that could change. The rules are yet to be fully implemented. And some entrepreneurs say they would think twice before fundraising from Chinese firms.

Says Ray Rothrock, chief executive of cybersecurity startup RedSeal Inc.: "I'd be smart about it. It doesn't necessarily stop people from doing things, but it is part of the conversation for how you capitalize [a] new business." WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley has the story.

Change at Tiger Global: A top venture and private-equity player is losing its leader. Lee Fixel is leaving Tiger Global Management LLC after a 13-year run.

Mr. Fixel, head of the group's private-equity and growth investing, is staying until June 30, according to the New York-based firm.

He will be replaced by Scott Shleifer, who co-founded the firm's private-equity arm.

In recent years, Tiger Global's investments include Postmates Inc., Stripe Inc. and Coinbase Inc. The firm closed a $3.75 billion investment fund last fall.

Delivery Wars, the recap: This week the WSJ's "Delivery Wars" series examined the promise and the problems surrounding the push to automate and otherwise digitize deliveries from restaurants and grocers. In case you missed it, here is each installment:

Consumers Love Food Delivery. Restaurants and Grocers Hate It.

Tonight's Dinner? In a Cooler-Sized Robot That Knows Where You Live

Why Food-Delivery Companies Want to Create Superusers

As Food-Delivery Firms Court Smaller Markets, New Hurdles Emerge

Starbucks Fights Hot Startup on Delivery in China

Walmart's Food-Delivery Challenges: Patchwork of Drivers, Tolls, Crowded Aisles

Exits

Stryker Corp. acquired OrthoSpace Ltd. in an all-cash transaction worth up to $220 million including milestone payments. Founded in 2009, OrthoSpace develops a device for the treatment of rotator cuff injury. Investors in the Israel-based company included HealthpointCapital, InnovaHealth Partners and TriVentures.

Industry News

Leonardo DiCaprio, the Oscar-winning actor and environmental activist, is taking on a different kind of supporting role as an adviser to growth-equity firm Princeville Capital. The 44-year old actor, who is also known for his environmental advocacy work, will become a senior adviser to Princeville Capital's new unit that targets investments in technology companies that seek to address climate change. Princeville is seeking $150 million for a new climate technology fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission, although the fund is expected to reach $250 million or more, according to people familiar with the offering.

Private-equity firm TPG ousted William McGlashan Jr., founder of its growth investment unit and head of the firm's impact investment team, on Thursday after he was charged in this week's wide-ranging criminal college admissions scandal. TPG said Mr. McGlashan's firing is effective immediately.

New Money

Surrozen Inc., a South San Francisco, Calif.-based regenerative medicine startup, completed a $50 million Series B round from investors including Horizons Ventures, Hartford HealthCare Endowment, NS Investment and Column Group. Banking & Finance: Leveraged Oil ETFs Are Lucrative but Risky

By Asjylyn Loder 
619 words
14 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B10
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Oil funds that use leverage to juice returns have been among the top-performing exchange-traded funds of 2019, gaining almost 90%.

Yet investors have pulled $325 million from the two biggest exchange-traded products that seek to double or triple the daily return of U.S. crude futures contracts, a sign there is more than meets the eye in this obscure and risky market.

Part of the reason is that profiting from leveraged exchange-traded products is harder than it looks.

The products are pitched as tools for daily trading and can be disastrous for buy-and-hold investors who don't understand the mechanics. Correctly predicting oil prices will rise is just one part of the equation.

So far this year, crude has been a juggernaut. U.S. futures have gained more than 28% as fears of a global glut diminished. This week, oil got another boost as supplies from Venezuela tightened and Saudi Arabia, the de facto head of the Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries, signaled it would continue curbing oil production and exports. After a government report Wednesday showed a decline in U.S. stockpiles, oil futures rose to a four-month high, closing at $58.26 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange.

Given oil's rise, doubling or tripling down on oil looks like a smart wager. The VelocityShares 3X Long Crude Oil exchange-traded note, which seeks to triple the daily return of U.S. crude futures, is up 89% this year, and the United States 3X Oil Fund has gained 88%, according to FactSet. The ProShares Ultra Bloomberg Crude Oil ETF, which aims for double the daily performance, has gained 54%.

Triple-leveraged bullish funds typically do well in a steadily rising environment, but they amplify losses when the market reverses.

Adding to the confusion, the funds need to rebalance daily, which can erode returns even when oil prices have risen, especially in choppy markets.

Unexpected losses can be a rude awakening for investors who don't understand the mechanics.

"To do well, you need to get two calls right," said Elisabeth Kashner, head of ETF research and analytics at FactSet.

"The first is the overall market direction, and that's the call that's obvious to most investors. The not obvious piece is that you also have to get the path the price takes right, the consistency of the move," she said.

Say oil prices gain 5% in a single day. A $100 investment in a triple-leveraged oil ETF would be worth about $115 at the end of the day. The fund then buys more oil futures to reset its leveraged exposure for the next day, which means the investor now has $115 riding on oil prices. If oil drops 5% the following day, the investor loses $17.25, ending up with $97.75 -- less than the initial wager made two days before.

"If you get a whipsaw pattern, even if it's moving overall in the direction that you pick, you could find yourself unpleasantly surprised because of all the buying high and selling low you did along the way," Ms. Kashner said.

Over time, the divergence can be significant. In the past year, for example, triple-leveraged products have lost almost 40%, while oil prices are down just 7%, according to FactSet.

Those unpleasant surprises are part of the reason some brokerage platforms like Charles Schwab Corp. and Vanguard Group impose guardrails on investors trying to buy leveraged products. The SEC warned brokers in 2012 that leveraged ETFs are potentially "unsuitable for long-term investors."

Worldwide AI Spending to Hit $35.8 Billion in 2019; International Data Corp. also projects spending will more than double to $79.2 billion by 2022

By John McCormick 
641 words
13 March 2019
22:31
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Global spending on artificial intelligence is expected to hit $35.8 billion this year, up 44% over last year, with retail and banking leading the way, according to International Data Corp.

The AI market shows no sign of slowing down, with AI spending projected to more than double to $79.2 billion by 2022, a compound annual growth rate of 38%, IDC said. The company increased its AI spending projection from September, the last time it looked at the market, when it anticipated AI outlays to hit $77.6 billion by 2022. The U.S. will be responsible for nearly two-thirds of all AI spending this year.

IDC's AI spending forecast is based on surveys of IT executives, analysts' research and other proprietary and secondary information sources.

The reason the market is growing so quickly now is that the infrastructure, the big data, and the analytics to build AI applications has finally come together, said Marianne Daquila, research manager of customer insight and analysis at IDC. "And that's only going to continue to get better over time," she said.

Breaking it down by industry sector, retailers this year will spend $5.9 billion on automated customer service agents, expert shopping advisers and product recommendations, and other artificial intelligence applications. Banks will invest $5.6 billion in automated threat intelligence and prevention systems, fraud analysis and investigation systems, and other AI systems, according to IDC. Other big AI budgeters include discrete manufacturers, health-care providers and process manufacturers.

Indeed, retailers are embracing AI, according to survey of more than 300 industry executives that was done by research firm Oxford Economics Ltd. for consumer financial services company Synchrony Financial. Although cautious about all the hype surrounding the technology, nearly three-fourths of the respondents said AI will be a competitive necessity in the next five years.

"Everybody says it going to be a competitive necessity But it's a very grounded response:  We know what it does. We get what it doesn't do. We're realistic about the expectations,'" said Edward Cone, Oxford Economics' technology practice lead, summing up the survey response.

Among the specific technology retailers are deploying are machine learning, chatbots and virtual assistants, according to the Oxford Economics and Synchrony survey. More than half, 55%, said they realized a substantial increase in revenue growth from the AI implementations.

Retailers see the primary benefits from AI as more relevant, customized marketing; an improved in-store experience; an improved online experience; better merchandise selection; improved products and service, according to the findings from Oxford Economics and Synchrony.

"Retail is a really great case of having automated customer service agents, expert shopping advisers and product recommendations, and merchandising for omnichannel operations," said IDC's Ms. Daquila. "I mean, it's very, very easy to see how retail benefits from AI."

Across all sectors, the AI projects that will receive the most funding this year, according to IDC, will be automated customer service agents, at $4.5 billion world-wide; sales process recommendation and automation, at $2.7 billion; and automated threat intelligence and prevention systems, at $2.7 billion. Other popular applications include automated preventive maintenance, diagnosis and treatment systems, fraud analysis and investigation, intelligent process automation, and program advisers and recommendation systems.

This year, $13.5 billion will be spent on AI applications and software platforms and $12.7 billion will be invested in hardware, with servers taking up a big portion of that spending, as companies build the infrastructure needed to support their AI systems. Spending on AI-related services is projected to match hardware spending, according to IDC.

Song Jung-a in Seoul

04:30

English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Regulatory changes prompt spending spree on commercial real estate

When 6,000 staff of Goldman Sachs move into their new London office in the city s financial district this summer, many may be unaware that the 826,000 square foot Plumtree Court building is owned by a South Korean pension fund.

South Korea s state-run National Pension Service (NPS) bought the new, unfinished European headquarters of the US bank for 1.2bn in August in the largest deal for a London office building last year. Goldman Sachs has taken a 25-year lease on the 10-storey building.

The big-ticket purchase is part of surging interest in European buildings by South Korean investors, who are attracted by cheap financing, high yields and currency hedging premiums, according to property agents.

They have become leading buyers of prime buildings in Europe after domestic regulatory changes in 2015 made it easier for South Korean institutions to invest abroad.

Specifically Seoul revised legislation to allow securities companies with at least Won4tn ($3.6bn) of capital to deploy new financing channels.

The spending spree has been led by state-run pension fund NPS and sovereign wealth fund Korea Investment Corp (KIC), followed by securities companies and asset managers.

Despite Brexit concerns, London was this sort of investor s favourite property investment target last year, followed by other European gateway cities such as Frankfurt, Brussels and Paris, according to data from Real Capital Analytics.

Last year set a record for South Korean investment in Europe s commercial property market, with volumes rising nearly sixfold over the past five years to  7.3bn in 2018, according to property group Cushman & Wakefield. The UK attracted more than 40 per cent of South Korea s investment in Europe s commercial property, with London receiving the bulk.

 The wave of Korean capital that flowed into the European real estate sector over 2018 was remarkable,  says Jonghan Kim, Head of Korea at Cushman s EMEA capital markets team.

South Korean groups invested 2.3bn in London property last year, accounting for 12 per cent of total property investment volumes in the UK capital, lagging only Hong Kong s 2.5bn invested in London property in 2018.

 The preference for London is borne out of the city s unrivalled liquidity and attractive pricing relative to compressed yields in key European cities such as Frankfurt and Paris,  says Jaewon Yoon, head of outbound investment at Savills Korea.

Although Chinese investors, especially from Hong Kong, have been the prolific overseas investors in Europe over the past few years, Beijing s clampdown on outbound capital has given South Koreans a chance to snap up prime buildings in London.

Other South Korean investments in Europe include the  670m purchase of Frankfurt s Trianon Tower by Hana Financial Investments and IGIS Asset Management. In London, 125 Shaftesbury Avenue was bought for $348m by a consortium led by KB Securities, and Mirae Asset Global Investments purchased 20 Old Bailey from Blackstone for 340m.

 Korean investors 	. 	. 	. 	look for assets with a stable income and tenants with a good investment grade,  says Mr Kim.

 We plan to gradually increase our exposure to overseas properties but will be more careful and selective about investing in the UK until Brexit worries are resolved,  says Heenam Choi, chief executive of KIC.

Axel Springer, rating agencies and buyout groups also said to be eyeing Acuris

Traditional media companies, including Rupert Murdoch s news business, and the world s largest rating agencies are set to enter a bidding war to acquire Acuris, the owner of Mergermarket, as an auction gets officially under way this week, people involved in the process said.

All companies declined to comment.

Acuris is considered an attractive asset because its subscription-based model, which can draw a steady stream of fees from its 190,000 subscribers, leaves it with a sticky customer base. The business has a renewal rate of 95 per cent, according to a person with direct knowledge of its finances.

BC Partners bought the business from Pearson   the former owner of the Financial Times   for 382m. It has since more than doubled in value after Singapore s GIC bought a 30 per cent stake valuing the business at about 1bn.

Since being acquired by BC Partners, Acuris has seen double-digit growth and has annual earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortisation of 75m, a person with direct knowledge of its performance said.

The private equity group has also cut or sold lossmaking units and introduced new products to expand the business since it bought Acuris in 2013.

If a sale goes through, the business will be worth more than the 844m that Nikkei, Japan s largest newspaper company, paid Pearson for the Financial Times four years ago.

Interest in Acuris comes at a time when private equity groups  competition for assets has intensified as they raise record funds and come under pressure to put capital to work.

A potential sale follows a string of acquisitions of subscription-based data companies.

Last year, US buyout group Warburg Pincus bought a majority stake in Reorg Research, a bankruptcy analysis and deal intelligence start-up, in a $375m to $400m deal.

In 2017 Moody s bought Bureau van Dijk, a Dutch data analytics group, for  3bn.

Chipmaker Nvidia beats Intel to buy rival Mellanox to buy rival

ERIC PLATT;JAMES FONTANELLA-KHAN; MEHUL SRIVASTAVA ; TIM BRADSHAW 
512 words
12 March 2019
Financial Times
FTFT

English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Technology

Nvidia yesterday agreed to buy rival Mellanox Technologies for $6.9bn, including debt, in the biggest deal ever for the US semiconductor company as it seeks to bolster its business in data centres.

The all-cash deal would help Nvidia shift its focus away from the gaming industry, which has been hit by slowing economic growth in China and the waning cryptocurrency fervour. Nvidia said it would pay $125 a share for Mellanox, a 14 per cent premium to the group's closing share price on March 8. The chipmaker beat Intel in a competitive race to secure Mellanox, which is based in both the US and Israel, said one person involved in the bidding process.

Intel is one of the biggest foreign investors in Israel. Just two months ago, the chipmaker announced plans for an $11bn production and research facility in the country. The company bought Mobileye, a Jerusalem-based maker of sensors and cameras for computerassisted and autonomous driving, for $15.3bn in 2017.

Mellanox, which reported sales of $1.1bn last year, makes cables and switches that transfer data between servers, storage systems and infrastructure equipment.

The technology will complement Nvidia's graphics processors, which despite their origins in visual effects and computer games, are increasingly used in machine learning systems.

"The emergence of [artificial intelligence] and data science, as well as billions of simultaneous computer users, is fuelling skyrocketing demand on the world's data centres," said Jensen Huang, the founder and chief executive of Nvidia.

"This type of workload is simply too big to fit on any one computer ... Thousands of computers have to be networked together."

The two companies said the combined entity would serve every big provider of cloud computing services and power more than half the world's fastest supercomputers.

Nvidia and Mellanox have collaborated for several years, but acquisition talks began only recently, Mr Huang said on a conference call yesterday.

"We would like to accelerate growth into data centres by being able to architect across both the compute as well as the network fabric," he said, adding that the deal was part of a broader strategy to change Nvidia "from a chip and system-level company to a data centrescale company".

The deal is expected to complete this year and will be funded by cash on Nvidia's balance sheet. It will require regulatory approval in the US, China and other jurisdictions, Nvidia said.

Nvidia generated roughly a quarter of its $11.7bn of revenues from its data centre business last year, which was dwarfed by its sales to gamers who want to supercharge their PCs. The company faced a turbulent end to 2018 as its gaming division's sales slid, driven in large part by a fall in sales of graphics cards to cryptocurrency miners.

Nvidia expects to return to growth in its core gaming business in the quarter ending in July, but warned of limited visibility among cloud computing clients.

Bot training by bank customers

Laura Noonan 
1,585 words
12 March 2019
15:16
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Your essential weekly fintech briefing

Who would you rather talk to, a virtual banking assistant whose life goal is  helping people overcome drama with style and a pout , a  born charmer  chatbot whose special skill is  modelling a zillion looks in one paparazzi flash  or a  fast n  curious  AI who wants to  bridge the gap between humans and bots  and  can type faster than the speed of light ?

Liv, the two-year-old  digital lifestyle bank  spun out of Emirates NBD, put all these choices and more to their customers in a bid to create something that has eluded virtually every other bank   a chatbot their customers actually want to deal with, instead of one that s viewed as a job-stealing menace to be endured in the name of progress.

Close to 60,000 people watched aFacebook videoof Liv's management interviewing  the five potential chatbots. Viewers were then encouraged to vote, via the comment stream, for who should be hired (an offer thataround 600 of them took up, lured in part by the chance to win $140).



Jayesh Patel,head of Liv, admits that the game was rigged. Olivia, the empathetic but fun bot with the lightning-fast typing skills, was the one who'd been developed for deployment, and the others gave such obviously inappropriate answers at their  interviews  that they were unlikely to win the popular vote. Liv was never going to unleash  born charmer  Matchio Bot or  geeky cheeky  Gizmo Bot to its 210,000 customers (though Matchio was a surprising hit with some Facebook commenters).

The integrity of the voting process was secondary to the true purpose of the process of engagingLiv s customers, making them feel like they were active participants in their bank s decision to transfer the handling of their day-to-day queries from real-life humans to a machine.

Some 3,000 of those Liv customers signed up to  mentor  Olivia by engaging with her and helping her to learn the skills she'd need to serve them when she finally began work this week. (The customers also get the chance to win prizes including an iPad for their participation).

Olivia wasn t starting from scratch. She s built on Kai, the artificial intelligence platform built by cutting-edge US AI firm Kasisto, which is already in use across eight global financial institutions including Singapore's DBS, Standard Chartered, JPMorgan Chase, TD Bank and Wells Fargo.

Zor Gorelov, head of Kasisto, said this project was different because Liv doesn t see itself as a bank in the traditional sense.  The idea is lifestyle assistant first, banking assistant second, that s the vision that the bank has and that s what we helped them execute on.  Or, as Mr Patel puts it,the key is  positioning Olivia as a buddy that our customers rely on, like and trust .

For Mr Gorelov and his team, that meant teaching Kai about spending habits, saving habits and goal setting, and developing a visual and conversational way of presenting the information.

The build-up for Olivia was also unique.  What they ve done is 	. 	. 	. 	groundbreaking in many ways,  said Mr Gorelov, who expects to roll Kai out across another eight banks in the next year.  We re watching the space very carefully. 

Mr Patel is refreshingly upfront about the fact that using Olivia means fewer human jobs. The man versus machinetension is an easier one at Liv, which has a headcount of just 63 and is acquiring new customers faster than any other bank in the UAE, than it is at massive banks that employ tens of thousands in their call centres and are growing slowly.  We will not hire as many new agents,  said Mr Patel.  We still have a lot of growth. 

The initial feedback from users testing Olivia has been so positive that Mr Patel sees her as a  prime contender  to provide similar servicesat Liv's parent bank, Emirates NBD, though likely under another name. Whether they'll go for an X-Factor style contest to find that name is anybody's guess.

One to watch: Raisin

Raisin, the German group that raised $114m in funding from PayPal last month to become one of the best-financed fintechs in Europe, announced on Thursday its purchase of its longtime German service bank, MHB.

A retail banking marketplace, Raisinlets customers place their money in international banks to take advantage of global interest and currency rates,writes Madison Darbyshire in London. It calls itself a  schengen experience  for banking, and has rapidly ballooned to 165,000 customers across nearly 70 European banks. The tie-up with MHB, Raisin hopes, will accelerate plans for expansion.

When Raisin was founded, interest rates were poised to rise from their record low levels, but the ECB has pushed back, forcing Raisin to look for other ways to drive growth.

The young company wants to end its dependency on service providers, acceleratingthe implementation of its technology and speeding up processing.  It s good to have full influence on the payment and customer due diligence side,  Raisin co-founder Tamaz Georgadze said.

Raisindidn't disclose what it paid for MHB (your estimates welcome!). However, the bank purchase, Mr Georgadze said, allows Raisin to utilise the bank'slicences. Previously it relied on its partner banks, primarily challenger banks Starling and Keytrade, to fill the void.

 We didn t have financial institutions licence that would allow us to work across Europe,  Mr Georgadze said.  The driving factor was the increasing complexity in our business model. We needed a licence to operate other than [a] brokerage licence. 

MHB is not a bank the Raisin customers place assets with; Mr Georgadze brushes off questions about competition among the banks on its marketplace.

On the MHB s price tag, Mr Georgadze joked that MHB thinks they overpaid, and Raisin thinks they underpaid.  We are a happy buyer,  he said.

Number of the week:65 per cent

Proportion of British peer-to-peer lender Lendy s outstanding loans that are distressed or in default.

Further fintech fascination

Crypto chronicles:#fintechFT s friends over at Alphaville are arch crypto-sceptics, and we detect a little schadenfreudein their reportthat blockchain firm Setl has appointed administrators. Along with developing blockchain software, Setl has been building a securities depository and a fund record keeping platform. Companies involved in market infrastructure need to hold regulatory capital, however. When that became hard to fund, the company decided to sell the latter two businesses. That has proven difficult to do, and the two units have been handed over to administrators, who'll sell them on behalf of creditors. Setl was founded by Peter Randall, who also founded the Chi-X alternative equity exchanges; several big shots of traditional finance sit on its board.

Regulators' advance:On Thursday the UK s Financial Conduct Authoritypublished researcharguing that many customers don't know what they're buying when they purchase cryptocurrencies. Those interviewed were often looking for a way to  get rich quick , citing external influences such as social media, and often performed little to no research before purchase. Shocking absolutely no one, the most likely to know what a crypto asset was, of UK residents, were men aged 20 to 44. The FCA is looking to address the potential harm of cryptoassets on a public that understand little about them.

Trend watch:Islamic finance keeps on keeping on. On Thursday TheCityUK and Borsa, the Turkish stock exchange, released a joint report examining the market s potential. The World Bank estimates that 6 per cent of the world s population opt out of the financial system for religious reasons, a number that is dramatically higher in Islamic countries   as high as 19 per cent in Turkey. Yet two-thirds of the unbanked have smartphones. This is an opportunity for fintechs offering Shariah-compliant products   those that, among other things, charge no interest, make no investments in gambling or alcohol, and relyon equity rather than debt. The $2tn market has been growing 10 per cent a yearor more, according to the study, and this is poised to continue.

Follow the money:Augmentum, the London-listed, Lord-Rothschild-backed fintech investment fund, had its investor day this week. The company said it has invested 71m and has committed another 15m to companies in its portfolio   meaning effectively all the proceeds of the IPO a year ago have been put to work. Zopa (personal lending and investing)BullionVault (online precious metal investing) and iwoca (small business credit) make up about half the portfolio. The stock market seems to like the progress: the (very illiquid) shares have risen 15 per cent since November.

South Korean cash finds a home in European prime property

Song Jung-a in Seoul 
716 words
12 March 2019
04:30
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Regulatory changes prompt spending spree on commercial real estate

When 6,000 staff of Goldman Sachs move into their new London office in the city s financial district this summer, many may be unaware that the 826,000 square foot Plumtree Court building is owned by a South Korean pension fund.

South Korea s state-run National Pension Service (NPS) bought the new, unfinished European headquarters of the US bank for 1.2bn in August in the largest deal for a London office building last year. Goldman Sachs has taken a 25-year lease on the 10-storey building.

The big-ticket purchase is part of surging interest in European buildings by South Korean investors, who are attracted by cheap financing, high yields and currency hedging premiums, according to property agents.

They have become leading buyers of prime buildings in Europe after domestic regulatory changes in 2015 made it easier for South Korean institutions to invest abroad.

Specifically Seoul revised legislation to allow securities companies with at least Won4tn ($3.6bn) of capital to deploy new financing channels.

The spending spree has been led by state-run pension fund NPS and sovereign wealth fund Korea Investment Corp (KIC), followed by securities companies and asset managers.

Despite Brexit concerns, London was this sort of investor s favourite property investment target last year, followed by other European gateway cities such as Frankfurt, Brussels and Paris, according to data from Real Capital Analytics.

Last year set a record for South Korean investment in Europe s commercial property market, with volumes rising nearly sixfold over the past five years to  7.3bn in 2018, according to property group Cushman & Wakefield. The UK attracted more than 40 per cent of South Korea s investment in Europe s commercial property, with London receiving the bulk.

 The wave of Korean capital that flowed into the European real estate sector over 2018 was remarkable,  says Jonghan Kim, Head of Korea at Cushman s EMEA capital markets team.

South Korean groups invested 2.3bn in London property last year, accounting for 12 per cent of total property investment volumes in the UK capital, lagging only Hong Kong s 2.5bn invested in London property in 2018.

 The preference for London is borne out of the city s unrivalled liquidity and attractive pricing relative to compressed yields in key European cities such as Frankfurt and Paris,  says Jaewon Yoon, head of outbound investment at Savills Korea.

Although Chinese investors, especially from Hong Kong, have been the prolific overseas investors in Europe over the past few years, Beijing s clampdown on outbound capital has given South Koreans a chance to snap up prime buildings in London.

Other South Korean investments in Europe include the  670m purchase of Frankfurt s Trianon Tower by Hana Financial Investments and IGIS Asset Management. In London, 125 Shaftesbury Avenue was bought for $348m by a consortium led by KB Securities, and Mirae Asset Global Investments purchased 20 Old Bailey from Blackstone for 340m.

 Korean investors 	. 	. 	. 	look for assets with a stable income and tenants with a good investment grade,  says Mr Kim.

Last year, South Koreans bought $8.4bn in income-producing assets and development sites abroad, with three-quarters of this outbound capital going to Europe, up from 59 per cent in 2017, according to Real Capital Analytics.

 [Korea s] domestic market has been tight, characterised by rising prices, sharp yield compression and a competitive business environment,  says Petra Blazkova, senior director of analytics for Asia Pacific at Real Capital Analytics.

Market watchers expect South Korean capital to continue flowing into Europe, with Paris likely to be their most popular destination this year.

 We plan to gradually increase our exposure to overseas properties but will be more careful and selective about investing in the UK until Brexit worries are resolved,  says Heenam Choi, chief executive of KIC.

Goldman Sachs to Open Up In-House Moneymaker to Outside Investors; Bank plans to raise outside money for its special-situations group, one of its most profitable businesses

By Liz Hoffman 
803 words
12 March 2019
19:43
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A Goldman Sachs Group Inc. profit machine that has invested the bank's own money in Asian property, African startups and troubled U.S. retailers, among other ventures, is opening up to outside investors.

Goldman plans to raise outside money for its special-situations group, according to people familiar with the matter. Also under discussion is a broader reorganization of the firm's various private investing activities into a new unit that would seek to raise new funds across a variety of strategies, the people said.

The special-situations group has been run since 2013 by Julian Salisbury, a detail-oriented Brit who joined the firm's management committee two years ago. It has grown from about $20 billion on the eve of the crisis to about $30 billion today, people familiar with the matter said.

In the early 2000s it pioneered the sort of go-anywhere investing that has caught on at private-equity firms such as TPG and Blackstone Group LP, and it remains one of the most profitable businesses at Goldman.

It will likely be combined with Goldman's merchant-banking group, the historical home of the bank's private-equity investing.

"Based on our track record, there is an opportunity to raise additional third-party funds across equity, credit and real estate," Goldman Chief Executive David Solomon told analysts earlier this year. "We have a world-class alternative investing franchise, which has generated strong returns over three decades [and] presents us with extraordinary opportunities to partner with clients to invest their capital alongside our own."

In a memo to employees last week entitled "Our Forward Strategy," Mr. Solomon said growing the bank's alternative asset-management business is a priority.

Related

* "https://www.wsj.com/articles/goldman-jpmorgan-eased-up-on-early-intern-recruiting-others-havent-11552041000?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=18"

* "https://www.wsj.com/articles/goldman-rolls-out-new-etfs-focused-on-artificial-intelligence-11551978432?mod=searchresults&page=2&pos=2"

The discussions show how much has changed on Wall Street since the financial crisis. In Goldman's last big push into private equity, in the 2000s, it put billions of dollars of its own money into megabuyouts. Now it seeks raise money from outside investors like pension funds and clip steady fees for managing it. Shareholders today value steady, low-risk businesses like money management, setting off an asset-gathering race across Wall Street.

The reorganization also shows Mr. Solomon busting up silos in an effort to modernize and streamline the firm. Under a "One Goldman" banner, he has launched a firmwide effort to better cover top clients.

Scattered across Goldman are at least a half-dozen units tasked with making investments, built over decades of ad hoc planning and guarded jealously by their owners. Their results are comingled in an "investing and lending" reporting segment that shareholders tend to dismiss as opaque and unpredictable.

Merging them will create a unit resembling a smaller version of KKR & Co. or Blackstone that executives hope will be better understood and more richly valued by shareholders. The move may eventually change how Goldman publicly reports its earnings, some of the people said.

The firm weighed such a combination once before, in 2008 as the crisis deepened, but the endeavor fell apart due to personality clashes, a person familiar with the matter said.

Most banks spun off their private-equity arms after the 2010 passage of the Dodd-Frank financial law, which banned banks from investing their own money into buyout funds and slapped heavy capital charges on illiquid portfolios. Goldman held on more tightly, betting that it could navigate the new rules.

It started doing deals straight off its balance sheet, sidestepping the ban on funds. Successful investments of that vintage "https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-goldman-sachs-made-more-than-1-billion-with-your-credit-score-1491742835"credit bureau TransUnion and financial-data firm Ipreo. In 2017 Goldman raised its first buyout fund since the crisis, a $7 billion pool that includes only a small amount of the bank's own money.

The special-situations group has wide leeway to invest wherever it sees an opportunity. In the late 1990s, it made a killing in the wake of the Asian financial crisis. These days it is more of a middle-market lender, serving midsize companies that have trouble borrowing elsewhere. It has invested in a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/with-c-o-d-and-goat-promotions-jumia-aims-to-be-africas-alibaba-1527073200", bought troubled commercial real-estate loans in New Zealand and backed Mexico's fastest-growing fintech lender.

It commonly lends to distressed companies, exacting terms that boost its profits and limit its potential losses. A recent loan to a struggling California turbine maker carries a 13% interest rate and requires the company to set aside $12 million in cash as collateral 40% of the value of the loan.

Nvidia to Buy Networking Firm --- With Mellanox deal, chip maker seeks to bolster its position in cloud computing

By Asa Fitch 
656 words
12 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Nvidia Corp. agreed to acquire networking firm Mellanox Technologies Ltd. for about $6.9 billion, its largest deal by far as it bolsters its bet on cloud computing after a stinging sales drop in its core graphics-chips business late last year.

The combination, announced Monday, marks the latest foray by Nvidia beyond chips known as graphics processing units. While Nvidia has pushed into artificial-intelligence applications and grown its data-center business in recent years, much of its growth has been rooted in finding new uses for its chips rather than venturing into entirely new areas.

With Mellanox, Nvidia is buying a maker of Ethernet switches and adapters that connect computers to each other, wiring together networks where users can rapidly exchange information. The company is a major supplier of equipment that conforms to the so-called InfiniBand networking standard widely used in supercomputers.

Nvidia's acquisitions outside the graphics-chip arena have been few, and none of its deals are in the same financial orbit as Mellanox's. It paid $367 million in 2011 for the modem-technology company Icera, its largest disclosed acquisition before the Mellanox deal, a spokesman said. Nvidia wound down that business in 2015.

"We rarely do deals, and the reason is on balance we really prefer to build" new businesses, Nvidia Chief Executive Jensen Huang said in an interview.

Nvidia, based in Santa Clara, Calif., will pay $125 a share in cash for Mellanox, a 14% premium to the company's closing price Friday of $109.38.

Shares of Nvidia, which have been battered in recent months, rose 7% to $161.14 on Monday. Mellanox shares rose 7.8% to $117.89.

The transaction, expected to close by the end of 2019, would immediately add to Nvidia's gross margin and bottom line once completed, the company said. On a call with analysts, finance chief Colette Kress said Nvidia doesn't expect to change its plans to return $2.3 billion to shareholders in its current fiscal year on top of what it already has done.

Nvidia's chips, long designed to enable advanced graphics for videogames, have proven well-suited to difficult computing tasks, and are widely used in high-performance computing and cloud infrastructure by companies such as Amazon.com Inc. and Alphabet Inc.'s Google.

The rise of cryptocurrency mining and artificial-intelligence applications that also made heavy use of Nvidia's chips propelled the company's sales in recent years and sent its stock price soaring.

But Nvidia suffered a reversal last year, in large part because cryptocurrency prices fell sharply and mining became unprofitable.

Mr. Huang had called Nvidia's most recent quarter "a real punch in the gut" after revising quarterly revenue guidance downward by $500 million due to slow sales of its latest graphics chips for videogames and the hangover from the cryptocurrency bust. The company's shares fell from almost $290 in October to around $151 at Friday's close.

The Mellanox acquisition likely will add modestly to Nvidia's profit and could be a good strategic fit, according to Bernstein Research analyst Stacy Rasgon, though he said some investors might scratch their heads about the timing of the deal.

Mr. Huang said the deal meshed with Nvidia's view that the future is about constructing a fast, interconnected fabric of computing power in data centers. "We have some short-term challenges that are largely behind us," he said. "Our investment thesis -- our company's thesis -- remains intact."

Mellanox, which has operations in the U.S. and Israel, has been up for sale since last year, according to a person familiar with the matter. Its market capitalization was around $5.9 billion as of Friday's close.

---

Kimberly Chin contributed to this article.
