Is Your ETF Conflicted? Advisory firms must make potential conflicts of interest clear in disclosures

By Asjylyn Loder 
419 words
11 March 2019
11:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

How can you tell if your exchange-traded fund is subject to potential conflicts?

Investors can find disclosures in brochures that advisory firms file with the SEC, known as a Form ADV Part 2. There are several JPMorgan business entities that file a Form ADV Part 2. The one for JPMorgan Private Investments, one of the business units that is a top owner of its ETFs, is 47 pages long. It reads, in part: "Conflicts of interest will arise whenever JPMorgan has an actual or perceived economic or other incentive in its management of clients' portfolios to act in a way that benefits JPMorgan."

It goes on to say, "It is important to note that JPMorgan receives more overall fees when internally managed strategies are included," and that JPMorgan "expects the proportion of JPMorgan managed strategies will be high (in fact, up to 100%)" in some strategies.

Failing to make such conflicts clear has gotten JPMorgan in trouble before. In 2015, the firm agreed to pay $307 million to settle charges that the bank improperly steered clients into its own mutual funds and hedge funds without disclosing conflicts of interest.

Northern Trust's Form ADV Part 2 says "it is expected that the proportion of Northern Trust investment products held in client accounts may be high (in fact, up to 100%)," and that "Northern Trust will receive more overall compensation when Northern Trust managed products are used."

Affiliates aren't the only potential conflict of interest. Any large financial company has business ties that present conflicts ranging from who underwrites their bonds to where they route their trades. The ETF industry is no different. For example, ETF providers including BlackRock Inc., State Street Corp. and WisdomTree Investments Inc. pay to trade commission-free on brokerage platforms like Fidelity Investments, Charles Schwab Corp. and TD Ameritrade, giving them a substantial advantage over competing providers that can't or won't pay for prime shelf space.

Vanguard Group is an exception. The Malvern, Pa., investment firm has a longstanding policy against paying for distribution. And as of last year, ETFs from every provider, including its competitors, trade commission-free on Vanguard's online brokerage.

Good day. And welcome to the food-delivery wars.

Companies large and small are experimenting with autonomous delivery to reduce the high costs and headaches of door-to-door service. But the robots aren't riding to the rescue anytime soon.

Winning over public sentiment at a time of growing hostility about the power and reach of technology in everyday life is also creating a challenge for the startups to make their robots which until now have largely been confined inside warehouses, factories and ports appear non-threatening, say venture capitalists examining the sector.

Roof's Take: Silicon Valley's IPO Parade Could Showcase Chasm With Wall Street

By Katie Roof

Silicon Valley and Wall Street could soon be set for another clash.

A slew of high-profile technology IPOs from the likes of Pinterest Inc., Uber Technologies Inc., Lyft Inc. and Postmates Inc. are coming up in 2019. As in past cases when household name-brand tech companies went public, this incoming freshman class could highlight how Wall Street values consumer-tech companies very differently from the way private investors bid up billion-dollar startups.

Venture capitalists tend to take a long-range view, placing bets on the belief that there could someday be a significant market opportunity. By contrast, Wall Street emphasizes how the company is performing today, in addition to its growth potential.

That chasm seems set to once again be on display as investors gauge whether the coming crop of closely watched tech IPOs will succeed or stumble out of the gate.

Lise Buyer, a former Wall Street analyst who is now a partner at the IPO advisory firm Class V Group, says institutional investors often rely on concrete metrics such as profitability because they "have an obligation to justify the prices they are paying today."

More in Roof's Take

Exits

Publicly traded Okta Inc. agreed to acquire Azuqua Inc. for $52.8 million. Azuqua was backed by investors including DFJ, Ignition Partners and Insight Venture Partners. The Seattle-based company enables users to create business processes that span multiple cloud services.

Versant Ventures seeks to raise up to $150 million for Versant Vantage I LP, according to a regulatory filing. In December, Versant said it had raised $700 million across two new biotech funds. Industry News

Consumer genomics company 23andMe Inc. unveiled a new health test to help people understand their risk of developing Type 2 diabetes based on specific genetic variants in addition to lifestyle and family history of the disease. The test is the first from 23andMe to be developed entirely with its own database, and is part of the $199 health and ancestry report that can test for 10 other diseases where genetics factor into risk.

Sen. Elizabeth Warren, the Massachusetts Democrat running for president, called for regulating dominant tech platforms as utilities and prohibiting them from both operating the platforms and owning and operating related businesses that run on those platforms. The senator also said she would appoint regulators who would unwind "illegal and anticompetitive tech mergers." Read more.

U.S. companies last month hired information-technology workers at the sharpest pace in more than four years, bucking a slowdown in job growth for positions outside of technology, according to CompTIA. Read more.

Hong Kong's stock exchange revamped its listing rules last April, allowing fledgling biotechnology companies to list before they turn a profit. It has hosted six such initial public offerings since. Read more.

Hungry for repeat customers who look beyond one-off discounts, food-delivery companies are striving to convert those who order meals or groceries as an occasional, luxury splurge into habitual users. Read more.

Credit unions and community banks are looking to startups like Payrailz LLC to tap artificial intelligence to help their customers juggle bills, budget expenses and manage their finances. Read more.

Canvas Ventures named Mike Ghaffary, a former investor at Social Capital, as a general partner. Mr. Ghaffary, who previously worked as an executive at Yelp Inc., will invest in early-stage consumer and marketplace businesses. At Social Capital, he invested in HubHaus Inc. and City Storage Systems LLC, better known as CloudKitchens. Mr. Ghaffary will be the fourth general partner for Canvas, based in Portola Valley, Calif.

Fannie, Freddie Get New Rivals for Loans

By Ben Eisen 
991 words
11 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Private investors are directly buying a small but growing share of loans that have long been the domain of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, a sign of the changing dynamics in the $11 trillion mortgage industry.

More mortgages that meet the standards for Fannie and Freddie to buy are instead flowing into the private market, where banks and other financial institutions pool them into bonds and sell them to investors without government backing. Firms that have recently issued these so-called private-label securities with a significant amount of such loans in them -- some up to 100% -- include Chimera Investment Corp. and Redwood Trust Inc., both real-estate investment trusts, as well as JPMorgan Chase & Co. and Flagstar Bancorp Inc.

Fannie and Freddie perform a similar middleman role in moving mortgages from lenders to investors, though in those instances the government takes on some of the risk. In the past year or so, investors have proven increasingly willing to forgo the government backstop and buy private mortgage bonds, indicating they have grown more comfortable taking risk for more potential return. The fact that more investors want to buy these securities means banks and other firms can package these deals more profitably, which in some cases lets them offer better bids than the agencies.

"I think there's a number of investors interested in playing and engaging in this market," said Kristy Fercho, president of the mortgage business at Flagstar. The company put together two deals last year made up largely of conforming loans -- or loans that meet Fannie's and Freddie's standards -- for investor properties.

Though the proportion of loans securitized by private buyers remains just a speck in the broader market, it is growing. Last year, $3.9 billion of Fannie- and Freddie-eligible loans went into private-label securities, and the year before $4 billion did, both roughly tripling 2016 levels, according to broker-dealer Amherst Pierpont Securities LLC.

Don Layton, chief executive of Freddie Mac, said in a recent interview that the agency is watching the trend, but that it doesn't affect most of the loans the agency guarantees. "It's stuff at the margins for us," Mr. Layton said.

The private market was a formidable rival to Fannie and Freddie before the financial crisis, but efforts to restart it since then have largely failed. If the latest activity is the start of a bigger shift, then Fannie's and Freddie's role in housing finance could shrink through market forces rather than congressional action.

Shrinking Fannie and Freddie is the goal of some lawmakers and policy makers, including Republicans who want the government to play a smaller role in housing. Lawmakers have tried unsuccessfully to remake the agencies since the financial crisis more than a decade ago. Some industry watchers think Mark Calabria, a sometime critic of the agencies who has been nominated to run their regulator, the Federal Housing Finance Agency, could try to encourage the private market's growth as a backdoor way to diminish the government entities, which guarantee nearly half of mortgages issued in the U.S.

"In effect, this effort to reduce the footprint is well under way," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics. "It's happening and it is consequential."

About 45% of mortgages originated in the January-to-September period last year went into Fannie and Freddie securities, according to the Urban Institute, a think tank. At its most recent peak in 2008, that proportion was 65%.

In the first nine months of last year, 1.9% of mortgage originations went into private-label securities, according to the Urban Institute. At the trough in 2009, essentially zero did. The rest are held by the lenders who made them or securitized by Ginnie Mae, the government-owned mortgage corporation that guarantees loans to many first-time buyers and veterans.

The recent moves stem from the changing calculus for lenders deciding where to sell their loans. Fannie and Freddie charge guarantee fees that erode lenders' haul from selling loans. When the fees are high enough, it can be more efficient to sell to the private market, which can be more nimble in its pricing.

When San Diego-based Guild Mortgage Co., for example, wants to sell Fannie- and Freddie-eligible loans, it collects bids from about a dozen private-market buyers and then compares those options with pricing offered by the agencies. Recently, private buyers have offered better deals on some loans. Like many lenders, Guild has found private-market pricing particularly attractive for loans for which the agencies charge higher guarantee fees, including those financing investment properties, as well as loans with high balances.

It isn't certain to stay this way. The growth in private buyers has forced them to also compete with one another, analysts say, and the share of private-label securities made of agency-eligible loans dropped at the end of last year.

Some lenders say Fannie and Freddie have flagged to them the recent market dynamics, reminding them that a lender is supposed to sell a representative sample of its loans to the agencies. They have interpreted that as a directive to not to sell their highest-quality loans to the private market while handing lower-quality ones to the government entities.

"They will fire a friendly shot across the bow" for lenders around the market, said David Battany, who oversees capital markets at Guild Mortgage.

In some instances, those in the market have noticed Fannie and Freddie adjusting their pricing, making them more competitive with the private market.

Amherst Pierpont estimated that about 10% of Fannie- and Freddie-eligible loans issued in September 2018 could be more cheaply pooled into private-label deals.

"Ultimately mortgage originators are in the business of best execution," said Chris Helwig, a managing director at Amherst Pierpont.

Cyber Daily: Information Security Chiefs Gain Stature; Citrix Attacked; U.K. Girds for Election Interference

1,052 words
11 March 2019
11:33
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Cyber Daily: Information Security Chiefs Gain Stature; Citrix Attacked; U.K. Girds for Election Interference

By Kim S. Nash

Good day. Chief information security officers are gaining stature, influencing business strategy as companies build products and services that rely on technology. Many CISOs now report to the chief executive. Being out from under the direct control of the chief information officer gives the CISO more freedom to assess the security of a company's technology, WSJ Pro's Catherine Stupp reports.

Also today: The FBI tells Citrix Systems Inc. of a cyberattack against it, the U.K. government adds cyber expertise to prepare for next election and Facebook Inc. fixes Messenger bug.

**********

Chief Information Security Officers Are Taking On More Prominent Roles, Reporting To CEOs

By Catherine Stupp

Chief information security officers report to chief executives more often than to any other senior leader, according to new research from Deloitte & Touche LLP.

In a poll of 100 CISOs, 43% said they report directly to the CEO, while 37% said they report to the chief information officer. Deloitte surveyed 500 C-level executives in January about cybersecurity issues, including hierarchy, budgets and the outsourcing of security tasks.

Cybersecurity leaders are becoming more important as companies shift to business models built on technology, experts say. That includes the Internet of Things as well as data analytics products and services. CISOs also gain stature as boards get involved in monitoring cybersecurity.

"The biggest driver is the number of public breaches and the visibility that came with those breaches and then, to some degree, panic that ensued from corporate boards," said Emily Mossburg, a principal in Deloitte's risk & financial advisory.

Credit ratings firm Equifax Inc., after revealing a breach in 2017, hired its first CISO, Jamil Farshchi, in February 2018. Mr. Farshchi reports to Equifax's CEO. Before the breach, Equifax's chief security officer reported to the company's general counsel. An Equifax spokesman didn't respond to a request for comment about its reporting structure.

CISOs need authority over budgets and to ensure members of their staff are present in crisis meetings about security issues, Ms. Mossburg said.

Out from under the CIO

Big data companies and other technology firms are more likely to have CISOs who report directly to CEOs, according to Phil Schneidermeyer, a partner at executive search firm Ward Howell International Ltd.

Most other CISOs who Mr. Schneidermeyer encounters report to their companies' CIOs. That structure can present a conflict of interest because CISOs audit the security of their company's technology systems, which CIOs manage, he said.

"How do you tell your boss his or her organization isn't keeping up?" he said.

Jadee Hanson, CISO at software company Code42 Software Inc., reports directly to the company's CEO. She briefs the CEO weekly about security risks and any investigations into cyber threats. She also meets with the company's board once per year.

"You're not fighting with a CIO in order to get risk discussions at that executive level," Ms. Hanson said.

Many CISOs are learning to interpret cyber risks for their executive counterparts, highlighting their consequences to the business, according to Ms. Hanson, who previously worked as a director of information security at Target Corp.

"As long as the CISOs can make the shift to that spot and really talk about risk in a way that resonates with the CEO and impacts the bottom line, that's when they get heard and when they find a spot at the executive table," she said.

High-profile data breaches have alerted many CEOs to the risks such incidents could pose to their companies and their own jobs, said Greg Touhill, president of the federal group of security infrastructure company Cyxtera Technologies Inc. He previously served as the federal government's CISO.

"CEOs like to have the people in organizations that are the most important close to them," he said.

**********

Quotable

"How do you tell your boss his or her organization isn't keeping up?"

-- Phil Schneidermeyer, a partner at executive search firm Ward Howell International Ltd.

**********

More Cyber News

Citrix investigates cyberattack from foreign hackers. Software company Citrix Systems Inc. is investigating how hackers gained access to its internal network, NBC reports. The Federal Bureau of Investigation alerted Citrix about the attack, the company said in a blog post. Hackers appear to have used a technique known as password spraying, which takes advantage of weak passwords to access a system.

Tracking the damage. Citrix has taken steps to contain the damage, it said. Hackers "may have accessed and downloaded business documents. The specific documents that may have been accessed, however, are currently unknown. At this time, there is no indication that the security of any Citrix product or service was compromised," it said in the blog post.

Iranian link? A group of Iranian hackers attacked Citrix as well as energy companies and government agencies, according to cybersecurity company Resecurity International Inc., NBC reports. Citrix was attacked by the same group in December and again last week, Resecurity said.

Facebook sues alleged hackers for collecting customer data. About 60,000 Facebook Inc. users revealed personal data after taking quizzes, the social media company revealed in a lawsuit Friday. Facebook is suing two Ukranians for allegedly tricking users, through quizzes, into downloading software that scraped personal data from Facebook accounts, and sometimes included information from their friends, The Daily Beast reports. The defendants haven't responded to the complaint, filed in U.S. District Court in the Northern District of California.

Facebook fixed Messenger bug. A bug allowed hackers to see a list of who Facebook users contacted on the Messenger app if the users visited a malicious site while still logged into their Facebook account, Fortune reports. Facebook updated the web version of Messenger to fix the problem, the company told Fortune.

U.K. adds experts to address election hacking. The U.K. is expanding its staff of cyber diplomats who will work with foreign governments to address the threat of election hacking, BBC News reports. The U.K. should prosecute cyber criminals and international allies should consider actions to deter cyberattacks, Foreign Secretary Jeremy Hunt said during a speech in Scotland.

Y Combinator's Sam Altman Stepping Aside as President; Altman will become chairman, allowing him to devote more time to OpenAI, a nonprofit artificial-intelligence  research group he created with Elon Musk

By Heather Mack 
332 words
8 March 2019
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Sam Altman is stepping aside as president of Y Combinator, the famed Silicon Valley accelerator that has spawned some 1,900 companies.

Y Combinator said in a blog post Mr. Altman will shift to the role of chairman, enabling him to devote more time to OpenAI, a nonprofit artificial-intelligence research group he created with Tesla Inc. Chief Executive Elon Musk.

Mr. Altman has served as Y Combinator's president since 2014, when he was tapped to succeed co-founder Paul Graham. The firm said in a blog post that little will change in the daily administration because it is run "as a partnership."

In a brief interview on Friday, Mr. Altman said he would still play a role guiding the strategic direction of Y Combinator as one of its partners, adding, "I'll still have a voice at that table."

The decision is the latest in a string of leadership changes within Y Combinator over the past few years. In 2016, Mr. Altman handed most of the day-to-day operations over to Michael Seibel, a longtime adviser at Y Combinator who is now chief executive.

Since then, the firm has launched several new programs including Startup School, the Series A program and YC China. Y Combinator said its network now has more than 4,000 alumni of founders.

Y Combinator's announcement comes less than two weeks ahead of its Demo Day. With 200 companies, the event will feature the largest cohort to date and will take place in San Francisco rather than the accelerator's Mountain View, Calif., headquarters. To accommodate the larger operations, the organization said it is looking for space in San Francisco to be its new headquarters.

Rolfe Winkler contributed to this article.

Water-Damage Claims Flood Home Insurers

By Leslie Scism 
795 words
8 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

More American homes are flooding from the inside.

Old pipes and valves, worn-out hoses on second-story washing machines and faulty connections for a proliferation of water-using appliances are causing a surge in increasingly expensive damage reported to insurers. The increase has occurred even as many other types of claims -- including fire -- have declined in frequency, industry figures show.

One in 50 homeowners filed a water-damage claim each year between 2013 and 2017, the latest data analyzed by Verisk Analytics' ISO insurance-analytics unit. It crunches industry data on a five-year rolling basis. The 2.05% frequency rate is up from 1.44% annually between 2005 and 2009.

The bottom line is a $13 billion water-damage bill for companies that insure homes in 2017. Claims average about $10,000, ISO says.

"Wildfires, hurricanes and tornadoes catch headlines, but the reality is that the No. 1 kind of risk that the everyday consumer has is a water claim," said Jon-Michael Kowall, an executive in the property-insurance business of USAA, one of the nation's biggest home insurers.

"It is lurking in the house," he said.

To tackle the problem, Texas-based USAA has 6,000 policyholders testing water-detecting sensors in a multiyear pilot project. This type of experimentation, where devices are installed to spot potential water damage, has morphed into one of the hottest corners of InsureTech, innovation focused on the insurance sector.

Chubb Ltd., one of the biggest insurers of high-end homes, said the number of annual water claims costing more than $500,000 has doubled since 2015, and those over $1 million have tripled.

Industry executives said there are myriad factors driving the costs higher.

The rise in overall claims is due partly to aging homes. A postwar building boom in the 1950s gave way to other booms, meaning much of America lives in houses that are decades old and become likelier candidates for plumbing failures. Even homes built during the real-estate bubble of the early 2000s can generate claims as they often have far more appliances with water connections.

In addition, more homeowners today want their laundry room on an upstairs floor.

"In the old days, if the washing machine had a leak, you'd get a mop" and scrub a concrete floor and be done with it, said Chubb Executive Vice President Paul Krump.

The damage can be particularly stunning in expensive homes.

Developer George Fermanian had always been more concerned about the ocean potentially damaging his two-decade-old oceanfront property in Southern California. The property even has a 12-foot sea wall.

But last year, a second-story toilet tank cracked and spilled water in the house for an unknown, extended period when he was away. Oak floors, walls, artwork, electronics gear and seats in a home theater were damaged.

"Walls are gypsum, wood absorbs water, insulation absorbs water, your cabinets absorb water," said Mr. Fermanian. Repairs on his property took eight months and cost Chubb just over $1 million.

Chubb also paid for installation of a water-shutoff system that detects unusual flows of water through the plumbing and can be set to shut off within a minute of such activity, Mr. Fermanian said.

In luxury homes, wet bars, water-filtration systems, hot- and cold-water taps, extra bathrooms and other features typically create 40 points of connection into the plumbing system, said Stephen Poux, a senior executive at American International Group Inc.

"That's a lot of opportunity for a valve or a connection . . . to spring a leak," he said.

Mr. Poux regularly meets InsureTech entrepreneurs and tests problem-detecting products. "I've personally piloted several devices," he said. "They don't always work."

AIG and some other insurers offer premium credits for policyholders' use of technology deemed effective.

Though insurers' payments are growing, it doesn't mean they pay every water claim. Since the 1960s, standard homeowners policies have excluded storm surge and river flooding. And in general, homeowners' policies cover "sudden and accidental" damage, not routine maintenance.

So homeowners who neglect an obvious slow leak for months before serious damage occurs could end up in a coverage dispute, insurance executives and lawyers said.

Among participants in USAA's pilot project is Mark Fredriksen, who placed sensors near the water heater and washing machine and under kitchen and bathroom sinks, in his Smithtown, N.Y., home.

So far, they have averted two potentially costly claims, he said. One sensor detected dripping from an old bathroom valve. On another occasion, as he was putting his children to bed upstairs, the kitchen sensor chirped. Water was spitting out from a dishwasher hose.

Banking & Finance: Robots Drive 5 ETFs Launched by Goldman

By Asjylyn Loder 
432 words
8 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B10
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Goldman Sachs Group Inc. launched five new exchange-traded funds that rely on indexes built with machine learning and artificial intelligence.

The New York investment bank is working with Motif Investing Inc. to identify companies that fit with broad themes that Goldman's money managers think will drive growth, like "finance reimagined" and "data-driven world."

Goldman's foray into computer-driven ETFs is the latest sign of Wall Street's increased reliance on sophisticated automation. Computers can read far faster than a legion of analysts, sifting company reports, news stories and regulatory filings for clues to a company's future. Two years ago, BlackRock Inc., the world's largest money manager by assets, announced that it would overhaul its actively managed equities business to rely more on robots than humans.

"With machine learning, you can get the benefits of hundreds of stock pickers in a low-cost quantitative way," said Todd Rosenbluth, senior director of ETF and mutual fund research at CFRA. "You can create indexes that evolve based on shifts in data."

Recent years have seen the proliferation of a new breed of indexes that veer even further from plain-vanilla benchmarks. ETF issuers have designed funds that try to pick companies that are less volatile, higher quality or more socially responsible.

Further blurring the line between active and passive investing, many of the smart index funds are really just attempts to codify trading strategies that active money managers have employed for decades. Indexes aren't all that different from robots. They follow their methodology -- essentially their programming -- to pick stocks and rebalance their portfolios at scheduled intervals.

The goal was to define themes that have mass appeal and staying power and avoid the downfall of being so narrowly defined that investors lose interest, said Michael Crinieri, global head of ETF strategy at Goldman.

Data mining and language processing help companies like index provider MSCI Inc. quantify how companies perform on environmental, social and governance criteria. Motif uses web crawlers to scan patent databases and academic journals as well as company financial filings.

"What AI allows you to do is to be more dynamic and data driven, and remove the human bias," said Art Amador, chief operations officer and co-founder of EquBot Inc., in San Francisco. "We think it will help you perform more consistently through different markets."

EquBot has two AI-driven stock ETFs with more than $150 million in assets and runs separately managed accounts for investors.

Agritech investment surges more than 40% in 2018

Emiko Terazono in London 
421 words
7 March 2019
17:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Money piles into sector as traditional agricultural and food industries come under pressure

The amount of money invested worldwide in agricultural and food technology jumped by more than 40 per cent in 2018, to $17bn, according to new industry figures.

According to AgFunder, an online venture capital platform, the number of deals also rose 11 per cent year-on-year, to 1,442. Nearly half of the fundraising took place in the US, while China was the second-biggest market, followed by India.

Entrepreneurs and technology experts have piled into  agritech  in recent years as the agricultural and food industries come under pressure from a growing global population, environmental concerns, labour shortages and changing consumer tastes.

At the same time, low commodity prices and significant technological developments, from machine learning to material science, are providing opportunities for entrepreneurs and investors to reinvent the food system.

S2G Ventures, a venture fund focused on investments in food and agriculture, was the most active investor in the sector last year, with 20 investments, according to AgFunder.

Sanjeev Krishnan, managing partner at S2G, said:  We have never seen the tectonic plates shifting as much as they are now, from the farmgate to the fridge. 

Most of the funding recorded by AgFunder in 2018 went to restaurant and grocery delivery services, as consumer demand for convenience and on-demand food grew across the globe. Restaurant food delivery deals almost doubled during the 12 months, to $3.9bn. Naspers, the South African investor, led a $1bn funding round in Indian food-delivery service Swiggy, while in the US, SoftBank led a $535m round in DoorDash, the San Francisco-based start-up.

Meanwhile, investments in biotechnology increased more than 50 per cent, to $1.5bn, in 2018. Indigo Ag, the Boston-based start-up that connects sellers and buyers of crops through a mobile app, raised $250m, bringing its estimated value to more than 3bn. Pivot Bio gained backing for its nitrogen-fixing microbes from Breakthrough Ventures, a billionaire investor syndicate fund with investors such as Jeff Bezos and Michael Bloomberg.

Funding for  novel farming systems , such as indoor agriculture systems and insect farms, dropped, with 8.6 per cent fewer dollars in financing at $596m.

The innovative food category, which includes alternative meat, also saw funding fall 4.7 per cent to $502m, even as Impossible Foods, one of the most mature plant-based meat start-ups, raised $114m from investors including Singapore s Temasek.

Why Aon abandoned efforts to buy rival in under 24 hours

1,605 words
7 March 2019
05:08
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Welcome to Due Diligence, the FT s daily deals briefing

FT premium subscribers can click here to receive Due Diligence every day by email.

Insurance brokers are supposed to help you to prepare for the unexpected. But Aon, the industry s number two player, caught the market off-guard on Wednesday when it said it had abandoned plans to buy Willis Towers Watson in an all-share transaction.

How long did it take for Aon to change its mind? Less than 24 hours from when it confirmed its interest.

To be fair Aon never even got close to combining with Willis Towers, the third-largest broker. On Tuesday afternoon it was forced admit it was considering a tie-up with its rival Willis, a deal that would have created the largest player in the industry. By Wednesday morning another statement landed:the potential all-share bid was being withdrawn.

What prompted Aon s quick about face? Here are some reasons:

*

the prospect of a lengthy, market-by-market examination of where the two compete by the antitrust regulators;

*

the share price reaction after the leak, which saw Aon s shares drop sharply. It recovered most (but not all) of the lost ground on news that the idea was dead;

*

the challenges associated with smashing together the cultures at two big, people-dependent businesses. DD understands that is was highly unlikely Willis boss John Haley(pictured below) would have been keen. He may have forcefully got that message across to Aon last night.

It was just last year that the UK s competition regulator investigated whether insurance brokers were engaging in anti-competitive behaviour.

The Competition and Markets Authority looked at whether some of Britain s largest investment consultants   including Willis Towers Watson and Aon   should be forced to split off their consultancy and fiduciary management businesses.

It eventually determined that it wasn t necessary to force a split but it said trustees should try to secure better prices.

The incident does leave Aon with questions to answer though, not least why it was looking at such a big and risky deal.

In an interview with the FT less than a year ago, chief executive Greg Case (pictured below) said he was keen to build up the company s data and analytics capabilities. He mentioned nothing about bulking up in traditional insurance broking, which is what a Willis Towers Watson deal would have done.

Marsh & McLennan   the world s number one insurance broker   bulked up last year with the 4.3bn acquisition of UK based Jardine Lloyd Thompson. Marsh boss Dan Glaser can now watch from the sidelines as his rivals work out how to respond.

Credit default swaps get a spring clean

GSO. Those three letters mean a lot in the world of credit, and yesterday we took DD readers behind the scenes of the latest palace intrigue at Blackstone s $130bn debt investing unit.

The private equity giant has slapped golden handcuffs on Bennett Goodman   the G in GSO   in a bid to prevent the co-founder of their powerhouse credit business from following his two original partners out of the door.

While Mr Goodman s firm likes to present itself as diversified and wide ranging credit investor, GSO has unfortunately become best-known for one highly controversial practice using credit default swaps: the manufactured default.

The FT last year took a deep dive into this sharp-elbowed trade pioneered by GSO, which caused an uproar even in the aggressive world of distressed debt trading.

But now trading powerhouses such as Goldman Sachs and Apollo Global Management have worked with the International Swaps and Derivatives Association in a bid to close the loophole that Blackstone s credit unit so ruthlessly exploited.

CDS geeks can read the full textof these proposals here. But for the rest of us, there s one key point worth noting: in the recent internal shake-ups at GSO, the senior executives who helped pioneer this aggressive CDS ploy have left the firm.

GSO s once-fearsome distressed debt unit has seen a string of senior departures over the past year, as Blackstone has looked to move away from these highly contentious and public spats. It seems Mr Goodman may have a much quieter time over the next few years as his $200m share award vests.

Italy s attempt to save Alitalia may not fly

Trains, planes and err 	. 	. 	. 	well in Italy that combination is enough to cause concern.

In one of the odder industrial combinations being contemplated in Europe, travellers in Italy may soon find out what happens when you put together the country's flag carrier with its state-backed railway operator.

Policymakers in Rome have been wrestling with a way to rescue Alitalia, the perennially lossmaking flagship Italian airline. The leading option: merge it with Italian railway operator Ferrovie dello Stato Italiane alongside co-investors easyJet and Delta.

It is a plan that is raising eyebrows and that could ultimately end in tears. While Alitalia would get badly needed funds, it would not fix the structural issues plaguing it: namely overcapacity across Europe, which has driven down fares. Add in a probe by the European Commission into a loan Alitalia received from the government, as well as large unionised and pension holding workforce, and there's a good question on how Delta and easyJet would help the airline reduce its cost base.

For any DD readers who are not following the situation (or if you're not planning a summer flight to Bologna or Verona), there s something else at stake that should get your attention. Any deal between Ferrovie dello Stato Italiane and Alitalia would signal a growing willingness by Italy s populist coalition to intervene in the economy.

Read the FT s full take on the transaction here.

Job moves

Mike Turner is to join the growing list of directors stepping down from the Barclays board as activist investor Edward Bramson piles on pressure for an overhaul. Turner previously ran BAE Systems and chaired GKN. More here.

Markets
ECB's Dour Growth Forecast Gives Investors the Jitters; Banking stocks drop and government bonds rally as the ECB doubles down on a key lending program

By Avantika Chilkoti and Joe Wallace 
807 words
7 March 2019
16:54
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

European bank stocks fell sharply and government bond yields plumbed recent lows as investors came to terms with the European Central Bank's dour outlook on growth and fresh efforts to boost lending.

ECB President Mario Draghi surprised investors by "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ecb-to-launch-new-stimulus-measures-11551963228", pledging to provide "significant monetary stimulus" and saying the bank stood ready to adjust all instruments at its disposal to support the regional economy.

"The weakening in economic data points to a sizable moderation in the pace of the economic expansion that will extend into the current year," Mr. Draghi said, pointing to geopolitical risks like the threat of protectionism and fragility in emerging markets.

Germany's 10-year bond yields fell to 0.07% from 0.13% the day before, the lowest level since October 2016, when Europe's economy was just getting back on its feet following a yearslong debt crisis. The rally in bonds spread to the U.S., with 10-year Treasurys falling as low as 2.63% from 2.7% earlier in the day.

"The downgrade to growth is pretty big, probably more than most anticipated and I think they have just responded appropriately by being pre-emptive," said Karen Ward, chief market strategist for Europe, the Middle East and Africa at J.P. Morgan Asset Management. "Pre-Draghi, the ECB wasn't seen to be the most pre-emptive or reactionary central bank and he has certainly dispelled that image to some degree."

The biggest surprise for many investors was the change in expectations for rate policy, with Mr. Draghi signaling that the ECB's benchmark rates, currently negative, would be held steady through 2019 rather than just through the summer.

Bank stocks dropped sharply when Mr. Draghi announced lower growth forecasts for the region. That reversed a short-lived rally earlier in the day after the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ecbs-latest-stimulus-will-help-banks-more-than-the-economy-11551973338", or TLTROs, which have benefited lenders in Europe's more precarious economies.

Shares in Deutsche Bank AG fell 5.3%. Spain's Banco Santander SA was down 4%. UniCredit SpA, Italy's biggest bank by assets, was down 2.2% and France's BNP Paribas SA lost 1.9%.

The euro was down 0.7% against the U.S. dollar following Mr. Draghi's comments, its lowest since November, with one euro buying $1.12.

The central bank slashed its forecast for real gross domestic product growth this year to 1.1%, from 1.7% just three months ago, and its forecast for consumer-price inflation to 1.2% from 1.6%. Lower growth and inflation expectations suggest interest rates will remain lower for longer, which could hurt bank earnings by squeezing net-interest margins.

Investors expected "https://www.wsj.com/articles/markets-warm-to-the-prospect-of-an-ecb-funding-boost-for-banks-11550656802" but the timing of Thursday's announcement, and the inclusion of details about the program, were a surprise. The program allows banks to borrow at much cheaper rates than those available in the bond market. The aim is to encourage banks to lend money to companies and consumers on more generous terms, getting credit to flow through the economy.

This funding follows two earlier batches, which ran from 2014 to 2017 and formed one prong of the ECB's extraordinary efforts to revive the eurozone economy after the sovereign-debt crisis. Eurozone banks still owe the ECB  722 billion ($811 billion) under the previous programs. Mr. Draghi said the new round will start in September and has been designed to avoid stress in bank funding markets as the loans from the existing program come due over the next two years.

"This helps at the margins by keeping the cost of credit cheap, but the issue in Europe has been demand for credit," Nick Wall, a fund manager at Merian Global Investors, said in a written comment. "For money demand to increase, Europe will be looking for an upswing in Chinese growth to boost its exports and for governments to boost spending."

The new TLTRO program is good news for bank financing, according to Marco Valli, chief European economist at UniCredit, but the wider impact on the economy is "difficult to assess" as the central bank has yet to give full details on the cost of these loans.

Italian banks, a weak point in the eurozone economy, benefited most from earlier rounds of funding and were seen as particularly vulnerable if the ECB didn't release more loans. They borrowed 33% of the second TLTRO program, according to Haver Analytics ahead of Spanish and French banks with 23% and 16%, respectively.

Pat Minczeski contributed to this article.

Cyber Daily: Fear and Gloating at RSA; Zuckerberg's Encryption Vision; Iranian Hackers

1,328 words

WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY

Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Cyber Daily: Fear and Gloating at RSA; Zuckerberg's Encryption Vision; Iranian Hackers

By Kim S. Nash

Good day. The RSA Conference this week in San Francisco hosts more than 40,000 cybersecurity professionals, law enforcers and industry executives looking to discuss the latest in offensive and defensive technology and practices. Reporter Adam Janofsky is on site.

Topics range from talk about the potential of a geopolitical cyberattack, to pitches for the latest and greatest technology to fight hacks. One CISO discussed using machine learning to analyze security alerts on a daily basis.

Also today: Iran is fingered in attacks on 200 organizations world-wide, and Facebook Inc. CEO Mark Zuckerberg explains his encryption plans.

**********

NSA Chief: Huawei's Involvement In 5G Networks  Concerns Me a Lot'

By Adam Janofsky

SAN FRANCISCO The U.S. and its allies should be concerned about letting Huawei Technologies Co. help build 5G networks because of the potential for the company to be used as a tool by the Chinese government, said Gen. Paul Nakasone, director of the National Security Agency, on Wednesday.

"[China] has very strong and deliberate ties to its private sector, and that concerns me a lot," he said during a keynote at the RSA Conference.

Huawei has denied allegations that it would spy on behalf of any government.

The U.S. has tried to persuade allies including Germany, Italy and Japan in recent months to limit their use of Huawei telecommunications equipment due to fears that it could be used to spy on communications or disrupt networks, The Wall Street Journal reported.

But not everyone is alarmed. Last month, German officials said the government was leaning towards letting Huawei participate in its high-speed internet infrastructure plans.

Gen. Nakasone remains steadfast in his opposition to Huawei in 5G infrastructure. "Are we comfortable with a nation that doesn't share our rule of law, that doesn't share our norms, that doesn't have due process having that much control over our communications [infrastructure]?" he asked.

Mr. Nakasone's comments suggest that the U.S. will continue to seek to block Huawei from 5G rollouts even as the company tries to assuage governments and consumers with ad campaigns and flashy displays of the trustworthiness of its technology.

"This is going to be a discussion that's going to continue for some time," he said.

**********

Aflac's Machine Learning Tools Interpret Barrage of Security Alerts

By Adam Janofsky

It's easy for companies to gather vast amounts of data about attempted cyberattacks on their systems and networks. It's harder to interpret the information and use it to formulate effective responses.

Machine learning can help, according to Aflac Inc.'s chief information security officer, Tim Callahan.

Aflac uses machine learning and automation tools to help process the flood of data it collects from its firewalls and attack mitigation services as well as threat intelligence data it purchases from security vendors. Also in the mix are alerts from the Department of Homeland Security and the Financial Services Information Sharing and Analysis Center.

"The problem most companies have is they are overwhelmed by the amount of information they have and can't do anything with it," he said.

How it works

Aflac's technology, which is a customized version of tools from commercial vendors, sorts alerts based on the likelihood they will impact the company's computer systems. It assigns each alert a confidence score depending on whether it has been a reliable indicator in the past. Repeated password attempts, for example, would generally be given a low score because the culprit is likely a user who forgot a password, not a hacker.

Malware signatures examined by Aflac's security researchers would be given a high score because the company trusts its researchers to verify whether the code is malicious or not, Mr. Callahan said.

The approach -- sometimes referred to as security orchestration, automation and response, or SOAR -- was implemented partly out of necessity. The company's security team was bombarded by the ever-increasing amount of threat data it collected, and investigations were often time consuming and unnecessary.

Machine learning and automation tools have allowed a five-person threat-management team to analyze and respond to thousands of alerts each day, Mr. Callahan said. The company employs about 300 security workers globally, with most in the U.S. and Japan.

About 15% of organizations will adopt SOAR technology by 2020, up from 1% in 2017, according to Gartner Inc.

Since Aflac implemented the SOAR system in September 2015, it has blocked more than 50 million malicious domains. There have been less than a dozen false positives, in which a legitimate user or website was accidentally blocked, Mr. Callahan said.

Clarification of an article in our March 6 newsletter about common supply-chain mistakes:

A customer misconfigured an Amazon Web Services server, making vulnerable data belonging to Dow Jones Risk & Compliance, which is owned by Dow Jones & Co. A spokeswoman for Dow Jones didn't name the company that made the data vulnerable. Dow Jones publishes The Wall Street Journal.

Percentage of CEOs who say their company might be affected by geopolitical cyber activity, according to a PricewaterhouseCoopers LLC survey of 3,000 business leaders world-wide.

**********

More Cyber News

Also at RSA: Agency in  deliberative process' over metadata surveillance. Gen. Nakasone also said the NSA is considering the future of its controversial surveillance program that collects domestic phone metadata, writes WSJ's Dustin Volz. The program is up for congressional renewal at the end of the year. The NSA will work closely with Congress and the White House to determine the program's fate.

NSA to offer free tool for malware analysis. Rob Joyce, a senior advisor at the NSA, said the agency plans to release an open-source software tool companies can use to reverse-engineer malware. Called Ghidra, the tool has helped the NSA for more than 10 years, ZDNet reports.

Iran linked to cyberattacks on about 200 organizations. Iranian hackers have hit hundreds of companies in past two years, WSJ reports, citing research from Microsoft Corp.

What happened: The campaign, the scope of which hadn't previously been reported, caused damages estimated at hundreds of millions of dollars in lost productivity. It affected oil-and-gas companies, heavy-machinery manufacturers and international conglomerates in countries including Germany, the U.K. and the U.S., Microsoft said. Italian oil company Saipem SpA was also hit by the hackers.

The attackers. Microsoft traced the attacks to a group it calls Holmium. The group targeted more than 2,200 people across nearly 200 organizations with phishing emails that, if clicked, can install code that steals information or wipes data from computers on the victim's network, Microsoft said. In the past, Iran has denied involvement in such cyber activity. A spokesman for Iran's mission to the United Nations didn't return messages seeking comment.

Facebook plans encrypted messaging, eventually. Facebook Inc. is betting that the future of social media will be private messaging and chats in small groups, rather than the public sharing of photos and messages that the company pioneered, WSJ reports. In an interview Wednesday, Chief Executive Mark Zuckerberg said people increasingly prefer to communicate in small groups or one to one.

Encryption coming: Facebook won't roll out significant encryption features this year, Mr. Zuckerberg said, because it wants to talk with privacy experts, regulators and law-enforcement entities around the world about how best to implement its technology and privacy goals.

Cyber tax scams about to take off. Phishing and other cyber scams targeting tax filers are increasing, the Internal Revenue Service warned this week. Data breaches at Marriott International Inc., Facebook Inc., Quora Inc. and other organizations have exposed personal data for millions of people, giving cyber criminals rich information to use in tax schemes, FedScoop reports.

WSJ Pro
Insurers Turn to AI to Better Assess Risk; Property and casualty insurers AXA XL and QBE hope AI can help in pricing policies

By John Murawski 
819 words
6 March 2019
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Property and casualty insurers are starting to deploy artificial intelligence to assess insurance risks with the goals of improving policy pricing and finding new business opportunities.

AXA XL, the Bermuda-based subsidiary of global insurer AXA SA, is testing a natural language processing program that can analyze hundreds of property-loss prevention reports in minutes, a task that would take a human risk analyst days or even weeks to complete. The reports, prepared by field engineers from on-site visits, contain details such as structural features and potential risks like hazardous materials.

The project is still in pilot phase and requires modifications to adjust for errors, but the company already is looking at replicating the program in other languages for use in multiple countries, said Scott Ewing, AXA XL's Americas engineering leader.

QBE Ventures, the investing arm of Australia-based QBE Insurance Group, has invested an undisclosed amount in AI startup Cytora, which develops machine learning and natural language processing. Cytora's technology can analyze building permits, news articles and satellite imagery, as well as insurer's internal data, to find hidden risks in its commercial insurance portfolio. The building permits, for example, include details about code violations, additions and modifications that provide clues about insurance risk. After a yearlong pilot, QBE's underwriters began making decisions based on the AI data this year, said Ted Stuckey, managing director of QBE Ventures.

"There's a lot of noise, a lot of promises made, a lot of big numbers put in front of you," Mr. Stuckey said about AI technology proposals. "The only way to prove if there's any potential is to run a pilot."

QBE also is experimenting with another AI application, a texting assistant for processing home insurance damage claims. Named Hi Marley, it is undergoing testing in the U.S. Hi Marley speeds up claims processing by allowing customers to interact with the program and send pictures, invoices and other documents at their convenience, not when an insurance rep is available on the phone, Mr. Stuckey said.

AI deployment among insurers, according to Boston-based research firm Novarica, is still low, but the earliest pilot programs are beginning to show promise. Among property and casualty insurers, 29% are using or testing machine-learning programs and unstructured text programs to analyze data. Unstructured text takes the form of narratives and notes, as opposed to more organized information typically in databases.

One reason for the slow AI adoption: The change can take a year or more because risk analysts have to train AI how to interpret construction permits, police reports, doctors' notes and other records. Then they have to fine-tune their programs to exclude irrelevant information and spot gaps in the data.

On top of the technological implementation challenges, insurers face cultural hurdles in transitioning to AI-derived solutions. At QBE, for example, the human underwriters still make the final decisions on risk and pricing of commercial properties, with the aid of the Cytora risk score. Over time, as the company becomes more proficient with AI, the technology could assume greater responsibility.

At AXA XL, the AI program is still prone to error, which has to be manually fixed. But the system is being refined with each iteration and is improving, Mr. Ewing said.

A wave of AI adoption could be under way, as most insurers say they have an interest in applying AI, even if they aren't currently testing it, according to a January report from Novarica.

Research firm Celent, based in Boston, said in an August report that most AI investment by insurers will support core insurance activities, such as underwriting and sales, as opposed to back-office functions.

AXA XL is a case in point. Its commercial portfolio comprises bundles of properties that contain hundreds or thousands of facilities in each bundle. Historically, the company's risk consultants only sampled a fraction of the properties in each bundle, leaving most properties unanalyzed.

Using AI, the insurer now analyzes all the properties in each bundle for a better understanding of its risk exposure. Mr. Ewing said the program is becoming more accurate as the software is tweaked, but it is too early to assess the cost implications of AI.

At QBE, the AI program passed the test and is now in use, and the expected result is more precise pricing of insurance policies. The company spent months aggregating, formatting and standardizing legacy data from numerous systems into one AI program, and validating that it worked.

"We believe we'll see a decrease in our loss ratio because we're underwriting with a much more comprehensive view of what the risk really is," Mr. Stuckey said.

Economy
U.S. Private Sector Adds 183,000 Jobs in February; Most of jobs added were in service sector; with medium-size businesses posting strongest growth

By Micah Maidenberg 
323 words
6 March 2019
14:03
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The U.S. private sector added 183,000 jobs in February, according to a report released Wednesday, although the gains were smaller than expected as job creation slowed from a strong performance in January.

Economists polled by The Wall Street Journal were expecting the addition of 185,000 jobs last month.

The ADP National Employment Report revised the number of private-sector jobs added in January to 300,000 from 213,000.

The ADP Research Institute and Moody's Analytics produce the monthly report using ADP payroll data to measure the change in seasonally adjusted nonfarm private-sector employment.

"Job gains are still strong, but they have likely seen their high watermark for this expansion," Moody's Analytics chief economist Mark Zandi said in prepared remarks.

Most of the jobs added in February 139,000 were in the service sector, while the rest were in the goods-producing sector.

Medium-size businesses, or those with between 50 and 499 employees, had the strongest job growth last month, adding roughly 95,000 jobs, followed by large businesses with 77,000 and small businesses with 12,000.

Small-business growth has declined as such companies struggle with offering competitive wages and benefits, said Ahu Yildirmaz, ADP Research Institute co-head and vice president, in prepared remarks.

The professional and business-services sector had the largest job growth within the broader services category in February at 49,000, the report says. It was followed by health services and education, which added 37,000 positions, though all of those gains were due to growth in health and social-assistance jobs.

Construction companies added 25,000 jobs in February, while manufacturers created 17,000 positions, according to the report.

WSJ Pro
Venture Investors Express Disappointment at Resignation of FDA Commissioner Gottlieb; Gottlieb, a former venture capitalist, pushed for more consistent dealings with industry, investors say

By Brian Gormley and Heather Mack 
429 words
6 March 2019
00:01
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Less than two years ago venture capitalists saw one of their own, Scott Gottlieb, take over as commissioner of the Food and Drug Administration.

Now, they are waving him a wistful goodbye.

Dr. Gottlieb, a medical doctor and former venture partner with New Enterprise Associates, became FDA commissioner in May 2017. On Tuesday the agency said he "https://www.wsj.com/articles/fda-chief-scott-gottlieb-to-leave-agency-11551816541", to the disappointment of venture investors who saw him and his priorities as positive for their industry and the medical system.

"It is really bad news for health care in our country," Steve Kraus, a partner with Bessemer Venture Partners, said in an email. "He [was] a clear, once-in-a-generation civil-servant star."

Dr. Gottlieb's initiatives affected companies across several industries. He sought to revamp clinical trials, speed approval of generic medications and reduce nicotine in cigarettes. The agency also made an impact in digital health, last year approving a medical device that uses artificial intelligence to detect the eye disease diabetic retinopathy, the first such screening technology that doesn't require a medical professional to interpret the results.

Among venture investors, the more significant change he encouraged was cultural. For years before his appointment, venture capitalists complained the agency was inconsistent in its dealings with industry, something that has been changing more recently, investors say.

"He is a very fair person and he understands the [challenge] of being a drug innovator," said Jay Lichter, managing director of Avalon Ventures. "You've seen some really good changes at FDA a lot more collaboration, a lot more willingness to listen and solve the problem together."

The FDA last year approved a record 59 new drugs, encouraging the view that the agency has been an ally of medical innovation. But in an interview last summer, Dr. Gottlieb said he didn't measure success by the number of new products approved, looking instead at how efficient the agency is in making decisions and providing feedback.

Investors say he succeeded on that last score.

"Scott was a great forward-thinking leader and understood the importance of working with industry to advance innovative medicines," said Atlas Venture Partner Bruce Booth in an email. "Hopefully, the next FDA commissioner will be as much of a champion of innovation as Scott has been."

Direct Line slashes dividend as it insures against Brexit

Oliver Ralph in London 
502 words
5 March 2019
08:11
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Group looks to strengthen balance sheet, citing  political uncertainty 

Insurer Direct Line has bolstered its balance sheet and cut payouts to shareholders as it braces itself for Brexit.

Like other insurance companies, Direct Line holds a large amount of bonds which could be vulnerable to economic turbulence.

The company on Tuesday said that its overall payout to shareholders for 2018 would be 17 per cent below the previous year as the size of the special dividend   which Direct Line has paid in each of the past six years   was cut sharply.

Penny James, chief financial officer, said the insurer had decided to strengthen its capital base because of  political uncertainty .

Direct Line s solvency ratio   a measure of capital available as a proportion of the minimum required   stood at 170 per cent at the end of last year, up from 162 per cent a year earlier.

Ms James is due to become chief executive later this year, taking over from Paul Geddes who will step down after 10 years. The two told investors that they would keep the capital base under constant review and would consider the best ways of returning any excess capital to shareholders.

The results were the last for Mr Geddes.  It s good for me to be bowing out on a strong set of results in competitive markets,  he said.

Overall premiums slipped 5 per cent as partnerships with Nationwide and Sainsbury s came to an end, but premiums from the company s own brands rose.

Pre-tax profits increased 8 per cent to 583m though, as lower investment profits and reserve releases were more than offset by lower finance costs.

The regular dividend rose 3 per cent, but the special dividend was cut by 45 per cent, leading to a total payout for the year of 29.3p per share, against 35.4p for 2017.

Analysts said the results were slightly ahead of expectations.

Andreas van Embden, at Peel Hunt, said:  Paul Geddes has successfully steered Direct Line back to a pre-eminent position in the UK insurance market and he leaves [the company] in a strong position. 

The company s shares were flat on Tuesday at 355p, more than double the price at which Mr Geddes floated the company in 2012.

Ms James said her priorities for the next couple of years were to deliver a range of technology improvements that would allow the company to improve its pricing and customer service, and to cut costs.

She also said Direct Line would launch a brand called Darwin, which would be rolled out on price comparison sites.  It is a little like building your own start-up,  she said.  It will use different pricing techniques based on machine learning. 

According to Citi analyst James Shuck, Direct Line s market share in motor insurance sold on price comparison sites is just 6 per cent, which is well below its share of the wider motor market.

Features
AI start-ups found to lack artificial intelligence 

ALIYA RAM 
469 words
5 March 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
14
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Technology

Four in 10 of Europe's "artificial intelligence" start-ups use no artificial intelligence programs in their products, according to a report that highlights the hype around the technology.

The research by MMC Ventures, a London-based investment firm, could not find evidence, based on public information and interviews with executives, of artificial intelligence applications at 40 per cent of 2,830 "AI" start-ups in Europe.

Nevertheless, the companies are often described as AI-focused, said David Kelnar, MMC's head of research, who added that many start-ups had plans to develop machine learning programs in the future.

Skyscape, for example, a Londonbased start-up that analyses rooftops to find space for drone landings, urban farms and other projects, does not use machine learning or artificial intelligence, but is classified as an AI start-up. "Our work today is increasing our data and knowledge to allow the implementation of AI," said Brandon Bell, chief executive and founder. "It's not as easy as just switching on AI. A lot of preparation work is required."

The start-up's website says: "Skyscape uses AI to analyse rooftops and identify opportunities to maximise utilisation and address urban challenges".

Companies branded as "AI" businesses have historically raised larger funding rounds and secured higher valuations than other software businesses. The median funding round for an "AI" start-up last year was about 15 per cent higher than for a software start-up, the research showed.

"A lot of venture capital groups in Europe are responsive to companies that are interested in raising money [for AI]," Mr Kelnar said.

The term "artificial intelligence" has been applied to a variety of technologies, from simple computer programs that automate tasks to more complex neural networks, which are systems for processing data, and machine learning algorithms.

The widespread use of the term has made it difficult for venture capital investors to distinguish between actual and purported "AI" businesses.

"There are different levels of sophistication when it comes to building these algorithms and many hype up the claims of what they're actually building," said Ophelia Brown, a partner at Blossom Capital. "It's the responsibility of the investor to do due diligence on the company ... but a lot of firms are not able to do it."

Politicians have contributed to enthusiasm for companies that work in "AI" by touting artificial intelligence success stories and ringfencing funds for the sector.

European countries have become particularly well known for nurturing AI businesses and seen an explosion of start-ups targeting health, finance and the media.

Almost 8 per cent of European startups founded last year were AI companies, up from 3 per cent in 2015, MMC's research showed, with the UK taking the lead.

By Laura Cooper

Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

One Equity Partners' software developer Orion Systems Integrators LLC is acquiring outsourced product development provider MERA Group.

The merger would nearly double the roughly $30 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization Orion had at the time of One Equity's original investment, according to a person familiar with the deal.

Zurich-based MERA Group specializes in customized software services, including product architecture, design, implementation, integration and product management.

Edison, N.J.-based Orion provides outsourced software development services, including information technology architecture, application development and support, data analytics, testing and assurance, managed services and project management. Its clients operate across industries that span financial services, education, health care, communications, professional services and sports operations.

MERA generally works with companies in industries that include telecommunications equipment providers, automotive and transportation as well as financial technology.

Chip Schorr, a senior managing director at One Equity Partners, said the firm began looking at MERA around the same time it started considering an investment in Orion. The deal for Orion closed last October and One Equity started talks with MERA last summer.

Mr. Schorr said the marriage of the companies provides "two pillars" of outsourced software development for its customers. Orion specializes in automation, while MERA focuses on outsourced product development.

"One of the things we love about it is every business in the world is consuming more and more software to sell to their customers or [to] consume internally to automate processes," Mr. Schorr said.

New York-based One Equity invests in companies with earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of between $10 million and $70 million. It focuses on companies in the health care, technology and technology services as well as industrials and related services industries.

The firm announced in February that it was combining Merfish Pipe & Supply Inc., a carbon steel standard pipe distributor the firm backed in 2013, with United Pipe & Supply Co., another provider of the same kind of pipes.

WSJ Pro
AI Speeds Up Reimbursements at Auto-Glass Company; Safelite is launching a system that is expected to improve the process used to repay shop owners for parts they buy

By John McCormick 
622 words
5 March 2019
09:37
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Safelite Group Inc., a company best known for its vehicle glass repair and replacement service, is integrating artificial intelligence with robotic process-automation tools to speed up and improve the way it reimburses shop owners for the parts they buy.

Safelite operates 720 facilities around the country and runs a mobile unit that travels to customer locations to perform repairs. But another part of Safelite's business is handling third-party claims on behalf of more than 200 car insurance carriers and it works with a network of 7,500 glass-replacement companies that handle windshield, car window or other repairs.

The network shops buy replacement parts and send in proof of purchases to Safelite, which then matches the receipts with the insurance claim reference numbers and pays the repair shops.

The entire process is labor intensive. The shops email or fax purchase receipts to Safelite for reimbursement. The receipts from parts providers come in different formats and shop owners often write in the customer reference numbers on the purchase order. Safelite customer-service representatives have to decipher the handwriting on the receipts before matching it with a claim.

"A really monotonous and repetitive thing that they have to do," said Bruce Millard, Safelite's vice president of digital and customer innovation.

To improve the process, Safelite looked to a combination of robotic process-automation technologies, which automate repetitive business tasks; Google Cloud Vision API image recognition; and Google Cloud Natural Language text analysis. Development was done in the fourth quarter of last year and Safelite is currently launching the system.

With the new system, proof of purchase documents are emailed to Safelite and directed to the system, which automatically opens the emails, pulls down any attachments, and passes proof of purchase documents on to the Google services. The Google services work together to read the proof of purchase images, detect the handwritten reference numbers and then match those numbers to an open claim to make a match.

Safelite found in testing that the system was able to perform a match 90% of the time, Mr. Millard said. With the new setup, the customer-service team will be able to spend their time reviewing the proof of purchases to ensure the charges are valid.

"This is getting the mechanics stuff in place where the humans can do the value-added work that needs to be done," Mr. Millard said.

More companies are starting to integrate AI and robotic process automation, said JT Kostman, leader for applied artificial intelligence and frontier technologies at Grant Thornton LLP. The audit, tax and advisory firm last month released the findings of a chief information officer survey that found that these executives are looking to AI and RPA as key technologies for future deployment.

"Within the last year to two years we've seen more of a change than we've seen collectively in the history of the time we've been doing RPA," Mr. Kostman said.

Still, there are steps companies need to take to ensure a smooth implementation.

For Safelite, Mr. Millard said, "the biggest challenge is getting the core IT team comfortable working with this new technology."

He said the key was getting the staff to understand what they needed to know and what they didn't about the system. Some wondered if they would be required to become data scientists. But Mr. Millard assured them that they didn't since Safelite decided to go with off-the-shelf technology.

Discover's Robots Screen Borrowers

By AnnaMaria Andriotis 
718 words
5 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Discount-store shoppers may soon get an unexpected benefit: better odds when applying for personal loans from Discover Financial Services.

Discover, best known for its credit cards, plans to use artificial intelligence to assess hundreds of unusual characteristics about personal-loan applicants in an attempt to get its rising losses under control.

A history of discount-store shopping, for example, will boost an applicant's chances of getting a personal loan, according to people familiar with the matter, while writing the full legal name of an employer on a loan application will lower it.

Applicants who report high incomes will be flagged as potentially more risky, the people said.

The new methodology will apply to U.S. consumers who apply for personal loans starting around the second half of the year, they added.

Lenders for decades primarily used information found on consumers' credit reports and scores to assess potential borrowers. In recent years, however, more lenders have been using more data -- such as bank-account balances and utility payments -- when making credit decisions. The lenders largely are looking for information that will allow them to approve more borrowers without taking on significantly more risk.

Discover has an additional reason for turning to new data sources: It is trying to get a handle on losses on personal loans. The lender has tightened underwriting standards and cut back on approvals, denying a larger percentage of applicants who didn't receive a solicitation from the company. Yet losses have continued to pile up.

Borrowers use these unsecured loans -- a fast-growing slice of the consumer-credit business -- to fund home renovations and vacations, among other things. The loans aren't secured by a house or car, so lenders have little recourse if borrowers stop paying.

The personal-loan market remains small compared with credit cards, auto loans and student loans, which each top $1 trillion in outstanding balances. Personal-loan balances totaled a record $138 billion at the end of 2018, according to TransUnion.

Discover wrote off 4.5% of personal-loan balances in the fourth quarter, compared with 3.2% and 2% of credit-card and private student-loan balances, respectively. Personal loans are the worst performer among the company's credit offerings.

The company also is trying to root out swindlers applying for credit with fake identities and made-up or stolen information.

Industrywide, $32.7 million of personal loan balances were owed by suspected fake identities as of the second quarter of 2018, up about 23% from the end of 2017, according to TransUnion. A Discover spokesman declined to comment on the company's fraud rates.

"We're looking for people who won't pay us back. It could be because they can't afford to or that it was fraudulent from the beginning," Discover Chief Executive Roger Hochschild said in an interview.

ZestFinance, which develops AI software for financial companies, is working with Discover on the effort.

Hundreds of data points gathered from loan applications and elsewhere will be considered in the underwriting process, according to people familiar with the matter. Among those who will be viewed with more suspicion: applicants who claim income in the low six figures or higher -- well beyond the median for U.S. households -- and those who list an employer's full legal name, a possible sign a swindler is copying and pasting information, the people said.

On the other hand, applicants who call Discover from a landline or cellphone, rather than Skype or other internet-phone services, will be considered safer bets because they're easier to trace back to an individual, the people added.

The new underwriting process also could help Discover approve applicants it might have previously denied.

ZestFinance tested the new methodology last year, according to a person familiar with the matter.

Discover gave the company a batch of loan applications from prior years, and ZestFinance ran the applicants through a new underwriting process, the person said. Using the additional data, ZestFinance was able to identify some approved applicants who ultimately defaulted, the person added.

"We're at an inflection point in the use of data for underwriting," said Douglas Merrill, CEO of ZestFinance and a former chief information officer at Google.
