Features
Alaska chief navigates political demands

CHRIS FLOOD 
1,124 words
4 March 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
4
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

INTERVIEW State governor wants fund to pay out more to help recession-hit economy

Alaska Permanent Fund Corporation Assets $65bn Employees 61 Established 1980 (the fund itself was created in 1976 and the initial investment made in 1977) Headquarters Juneau As chief executive of Alaska's sovereign wealth fund, Angela Rodell leads an organisation that has a vital role in sustaining North America's last frontier state. The $65bn Alaska Permanent fund, which was established with just $734,000 in 1977, was created to channel state royalties earned from oil and mining activities into financial investments to benefit future generations.

Alaska's oil-rich economy, however, is mired in recession and politicians want more help from the fund, which pays an annual cash dividend to all eligible citizens.

Mike Dunleavy, the state governor, has proposed that larger dividends should be paid over the next three years to make up for cuts introduced in 2016. This plan is under debate in Alaska's legislature so Ms Rodell treads carefully when I ask if she favours a higher payout.

"Alaska has been in recession for the last three years. Many citizens feel that an unchanged dividend could have helped to alleviate the economic downturn," she says.

Alaska Permanent is structured as two pools. A $46bn protected principal pool and an $18.7bn earnings reserve account (ERA), which can be spent on dividends and other government activities. With Alaska's deficit forecast to reach $1.6bn in the year ending June 2019, pressure is growing for the fund to contribute more.

Annual withdrawals are scheduled to rise over the next three years to about 5.25 per cent of the fund's assets, more than double the previous rate. It is yet to be decided how those withdrawals will be split between dividend payments and other priorities. "The withdrawals should be manageable but it does mean that the fund will not grow at the same rate as in the past. However, if the rules on withdrawals continue to be rewritten, then the fund could become depleted," cautions Ms Rodell.

Further changes in the withdrawal rules may also impinge on the fund's investment policies. "We cannot commit to the 10-year illiquid alternative investments which have performed so well for the fund if we have a debate every year about withdrawals," she notes.

The fund has delivered annualised returns of 8.9 per cent since its inception. Recent performance has improved. Helped by larger allocations to private equity, the fund has generated returns of 2.1 percentage points above its passive benchmark in the five years up to June 2018, creating an additional $4.7bn in value. "We can show where the investment teams have added alpha but we have to work on keeping talent if we want to maintain this level of performance. We can't take it for granted," Ms Rodell says during a visit to London in February.

Even so, Alaska Permanent faces a wage and hiring freeze that is already in place in other branches of the state's government. Ms Rodell worries that this could hit staff retention. Plans to introduce an incentive compensation scheme for investment staff have been scrapped. "We were not proposing million-dollar bonuses but the compensation scheme is dead in the water," she says.

MSCI, the indices and data analytics provider, poached Russell Read, the fund's chief investment officer, in 2018. "We have been successful at finding talent that wants a certain lifestyle but the industry is a global playing field," says Ms Rodell.

Life in Alaska appeals to the 51-year old who, outside of work, enjoys walking her Labrador retriever. "He's a 'climb any mountain, jump on any boat' kind of dog. Ten minutes down a trail from Juneau and there's no mobile phone signal."

The hiring freeze will disrupt plans to bring more investment management in-house. Most of its fixed-income portfolios are run internally, which saves $15m a year in fees.

Dressed casually in a white denim jacket, Ms Rodell was speaking after a flight from Dubai where she met potential partners for the Middle East, Africa and South Asia-focused fund created by Alaska Permanent in 2017. Although jet-lagged, she was comfortable answering questions without any PR staff present.

An eye-catching initiative agreed last year was a joint venture with RPMI Railpen, the pension fund for UK railway workers, and Wafra Capital Partners, a New York manager acting on behalf of the Public Institution For Social Security of Kuwait. The three have together allocated an initial $700m into Capital Constellation which will invest in promising private equity and alternatives managers.

Efforts to internationalise Alaska Permanent's investments stand in contrast to its policies in its home territory, where its only asset is the headquarters building in Juneau. An ex-employee who declined to be named says more assets should be invested in Alaska, following the example set by sovereign wealth funds in Singapore and Abu Dhabi.

Ms Rodell's reluctance to change the policy on in-state investments has led to tension between her and the board, he says. Pressed on this point, Ms Rodell responds that the CEO alone cannot set the fund's investment policy. The statutes also prohibit it from considering non-economic interests, such as job creation, and required that any in-state allocations must provide the same return and risk profile as non-Alaskan investments.

Climate change poses a threat to Alaska's fragile environment but the fund has decided against adopting the objectives of the 2015 Paris conference. Ms Rodell defends this position, noting that much of the fund's income has derived from royalties from mining and oil companies.

It is set to allocate $200m to upand-coming managers and some of this could go to companies developing alternative energy technologies to benefit Alaskan citizens.

"We are also looking at the opportunities presented by Arctic ice melt in logistics and shipping," she says.

A bigger debate about the fund's future lies ahead, most probably in state elections next year. "There has to be a deeper conversation about the fund and its objectives to ensure that it continues to serve the interests of all Alaska's citizens," says Ms Rodell.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in the UK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Single price: Based on a mid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name.

The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge from fund managers/operators.

Alaska Permanent chief navigates political demands

Angela Rodell talks about withdrawals, keeping her staff and climate change

As chief executive of Alaska s sovereign wealth fund, Angela Rodell leads an organisation that has a vital role in sustaining North America s last frontier state.

The $65bn Alaska Permanent Fund, which was established with just $734,000 in 1977, was created to channel state royalties earned from the mining and oil sectors into financial investments to benefit future generations.

Alaska s oil-rich economy, however, has been mired in recession and politicians want more help from the fund, which pays an annual cash dividend to eligible citizens.

Mike Dunleavy, the state governor, has proposed that larger dividends should be paid over the next three years to make up for cuts introduced in 2016. This plan is under debate in Alaska s legislature so Ms Rodell treads carefully when I ask if she favours a higher payout.

 Alaska has been in recession for the last three years. Many citizens feel that an unchanged dividend could have helped to alleviate the economic downturn,  she says.

Alaska Permanent is structured as two pools. A $46bn protected principal pool and an $18.7bn earnings reserve account (ERA), which can be spent on dividends and other government activities.

With Alaska s budget deficit forecast to reach $1.6bn in the fiscal year ending June 2019, pressure is growing for the fund to contribute more.

Annual withdrawals are scheduled to rise over the next three years to about 5.25 per cent of the fund s assets, more than double the previous rate. It is yet to be decided how those withdrawals will be split between dividend payments and other priorities.

 The withdrawals should be manageable but it does mean that the fund will not grow at the same rate as in the past. However, if the rules on withdrawals continue to be rewritten, then the fund could become depleted,  cautions Ms Rodell.

Further changes in the withdrawal rules may also impinge on the fund s investment policies.

 We cannot commit to the 10-year illiquid alternative investments which have performed so well for the fund if we have a debate every year about withdrawals. That uncertainty probably mean investing more in low-yielding fixed-income assets,  she notes.

The fund has delivered annualised returns of 8.9 per cent since its inception. Recent performance has improved. Helped by larger allocations to private equity, the fund has generated returns of 2.1 percentage points above its passive benchmark in the five years to June 2018, creating an additional $4.7bn in value.

 We can show where the investment teams have added alpha but we have to work on keeping talent if we want to maintain this level of performance. We can t take it for granted,  Ms Rodell said during a visit to London in February.

Even so, Alaska Permanent faces a wage and hiring freeze that is in place in other branches of the state s government.

Ms Rodell worries that this could undermine morale and hit staff retention. Plans to introduce an incentive compensation scheme for investment staff have been scrapped.

 We were not proposing million-dollar bonuses but the incentive compensation scheme is dead in the water,  she says, adding that the staff are  mission-focused .

MSCI, the indices and data analytics provider, poached Russell Read, the fund s chief investment officer, in 2018.

 We have been successful at finding talent that wants a certain lifestyle but the finance industry is a global playing field with competition from London, Hong Kong and the Middle East,  says Ms Rodell.

The outdoor Alaskan lifestyle appeals to who the 51-year-old, outside work, enjoys walking her Labrador.  He s a  climb any mountain, jump on any boat  kind of dog. Ten minutes down a trail from Juneau and there s no mobile phone signal. 

The hiring freeze will disrupt plans to bring more investment management capabilities in-house. Most of its fixed-income portfolios are run internally, which saves about $15m a year in fees.

 We would like to bring more in-house to help lower the cost of investing. It also promotes a better alignment of interests in decision-making, especially in alternatives, which in turn generates better returns,  says Ms Rodell.

Dressed casually in a white denim jacket, Ms Rodell was speaking after an overnight flight from Dubai where she met potential partners for the Middle East, Africa and South Asia-focused fund created by Alaska Permanent in 2017. Although jet-lagged, Ms Rodell was upbeat and comfortable answering questions without any public relations or media staff present during the interview.

An eye-catching initiative agreed last year was a joint venture with RPMI Railpen, the pension fund for UK railway workers, and Wafra Capital Partners, a New York manager acting on behalf of the Public Institution for Social Security of Kuwait. The three partners have together allocated an initial $700m into Capital Constellation, which will invest in promising private equity and alternatives managers.

These efforts to internationalise Alaska Permanent s investments stand in contrast to its policies in its home territory, where its only asset is the headquarters building in Juneau, the state s capital.

A senior ex-employee who declined to be named said that more of the fund s assets should be invested in Alaska, following the example set by sovereign wealth funds in Singapore and Abu Dhabi.

Ms Rodell s reluctance to change the policy on in-state investments has led to tension between her and the board, he says.

Pressed on this point, Ms Rodell responds that the chief executive alone cannot set the fund s investment policy. The fund s statutes also prohibit it from considering non-economic interests, such as job creation, and require that any in-state allocations must provide the same return and risk profile as non-Alaskan investments.

Climate change poses a profound threat to Alaska s fragile environment but the fund has decided against adopting the objectives on limiting global warming agreed at the landmark Paris conference in 2015 as part of its investment framework.

Ms Rodell defends this position, noting that much of the fund s income over the past 40 years has derived from royalties from mining and oil companies.

The fund is set to allocate $200m to up-and-coming managers and some of this could go to companies developing alternative energy technologies to benefit Alaskan citizens.

 We are also looking at the opportunities presented by Arctic ice melt in logistics and shipping,  she says.

A bigger debate about the fund s future lies ahead, most probably in state elections next year.

 There has to be a deeper conversation about the fund and its objectives to ensure that it continues to serve the interests of all of Alaska s citizens,  says Ms Rodell.

Ms Scrimgeour will join LGIM, the 985bn investment arm of the UK-listed insurer Legal & General, after agreeing her notice period with the US asset manager Columbia Threadneedle where she serves as European chief. She will replace Mark Zinkula, who plans to step down in August.

Fabio Zoccoletti has joined Carmignac as head of Italy. Mr Zoccoletti moved to the  42bn French asset manager from Intesa Sanpaolo, the Italian bank. He succeeds Giorgio Ventura who moved to the role of global head of sales for Camignac in April.

Charles Diebel has moved to Mediolanum Asset Management as head of fixed income strategy for the  33bn Irish investment unit of the Italian financial services provider. Mr Diebel, who will be based in Dublin, joins from Aviva Investors.

Nikko Asset Management has recruited Michael Chen as an analyst in its global equity team. Mr Chen has moved to the $220bn Japanese fund house from Schroders.

Lee Phillips has joined MSCI,the indices and data analytics provider. He is head of fixed income client coverage for Europe, the Middle East and Africa as well as country head for the UK and Ireland. Mr Phillips previously worked for FTSE Russell.

European Banks Tighten Belts to Fund Compliance Investments; Financial institutions are investing in technology and adding staff as regulators increase scrutiny

By Kristin Broughton 
661 words

16:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

European banks are investing more resources in staff and technology to spot financial crime following a recent string of money-laundering scandals. In some cases, they're cutting costs elsewhere in the enterprise to fund the initiatives.

In recent weeks, ABN Amro Group NV in Amsterdam and Commerzbank AG in Frankfurt voluntarily disclosed, for the first time, details about increases in spending on anti-money-laundering compliance. Amsterdam-based ING Groep NV, meanwhile, said it boosted investments in its defenses in part by using cash freed up from expense cuts elsewhere.

Banks with global operations, not just in Europe, have been spending more on compliance-related technology, including systems powered by artificial intelligence, and hiring employees with advanced training in areas such as data science, said Walter Mix, managing director at Berkeley Research Group, who advises banks on anti-money-laundering regulations. The investments have put pressure on banks' bottom lines, he said.

Banks in Europe "https://www.wsj.com/articles/europe-ends-scandal-plagued-2018-with-plan-to-target-money-laundering-11545926517"to demonstrate they have strengthened their money-laundering detection tools. Europe has been rocked by high-profile money-laundering scandals over the past year, including"https://www.wsj.com/articles/danske-banks-finds-more-than-200-billion-in-transactions-at-branch-suspected-of-money-laundering-1537345254"by Denmark's Danske Bank A/S that it allowed  200 billion ($230 billion) in Russia-linked transactions to flow through a small branch in Estonia.

Incidents such as these have elevated scrutiny on the systems European banks are using to monitor transactions and assess risk. That could prompt more lenders to disclose their compliance investments in the future, said Robin van den Broek, an analyst with Mediobanca SpA.

"With the regulator putting more focus on this and reputation and high fines at risk, I think it makes sense to expect a reaction in the form of more disclosure," he said.

Regulatory scrutiny was a factor behind ABN Amro's decision to accelerate its planned investments in customer due diligence, Kees van Dijkhuizen, the company's chief executive, said during the bank's earnings call this month.

ABN Amro took an  85 million provision during the fourth quarter to cover the cost of technology and staff that will help the bank verify customer information and spot suspicious transactions. That's in addition to at least  100 million ABN Amro had already budgeted for in 2018, according to the company.

"Down the road this year, we might have other provisions," Mr. van Dijkhuizen said during the call. Since 2013, ABN has tripled the number of employees who work on client due diligence, to about 1,000, he said.

Commerzbank, meanwhile, said it has spent an additional  600 million on compliance since 2015, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/commerzbank-to-settle-u-s-allegations-of-sanctions-and-money-laundering-violations-1426177346"by U.S. authorities into allegations of sanctions and money-laundering violations. The bank agreed to install an independent monitor as part of the settlement.

The investments, made under the guidance of the bank's U.S. monitor, have gone toward implementing a more robust model for managing risks, as well as an enhanced transaction-monitoring and sanctions-screening system. Commerzbank said it also plans to invest in automation within its compliance division.

The additional compliance spending was financed in part by belt-tightening and job cuts elsewhere in the bank, the company said during a Feb. 13 call with analysts.

"This is a price worth paying and is money well spent," Stephan Engels, Commerzbank's chief financial officer, said during the call.

ING's spending on financial-crimes controls has also increased, as it adds staff to strengthen the systems it uses to verify customer identities and information, the bank said this month. ING didn't provide details on costs. Total operating expenses, however, declined 2% from a year earlier, to  2.6 billion.

ING in September"https://www.wsj.com/articles/europe-goes-harder-on-money-laundering-with-record-ing-fine-1536072973" to settle an investigation by Dutch prosecutors into alleged failings in its anti-money-laundering controls.

Good day. Lyft Inc.'s filing to go public has finally arrived, bringing with it a rundown of its top shareholders. Did you know one its first venture backers remains a key institutional owner, including holding a board seat? WSJ Pro's Tomio Geron's piece today will tell you who that is, along with the back story of the company's earliest funding rounds.

And now on to the news...

In the News

From Zimride to Lyft-off: When seed-stage firm Floodgate invested in what is now Lyft in 2010, the startup was called Zimride and its main customers were ride-sharing college commuters.

The firm invested out of its maiden $35 million fund at a $5 million valuation, as Floodgate was one of the first two venture funds to back the company.

For Floodgate, based in Palo Alto, Calif., the investment in Lyft was meaningful on several levels. It gave the firm a feather in its cap as it sought to raise future funds and take stakes in other startups. Floodgate has gone on to raise four more funds, including its latest in 2017, a $131 million vehicle.

The firm sold a sizable portion of its stake several years later to lock in gains, according to a person familiar with the matter. Floodgate still holds about 1.5 million shares, according to Lyft's IPO filing Friday. WSJ Pro's Tomio Geron has the scoop.

But one of the biggest beneficiaries of Lyft's IPO lies across the Pacific, writes WSJ's Jacky Wong.

NEA's fresh start: One of the nation's largest venture firms is attempting to raise its biggest fund to date following a string of changes to its investment team.

New Enterprise Associates is looking for $3.6 billion for its New Enterprise Associates 17 LP fund, according to a regulatory filing. At that level it would be second only to Sequoia Capital, which raised $8 billion last year for its new global growth fund.

New on NEA's roster of general partners is Carmen Chang, who joined the firm in 2012, and serves as its chairman and head of Asia. Longtime NEA health-care investors Ali Behbahani and Paul Walker are also listed as GPs on the new fund, while they weren't previously. An NEA spokeswoman declined to comment on fundraising. WSJ Pro's Yuliya Chernova has the story.

Brain train: A term like "brain-computer interface technology" might not roll off the tongue as easily as "AI" or "robotics." But that may change before long.

Investors who are active in the nascent field say so-called BCI companies just got a big boost now that regulators are pushing to speed this futuristic technology out of the lab and onto the medical-device market.

Advances in technology and biological understanding in the past few years have paved the way for broader investor interest in BCI startups such as Paradromics Inc. and Kernel Co., said Enke Bashllari, who heads Arkitekt Ventures, a backer of Paradromics.

Adds Ms. Bashllari: "Neuroscience, computing power and hardware are all coming together now as the next big industry, and I predict in the next three years, it will be just as buzzy as artificial intelligence has been." WSJ Pro's Heather Mack has the story.

Gormley's Take: Startups Make Do With More Modest Fundraising

By Brian Gormley

Venture capitalists are playing things safer when betting on innovations in eye care.

That's forcing leaders of early-stage companies in the sector to adapt by making do with smaller chunks of capital.

Venture investment in ophthalmology businesses topped $1 billion in 2018, but mature companies drew more interest than raw startups, according to VentureSource, a data provider owned by WSJ Pro publisher Dow Jones & Co.

That means early-stage companies must fight for funding, and often tap sources other than venture capitalists.

"They have to be scrappy and resourceful to get access to adequate capital," said Bill Link, managing partner of ophthalmology-focused investment firm Flying L Partners.

Not long ago, there was more funding for the earliest-stage startups developing treatments for eye conditions, but recently capital has flowed more readily to companies with products on the market. In each year since 2011, the largest category of vision startups raising venture capital has been revenue-generating companies, according to VentureSource.

More in Gormley's Take

Exits

Verizon Communications Inc. acquired ProtectWise Inc. for an undisclosed amount. Denver-based ProtectWise provides a cloud-delivered enterprise security platform for threat detection and other security issues. The company was backed by investors including Arsenal Venture Partners, Top Tier Capital Partners and Tola Capital.

New Money

Scooter wars continue to heat up abroad. Voi Technology, a Stockholm-based electric-scooter startup, raised a $30 million from investors including European firms Vostok New Ventures and Balderton Capital, with participation by San Francisco-based Raine Ventures.

U.S.-based Lime and Bird compete with Voi in European cities such as Paris and Madrid. In October, Berlin-based Tier Mobility raised a roughly $27 million round led by Stockholm's Northzone Ventures and has moved into several European cities in recent weeks, including Madrid. Tier already competes with Lime and Bird in Vienna, Austria.

More funding news:

Datical Inc., an Austin, Texas-based database release automation provider, secured $10 million in Series C funding. River Cities Capital Funds led the round, with Vice President Adam Midkiff joining the company's board. S3 Ventures and Mercury Fund also invested. "https://www.datical.com"

Kuli Kuli Inc., an Oakland, Calif.-based maker of snacks with the superfood moringa, raised $5 million in Series B funding. Griffith Foods and eighteen94 capital led the round, with participation from investors including InvestEco and S2G Ventures. "https://www.kulikulifoods.com"

Breathe Life, a Montreal-based insurance software startup, landed 4.5 million Canadian dollars ($3.4 million) in seed funding. Diagram Ventures led the round and was joined by Real Ventures. "https://www.breathelife.com"

Coin Metrics Inc., a Cambridge, Mass.-based provider of cryptoasset market and network data, closed a $1.9 million seed round. Castle Island Ventures led the investment, with participation from Fidelity Investments, Highland Capital Partners and Dragonfly Capital. "https://www.coinmetrics.io"

People

ExecThread Inc., a New York-based peer-to-peer job sharing network, appointed Ryann Richardson as president. Ms. Richardson joins the company from on-demand private jet marketplace Victor, where she was vice president of North American marketing.

Impossible Foods named Vanessa Wittman to its board of directors. Ms. Wittman previously held chief financial officer positions at Oath, Dropbox, Motorola Mobility and Marsh & McLennan Cos.

Cybersecurity Company eSentire Receives $47 Million from Warburg Pincus-Led Consortium; Canadian business will use investment toward research and development in addition to tuck-in acquisitions

Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Warburg Pincus is doubling down on cybersecurity company eSentire Inc.

Cambridge, Ontario-based eSentire raised an additional $47 million from Warburg Pincus and minority investors Georgian Partners and Edison Partners. Warburg Pincus became a majority backer in the company through an investment made in 2017.

Founded in 2001, eSentire offers managed detection and response services to roughly 700 organizations. It monitors network data, identifies security threats and shuts them down when detected.

The company will use the new capital largely toward investing further in its technology platform, said eSentire Chief Executive Kerry Bailey, who previously worked at Hewlett Packard Enterprise Development LP and Terremark Worldwide Inc. It will also consider potential acquisitions to boost its technology offerings and geographic footprint.

"The security industry is at a tipping point right now," he said, adding that the digitization happening across the technology sector has forced traditional security businesses to rethink their operations.

The cybersecurity sector is increasingly shifting toward services from products and such disruption is fueling private-equity investment in newer security companies, Mr. Bailey explained. ESentire is among those riding the wave, with revenue growing at 50% annually. The company itself bought artificial intelligence business Versive Inc. in October.

Recent deals in the space include CACI International Inc.'s $750 million purchase of LGS Innovations from Madison Dearborn Partners and Battery Ventures' $29 million investment in Guardsquare NV.

New York-based Warburg Pincus has been an active backer of cybersecurity companies such as CrowdStrike Inc. and Contrast Security Inc. The private-equity firm invests across a wide range of sectors: energy; financial services; health-care and consumer; industrial and business services; and technology, media and telecommunications.

LSE to cut 250 jobs as it resets earnings target

Philip Stafford in London 
575 words
1 March 2019
07:54
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Chief David Schwimmer says he will prioritise investment for longer-term growth

The London Stock Exchange Group is to cut 250 jobs as its new chief executive David Schwimmer reset earnings targets and signalled he would prioritise making investments for longer-term growth.

In his first set of earnings since taking over in August last year, Mr Schwimmer said the LSE s businesses continued to perform well and it was  very well prepared for whatever comes from a Brexit situation .

The cuts cover about 5 per cent of the LSE s workforce and will be global. Contracting jobs as well as full-time employees will be affected. The LSE will also reduce the number of offices in New York, and the programme is expected to cost about 30m to implement, while generating annual savings of about 30m.

Mr Schwimmer, who has been largely silent since taking over the role from his predecessor Xavier Rolet, stressed the job cuts had nothing to do with the UK s planned departure from the EU in four weeks  time.

 It s a broader efficiency initiative we re working on,  he said. After a series of deals in recent years,  we have found some duplications .

He also used Friday s annual results to announce the LSE was abandoning targets on earnings margins in favour of further investment in its post-trade and data and analytics businesses.

In 2017 Mr Rolet had set the group targets of making earnings before interest, depreciation, amortisation margins of 55 per cent this year; in 2018 the LSE hit 49.9 per cent.

 Inevitably there s a balancing act between investing for incremental growth and trying to drive for efficiencies. We are still aiming for that target but I m not giving a specific timeframe for it,  he said.

In Mr Schwimmer s first few months the LSE has bought a 4.9 per cent stake in Euroclear for  279m, giving it a minority stake and seat on the board in the Brussels-based central securities depository. It also tightened its grip on LCH, the clearing house, paying  424.5m (382m) to a group of selling banks and exchanges, to raise its stake by 14 per cent to 82.6 per cent.

Chris Turner, analyst at Berenberg, said dropping the target was  disappointing but widely anticipated by investors, and is unlikely to be taken particularly negatively . Shares in the group rose almost 3 per cent to 46.36.

Other growth targets set in 2017, such as turnover for the group and subsidiaries such as LCH and FTSE Russell, the index business, remain.  Overall we suspect 2020 numbers won t move much at all and, given investors usually buy the LSE for revenue growth, this strikes us as fine, if a bit frustrating,  said Philip Middleton, analyst at Bank of America Merrill Lynch.

Mr Schwimmer also said the LSE was prepared for a no-deal Brexit. Rival Deutsche Brse has tried to pull clearing of euro-denominated swaps from London to Frankfurt, but to little success.

 Brexit has provided an interesting marketing opportunity for our competitors 	. 	. 	. 	but there has been no discernible change in our position or market share,  he said.

For the year to December 31 total revenues at the LSE rose 8 per cent to 1.9bn while operating profit rose 14 per cent to 751m on a constant currency basis.

GE CEO Letters Decoded: Shrinking Ambitions and Disappearing Buzzwords; A look at how Larry Culp's first shareholder missive compares with ones written by Jeff Immelt and John Flannery

By Inti Pacheco and Stephanie Stamm 
1,315 words
1 March 2019
12:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Larry Culp's first shareholder letter as General Electric Co.'s CEO was relatively brief and deployed less frequently some words and phrases that longtime leader Jeff Immelt often used to define the goals of "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ge-powered-the-american-centurythen-it-burned-out-11544796010".

The shorter missive reflects changes in GE itself, which has had three CEOs in three years, can only afford to pay a token dividend and is "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ge-to-sell-biopharma-business-to-danaher-for-21-4-billion-11551096661". The company lost some $200 billion in market value over two years and was dropped from the Dow Jones Industrial Average last year.

Here's a look at how Mr. Culp's letter, released this week, compares with ones written by his two immediate predecessors: Mr. Immelt, who left in 2017, and John Flannery, who was replaced in October after 14 months.

LENGTH

The biggest difference is length. Mr. Immelt's last letter was 7,760 words and discussed his vision for GE as well as political and economic forces. "Your company delivered in a year of sluggish growth and geopolitical surprise," Mr. Immelt wrote as he looked back on 2016. The letter was his last after 16 years as CEO but was released before the depths of GE's problems were known. "The investments we are making to become a Digital Industrial will create the next level of growth."

Mr. Flannery's only such annual letter as CEO, which came in early 2018 after he had slashed GE's dividend but was still debating whether to break apart the conglomerate, was just under 5,000 words. He focused on the challenges the company faced and changes they required. "GE had a very tough year," wrote Mr. Flannery, who spent three decades at GE before getting the top job. "There are things we need to fix. But we can. We know how to. And we will."

Mr. Culp, an outsider who took over as CEO in October 2018 and is moving ahead with plans to shrink the company, came in at 2,788 words. He wrote about room for improvement and needing to get on firmer financial footing. "We are embracing our reality and executing the plan we've laid out," he wrote, "Now it's more about what we do than what we say."

BUZZWORDS

Mr. Immelt used catchphrases such as "industrial internet" and "additive manufacturing" to describe ideas that he saw as key to GE's future. But some of those concepts and others such as the GE Store, where business units would tap central research and sales groups were mentioned less frequently over time, as was the data-analysis software Predix. Neither Mr. Flannery nor Mr. Culp mentioned the GE Store.

Some of those terms are remnants of GE Digital, a software unit outside San Francisco that was touted by Mr. Immelt but drastically scaled back after his exit. GE reached a deal last year to sell part of its digital business and plans to move the rest into a newly created company. Neither Mr. Flannery nor Mr. Culp referred to GE as a "digital industrial" company, a phrase used seven times by Mr. Immelt in 2017.

WORD FREQUENCY

Excluding generic terms like "GE" and "business," Mr. Culp's most-commonly used words were "need" and "improve." And he used both of them more frequently than his successors. "We need to radically change how we operate across GE," Mr. Culp wrote. "We need to shift our lens back to the customer and work backward to improve what matters to them."

Mr. Flannery used the word "team" far more often than either Mr. Culp or Mr. Immelt once every 180 words, on average, compared with every 250 words in Mr. Culp's letter and every 450 words in Mr. Immelt's longer letter. "Our leadership team, board and employees have devoted meaningful time to identifying lessons we ought to learn and how we ought to be applying them," Mr. Flannery wrote.

For Mr. Immelt, the words "global" and "growth" stood out, appearing far more frequently in his letter than those of his successors, even after adjusting for the length of his letter. "We see many giving up on globalization, that means more for us," he wrote, shortly after President Trump's election.

DIVISIONS

As business conditions changed, so did the parts of GE that got the most attention from the boss in his annual missive.

GE Power has been saddled with excess inventory and manufacturing capacity as global demand slowed. It also struggled with the integration of Alstom, a French rival acquired in 2015. Mr. Immelt mentioned Alstom nine times; his successors barely mentioned it, even as they detailed "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ge-power-has-a-92-billion-backlog-for-new-boss-thats-a-problem-11550068479" that triggered a $22 billion write-down in 2018.

In his letter, Mr. Immelt wrote about Alstom improving the power division. A year later, Mr. Flannery dedicated an entire section to the new strategy he planned for the troubled unit. "Reset, Refocus, and Renew GE Power," he wrote. This year, Mr. Culp said the division faced challenges in 2018 and it was necessary to run it better.

If there is one thing that all CEOs agreed on, it is on making GE Capital smaller, which has been a theme at the company for more than a decade. Though shrunken, the lending business has been a drag on results after GE uncovered shortfalls in a legacy insurance unit that forced it to boost its reserves by $15 billion.

In his letter, Mr. Culp wrote about a strategy to reduce risk on the balance sheet of GE Capital. Mr. Flannery expressed disappointment in "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ges-capital-dilemma-keep-or-sell-jet-leasing-business-11548846000", talking about cuts to the company's dividend and employment, and Mr. Immelt before him highlighted the sale of the division's assets.

SENTIMENT

The text in each letter was analyzed using a natural language processing tool that assigned each sentence a score for sentiment. All three CEO letters skewed positive, with less than 15% of sentences getting negative sentiment scores. Here are some of the sentences that received very positive and negative scores from each CEO's letter.

Immelt

Positive:

"We win by being a valuable, global company and by engaging members of every community we operate in all over the world." (score=1.0)

Negative:

"Many fears of the American workforce are created by a lack of competitiveness." (score=-0.5)

Flannery

Positive:

"The best people, the best culture this is what makes everything else possible." (score=0.7)

Negative:

"As difficult as 2017 was for everyone connected with GE, it was also a chance to reflect on what this company means and why it exists." (score=-0.5)

Culp:

Positive:

"What I found were talented colleagues from all over the world with grit, resolve, and intelligence." (score=0.7)

Negative:

"We also took the painful but necessary action of reducing GE's dividend, allowing GE to retain approximately $4 billion of cash a year compared with the prior payout level." (score=-0.2)

METHODOLOGY:

The Wall Street Journal analyzed the shareholder letter portion of GE's annual report using natural-language processing libraries for Python, including TextBlob, NLTK, spaCy and vaderSentiment.

To determine word frequency, the analysis omitted so-called stopwords containing little information (such as "the", "a", "an" and "in"). The remaining words were each stemmed (to remove suffixes) using the Porter Stemming Algorithm, and variants of the same word were grouped, such as "improve" and "improving."

To analyze sentiment, the Journal used TextBlob and vaderSentiment, which are often used for opinion mining, to assign each sentence in the text a polarity score ranging from 1 (most positive) to -1 (most negative). The Journal selected examples from the 10 highest- and 10 lowest-scoring sentences.

Business News: Investors Ask Firms to Disclose Climate Risks

By Gabriel T. Rubin 
583 words
1 March 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B3
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Companies are under more pressure than ever to disclose their exposure to climate-change risks.

In the coming annual-meeting season, companies are projected to face a record 75 or more climate-related shareholder proposals, up from 17 in 2013, according to ISS Analytics, the data-intelligence arm of Institutional Shareholder Services. Investing powerhouses such as BlackRock Inc. and Vanguard Group are separately backing voluntary climate-change reporting standards for public companies and hope to prompt an uptick in disclosures this spring.

Annual meetings, which typically take place in the spring, are an opportunity for activist shareholders to put pressure on companies because of a process that allows them to submit proposals for a shareholder vote.

DowDuPont Inc., for instance, received a proposal for its 2019 proxy ballot that asked for more disclosures about risks from expanding chemical-plant operations near the Gulf of Mexico. Major storms there, such as Hurricane Harvey, can cause damage and spills. A spokesman for DowDuPont declined to comment.

Starbucks Corp. is facing pressure from both shareholders and its own environmentally conscious consumer base. Last year, 29% of Starbucks shareholders voted for the company to make public detailed plans about how it was going to promote reusable packaging and cut its use of plastic.

The proposal warned that Starbucks's reputation as an environmental leader could be hurt by its failure to meet its own sustainability goals. The proposal is on the company's 2019 proxy ballots. Starbucks's board of directors recommended against the proposal.

Beyond individual company initiatives, BlackRock, Vanguard and State Street Global Advisors are among institutional investors backing the Sustainability Accounting Standards Board, a nonprofit organization that wants to standardize and increase corporate environmental disclosures. The group in November formally released a set of voluntary standards that companies can follow.

"What we're trying to do is maximize the value of the companies in the portfolio, protect the downside, and move the needle in a direction that we think reflects important material issues company by company," Barbara Novick, a BlackRock senior executive, said in a Feb. 7 Washington speech.

The Financial Stability Board, an international consortium of regulators, is tracking voluntary climate reporting and will issue a report in June.

JetBlue Airways was an early adopter of SASB's approach, launching its voluntary disclosures in 2016. In its 2017 sustainability report, the company said a rise in interest in climate issues followed years of especially volatile hurricane seasons in the Atlantic Ocean.

"For an airline like JetBlue with a significant focus in the Caribbean, the prospect of more extreme storms raises a legitimate concern: How prepared is JetBlue to deal with changing weather patterns that may be caused by climate change?" JetBlue's report said.

Some companies are taking an additional step by making climate-related pledges. Glencore PLC, one of the world's biggest coal producers, said in February that it would cap its output of the fossil fuel. Royal Dutch Shell PLC, Europe's largest oil-and-gas company, bowed to shareholder demands in December to set emission-reduction targets from the use of its products.

Some investors, Democratic politicians and environmental groups are pushing for mandatory disclosures. But the Securities and Exchange Commission so far hasn't required specific climate-related disclosures, instead telling companies to hew to broader criteria for what public companies must disclose as a material risk.

CVS Names Chief Information Officer to Lead Aetna Integration; Roshan Navagamuwa joined CVS in 2012 and over the past year served as interim CIO

By Angus Loten 
534 words
28 February 2019
23:15
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

CVS Health Corp. has tapped a new chief information officer tasked with integrating its information technology and data with IT systems used by Aetna Inc., which the drugstore chain acquired last year in a $70 billion deal.

Roshan Navagamuwa, who over the past year served as interim CIO, was named CIO and executive vice president of the combined health-industry giant, leading all areas of technology operations and innovation, according to a statement provided by the company. The move is effective immediately.

He reports to the firm's executive vice president and chief operating officer, Jonathan Roberts, the company said.

"We are bringing two world-class IT organizations together as part of this integration," Mr. Navagamuwa told CIO Journal in an email. "While there are opportunities to integrate enterprise systems, the most exciting opportunities are what we can build as a result of the two entities coming together," he said.

He said cloud computing was an enabling factor in the integration process, by helping the firm move quickly to take advantage of both existing and emerging software and services.

Mr. Navagamuwa joined CVS in 2012 as vice president of IT for the firm's government business unit. Within three years he became its client services leader.

As CIO, he now oversees an enterprise-technology team made up of a mix of senior IT leaders from both CVS and Aetna. In the months ahead, he will work with Meg McCarthy, executive vice president of operations and technology and former CIO of Aetna, during a transition period to bring together the two firms' IT organizations.

Among other tools, Mr. Navagamuwa said he plans to use big data and analytics to help the firm integrate and deliver care that will be easier to use, while shifting services to be more relevant and personalized and creating platforms that support health-care experts.

By combining operations and reducing corporate expenses, among other factors, CVS has said it expects savings of more than $750 million within the next two years.

Greg Schott, chief executive officer of MuleSoft LLC, a maker of programming tools that bridge diverse software applications or operating systems that was itself acquired by Salesforce.com Inc. last year, said IT cost savings from a sizable acquisition can be difficult to achieve before the firms' diverse data is brought together in a shared system.

Jeff Vail, chief executive officer at executive IT advisory firm WGroup, said that the integration process involves working with disparate systems and technologies across teams under new leadership, and while maintaining security thresholds.

"Business units, sales, marketing, corporate communications, all of the mission-critical front-line business functions will need organized data to communicate with customers, maintain confidence, and convey the upside value of the combined businesses," Mr. Vail said.

Mr. Navagamuwa's "knowledge of our business and the complexities of our operating environment" made him a clear choice to take on these and other challenges as CIO, CVS's Mr. Roberts said in a statement.

Good day. Experts say the full benefits of 5G technology are years off, in part because they will depend on completion of an expensive expansion of wireless networks now under way.

In the News

Drivers' cut: As they chart a course toward initial public offerings, Lyft Inc. and Uber Technologies Inc. both plan to let some of their drivers in on the deals.

The plan represents a rare move that would offer drivers access to two of the most hotly anticipated IPOs ever.

Both Uber and Lyft's IPOs will include programs that would give some of their most-tenured drivers a cash award with an option to put it toward stock in the IPOs, according to people familiar with the matter.

Uber is working out the details of a program expected to be valued in the hundreds of millions of dollars that would give a significant portion of its 3 million active drivers and couriers globally either a cash bonus or the option to use that cash to purchase shares at the IPO price, people familiar with the matter said. The WSJ's Maureen Farrell has the exclusive story.

Gene shopping: Continuing a string of acquisitions of gene-therapy companies, Sarepta Therapeutics Inc. has exercised an option to buy startup Myonexus Therapeutics for $165 million.

On Monday, Roche Holding AG said it would acquire Spark Therapeutics Inc. for $4.8 billion. Spark was the first company to introduce a gene therapy for a genetic disorder in the U.S., winning regulatory approval in 2017 for Luxturna, a treatment for a rare form of inherited vision loss.

Last year, Novartis AG paid $8.7 billion to purchase AveXis Inc. and secure a gene therapy for spinal muscular atrophy, a genetic disease affecting voluntary muscle movement. WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley has the story.

Geron's Take: Ripple Gets Boost From Coinbase, But Faces Hurdles

By Tomio Geron

Coinbase, the cryptocurrency digital wallet and exchange, announced this week that the XRP token is available for trading on its Coinbase Pro product bringing some good news for investors in the token and Ripple Labs Inc., the venture-backed company that created it.

Before this decision, it was curious why the largest U.S. crypto exchange wouldn't list XRP, which has been one of the three largest tokens measured by market value. After all, Coinbase has listed 18 other tokens, including much smaller tokens such as ZRX and BAT, on Coinbase or its professional platform Coinbase Pro.

Coinbase's decision will provide more liquidity and volume for XRP and it led to an immediate bump of about 10% in the token's price soon after the news was announced. That shows the power Coinbase wields when it lists tokens on its exchange.

But questions remain about Coinbase's listing of XRP. For one, what if the Securities and Exchange Commission deems XRP a security? The crypto industry is still waiting to hear from regulators about which tokens are and which aren't securities that can be regulated as such. Critics of XRP say it isn't a decentralized token, since Ripple or its founders own a majority of the tokens in circulation and as a result the SEC could decide that XRP is a security.

Good day! Parting is such sweet sorrow for some private-equity firms and their portfolio companies. So, why part at all?

That's the sentiment that seems to prevail in a growing number of deals we've seen of late, as private-equity firms tap newer funds to reinvest in portfolio companies that they are cashing out of in older funds. English

Good day. Founders Fund, the premier venture firm co-founded by Peter Thiel in 2005, is regrouping after several partner-level departures, but documents show the firm continued to rack up outsize returns for its investors and kept deal flow steady.

Keith Rabois, who spent the last six years at Khosla Ventures, joins Founders Fund at a time when the firm has been delivering above-average returns in some cases, stellar returns to investors.

On paper, every dollar that Founders Fund invested from its 2011-vintage fourth fund has more than quadrupled to $4.60, as of the third quarter last year, according to documents containing firm data that were reviewed by WSJ Pro.

The industry average for funds of that vintage was $2.11 during the same period, according to market tracker Cambridge Associates. Its third fund from 2010 has seen its dollars go up to $3.80, above the average of $3.15. WSJ Pro's Katie Roof has the exclusive story.

And now on to the news...

In the News

Gurley's gig: Benchmark's Bill Gurley, known for making an early investment in Uber Technologies Inc., is backing a different kind of gig platform Instawork which matches restaurants and hospitality businesses with job-seeking waiters, cooks and dishwashers.

Mr. Gurley said Instawork, founded in 2015, has kept a low profile to avoid problems that can arise when a company raises too much capital and grows too fast.

"We wanted to get the model right with unit economics and customers' satisfaction right before we scale it," he said. "A lot of companies make a mistake because money is available and then they try to perfect it at a much bigger level, which I think is a mistake."

While gig work is often criticized as offering low pay and little opportunity for advancement, Instawork could provide a way for workers to find better, higher-paying jobs. The company aims to help them "learn skills to move up the ladder," said Mr. Gurley, who has joined Instawork's board. WSJ Pro's Tomio Geron has the scoop.

Dry-eye deal: Venture investors hope Oyster Point Pharma Inc. will be the next biotech startup to make an impact on the giant market to treat dry eye. Oyster Point recently raised $93 million to take on the disease, which affects more than 16 million U.S. adults, according to the American Journal of Ophthalmology. The company's Series B round was co-led by Invus Opportunities and Flying L Partners, in collaboration with Falcon Vision. WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley has more.

Chernova's Take: Big, Unanswered Question Looms Over Home-Selling Startups

By Yuliya Chernova

In recent years, venture capitalists have flocked to home-selling startups like Opendoor and Knock, partly supported by a robust U.S. real-estate market.

But this new category may be about to face its biggest test yet as home prices head into their first rough patch since the companies came on the scene.

Investor faith in home-sales startups could be at a high point. Opendoor Labs Inc., which takes homes quickly off sellers' hands, raised some $725 million last year alone. Knock, which helps consumers trade in their old home for a new one, pulled in $400 million in a combination of Series B equity and debt in January.

Asked a few weeks ago whether Opendoor would be vulnerable to a weaker market, Keith Rabois, who founded the company as a general partner at Khosla Ventures and who is joining Founders Fund, replied that there is "zero vulnerability." Not only that, he added, "We are going to do phenomenally well in a depreciating market."

That does sound very optimistic.

Just like any home buyer, companies such as Opendoor and Knock are exposed to the risk of buying high and selling low. That risk increases in a market downturn, which the cyclical real-estate market might be starting to enter.

Upstarts challenge private equity s top tier

Eric Platt and Mark Vandevelde in New York 
1,162 words
26 February 2019
04:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Veritas joins once-obscure groups writing big cheques and grabbing assets from clutches of larger rivals

Before Veritas Capital teamed up with activist hedge fund Elliott Management to pull off one of the biggest private equity deals of 2018, the New York buyout firm was known for its work with niche businesses such as a manufacturer of components for satellites.

But the $5.6bn buyout of Athenahealth catapulted Veritas into a higher orbit. There, it joins half a dozen once-obscure private equity players that have broken into the industry s top tier, capable of writing large equity cheques and snatching coveted assets from the clutches of Apollo, Blackstone and KKR.

The $6.5bn takeover of analytics group Dun & Bradstreet and $4.1bn purchase of travel technology provider Travelport were sealed by buyout firms that had long focused on smaller mid-market deals, with competition for assets intensifying as investors pump billions of dollars into new PE funds, bankers and lawyers say.

The rarefied world of the biggest deals is attractive as it contains the best return prospects, according to Michael Chae, chief financial officer of the Blackstone Group. Smaller deals, in contrast, tend to have three or four groups competing and bidding prices higher.

 We think mid-market secondary buyouts have been the most crowded, competitive space where we ve seen the most return pressure,  said Mr Chae.  Larger scale primary deals, corporate sellers, corporate partners, more complex, more operational value creation potential, we think that's where the better fishing has been. 

An increasing number of firms are angling for bigger buyouts.Siris Capital, Francisco Partners and Veritas Capital are among the firms that struck deals for more than $3.5bn each last year.

 There is a longer list of private equity firms with unused equity capital highly motivated to put that capital to work,  said Chris Ventresca, global co-head of M&A at JPMorgan.  They are more willing to utilise a significant amount of equity capital and make more sizeable bets. 

For both Siris and Veritas, Elliott Management s private equity arm Evergreen Coast Capital was critical to clinch their largest deals. Elliott has ramped up its buyout efforts, which are often directed at companies that the firm has itself targeted as an activist. Founded by Paul Singer and known for its brash style, Elliott is raising $2bn to fund outright takeovers.

Marni Lerner, a partner at law firm Simpson Thacher, said those efforts are fuelled by cash inflows from pensions, endowments and sovereign wealth funds, as well as a $1.2tn stockpile of committed capital that has yet to be deployed.

 You see larger firms working on mid-market deals 	. 	. 	. 	and mid-market firms are looking for larger deals,  Ms Lerner said.  Fund sizes are getting bigger, which is allowing [mid-market firms] to do larger deals. 

The fundraisings have been significant. Francisco Partners, founded by former TPG principal Dipanjan Deb, raised more than $3bn for its latest fund in 2017. So did Veritas Capital, which is where chief executive Ramzi Musallam set up shop after spending part of his career working for noted investor and hospitality heir Jay Pritzker.

Both firms exceeded their previous fundraising records by more than $1bn, according to data provider Preqin. Siris Capital, founded by two former members of the mergers and acquisitions department at Goldman Sachs, is making an even bigger leap from earlier vehicles with its latest fund, also pegged above $3bn.

Technology-focused buyout group Thoma Bravo has eclipsed all three groups with a $12.6bn fund, its largest to date. The firm is looking at clinching its biggest takeover, approaching US cyber security maker Symantec about a potential deal, which would rank among the biggest buyouts since the crisis if completed.

Chinh Chu s CC Capital has taken a different approach to raising cash, floating  blank cheque  companies on the stock market, rather than by raising traditional funds.

Mr Chu purchased Dun & Bradstreet alongside an investor consortium earlier this year. The group, founded by the former Blackstone dealmaker, has trained its sights on deals between $2bn and $15bn, said a person familiar with the matter.

Some buyout firms have also punched above their weight by tapping favoured investors for top-up capital to invest alongside their main funds. These  co-investments  are attractive for pension and sovereign wealth funds, because they are usually exempt from the private equity firm s fees, which can amount to 1.5 per cent a year of the amount invested and 20 per cent of any profits.

When Francisco Partners paid $3.6bn last year for Verifone, the payments company, it turned to the Canadian pension fund British Columbia Investment Management Corporation to help fund the deal.

In interviews with the Financial Times, executives from several firms were keen to emphasise common themes between their most recent deals and the far smaller transactions from which they earned their stripes with investors.

 There is not much difference between a $500m transaction and a $3bn transaction, other than transaction size,  said Mr Deb, chief executive of Francisco Partners.  You still have to pursue [the] same steps in diligence and deal process. 

Mr Musallam of Veritas said his team was  consistent  in its approach even if the targets  size was changing.  We don t want a far-flung enterprise where our intellectual property becomes 	. 	. 	. 	dissipated across a broader base [of industries]. 

Sharp increases in private equity deal size can ring alarm bells with investors stung by their experiences with groups such as Candover, which expanded its ambitions dramatically in the years before the financial crisis only to topple when deals soured and financing dried up.

But bankers and others who invest big pension funds on their investments say investment opportunities are so scarce, and the competition to sign up to funds so intense, that they have little choice but to go along with sponsors  plans. Lacklustre performance from other alternative investors, notably hedge funds, has propelled the shift into private equity.

The flood of capital is also contributing to higher asset valuations, forcing private equity managers to pay as much as 11 to 12 times earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortisation for a company, higher even than the inflated prices at the previous market peak in 2007.  Everything is priced to perfection,  one leveraged finance veteran said.  There is not much room for error, or a market downturn. 

But investors are not  indiscriminately throwing money  at every mid-market private equity group seeking to raise fresh capital, said Max Justicz, head of financial sponsors for the Americas at UBS.  We are certainly seeing some signs that the new generation of leadership at some of these firms are willing to take more sizeable or concentrated investments. 

 [Investors] are extremely focused on performance and are giving the top-quartile PE firms considerably larger new fund size if they seek it,  he said.

Robots head to Nomura s trading floors

Laura Noonan 
1,466 words
26 February 2019
21:05
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Your essential weekly fintech briefing

No one could accuse the Japanese of being backwards at coming forward when it comes to the robotic revolution. This is the country that broughtus the world's firstrobot hotel,the world's first  humanless warehouse and, more recently, arobotic petdesigned to offer its human overlords love and affection.

Now Japanese bank Nomura is getting in on the act, investing in astart-up that deploys artificial intelligence (AI) in financial markets.

Named Aim2, the start-upwas set up by Brevan Howard back in 2015 to help the hedge fund with its alpha investment strategies, ie to find ways of making money based on the particular profile of an investmentrather than through market exposures.

Nomura   which is understood to have taken a 22 per cent stake in Aim2   will use the same technology to price trades and markets in its wholesale business.

 Typically an electronic trading engine or trader makes prices based on certain tiering and logistics,  said Jez Mohideen, chief digital officer at Nomura's wholesale business, describing a pricing process that involves  using large sets of data, understanding behaviours of the market, the market impact of clients and so forth .

Machines, of course, excel at making sense of big data sets, and intelligent ones have the added edge of learning how market behaviours and trends evolve. Hence Nomura's desire to deploy AI so it can find a  probabilistic price  that will generate maximum revenue from a client while also helping Nomura to maintain its target market share.

Nomura's roll-out of its robot traders will be initially focused on its fixed income, currencies and commodities division, or FICC as its known in banking circles, since it'sfar more reliant on human traders than equities, which has been largely electronified. Within FICC, fixed income is the main target since it has been less automated than foreign exchange.

 We'd gradually look at foreign exchange and equities,  Mr Mohideen added, as he outlined a plan to deploy the robots across Nomura's trading business within two years.

While the prospect is new within Nomura, and may be greeted with concernby traders who have already seen some of their human colleagues laid off this year, bigger investment banks have been using AI in their fixed income businesses for some time.

Most banksdidn't want to go public on exactly how they use AI, but informed sources said robots have been used in some form or another in the FICC businesses at JPMorgan Chase, Morgan Stanley, Goldman Sachs, Citigroup and Bank of America for a few years now.

Europeans have been at it too. Barclays uses AI for everything from forecasting business volumes to recommendations engines that help the bank target its client pitches.Chris Purves, head of UBS's strategic development lab, says the Swiss bank is using AI across  most  of its fixed income business lines, both for executing client orders and for market making and pricing.  The key to us is that it's explainable,  he adds.  Can you say why something made the price that it did? 

Certainly, the commercial imperative is there.

Banks have had a bruising few yearsin FICC, with annual revenues across the big five US banks falling 15 per cent since 2013, and smaller playersfaring even worse. The industry's most senior executives have described the pivotal role technology will play in determining the winners and losers in FICC's next era, arguing that the biggest banks will fare best because they can invest the necessary sums for true innovation.

Nomura,a sizeable bankbut not big enough to make the global top 12 investment banks as ranked by industry monitor Coalition, is getting around the problem by investingin a cutting-edge AI firm.

Mr Mohideen expects other smaller investment banks to join them in deploying third-party technology to bridge the gap between whatthey spend on R&D and the $11bn technology budget of JPMorgan. Indeed he'd even welcome them as clients of Aim2.  The way we look at it, the toolkit is beneficial for the industry as a whole,  he said.  Going forward the world is getting to a place where that (exclusivity) is less of a concern. 

The prospect of wider deployment of robot traders raises the thorny question of what happens to the human traders. The obvious conclusion   which bank executives go to great lengths to avoid acknowledging  is another wave of lay-offs.

It might not be as bad as some fear though. Earlier this year, Japan's pioneering robot hotel admitted it hadsackedmore than half of its 243 robots because they were more hindrance than help. Humans: 1, Robots: 0.

One to watch: Hundsun Technology

RememberHundsun Technologies, the Ant Financial-backedfintech that ran intotroublefor providing software that enabled speculative margin trading by retail investors during China's 2015 boom and bust?

From that mostunlikely ofstarting points, Hundsun hascreated a booming business selling software to help financial institutions strengthen their risk control,writes Gabriel Wildau in Shanghai.

Net income rose 37 per cent to Rmb233m (approx $35m) for the first three quarters of 2018, and in a few weeks' time we'll find out if the full-year results are as encouraging.

The seeds for its growth spurt were sown in 2017, whenHundsun shifted towards technologies that match regulators  emphasis on controlling financial risk.

The crown jewel is the O32 software package that offers a comprehensive risk management systemspanning everything from monitoring irregular transactions to real-time tracking of profits and setting trading limits.

Hundsun says O32 can avoid trading errors resulting from computer errors, like the2013 incidentthat triggered a Rmb523m fine forbrokerage Everbright aftermistaken orders were placed by a trading algorithm in its proprietary trading unit. Fund managers are a key target market for O32 as well.

It's all a far cry from 2015, when Hundsun was fined Rmb400m becauseproviders of grey market finance used Hundsun's HOMS software to allow hundreds of retail investors to trade separately   and access margin loans  using a single brokerage account.

Number of the week: $8bn

The valuation of Ping An s OneConnect unit, which the Chinese insurer is preparing to list, according to Bloomberg.

Further fintech fascination

Trend watch:It's been a long time coming;now Hong Kong is finally opening up its banking industry to online competition. In the next few weeks, six of the new licences are expected to be issued to regional powerhouses in digital bankingincluding Tencent, Ant Financial and insurer ZhongAn. Western digital banks may also ultimately join the rush to win share in the potentially lucrative market, where 66 per cent of all lending is done by just four banks (HSBC, Bank of China, Hang Seng Bank and Standard Chartered).

Regulators' advance:Most countries would be glad to secure a fintech with the profile of Revolut, the foreign exchange app that's fast becoming an online financial services supermarket and commands avaluation north of $1.7bn.Not Lithuania though, where Revolut secured a banking licence last year. Senior politicians in the Baltic country are now voicing fears that its central bank has bitten off more than it can chew.

Follow the money:Funding for insurtech companies reached a record $4.1bn last year, easily eclipsing the $2.7bn raised in 2015, according tonew data from Willis Towers Watson, the insurance broker. The fourth quarter of the year was particularly busy, with $1.6bn raised in 63 deals. They included $68m raised by Germany s simplesurance, which specialises in cover for products sold online, $116m for Italian motor insurer Prima Assicurazioni and $500m in a SoftBank-backed funding round from Cambridge Mobile Telematics.

New frontiers:Want to protect yourself from losing a fortune if the head of your chosen crypto exchange dies suddenly and takes your access codes to the grave with him? Then look no further than the Lloyd's insurance marketplace, which has sold crypto custodian BitGo Inc a policy of as much as $100m for losses, including the loss of access keys, an unforeseen riskthat has left clients of Canadian exchange QuadrigaCX out some $150m. Moreherefrom Bloomberg.
